NVIDIA GPU SuperPOD: Complete Infrastructure Walkthrough

From concrete floor to container orchestration — everything you need to know to be a GPU datacenter SRE

This interactive guide dismantles the full stack of an NVIDIA DGX SuperPOD, layer by layer. You will follow the path electricity takes from the utility transformer to a running distributed training job—touching power distribution, liquid cooling, GPU hardware, InfiniBand fabric, network architecture, virtualization, parallel storage, Kubernetes orchestration, and day-two operations. Every section includes real-world reference numbers, topology diagrams, and a short self-assessment quiz so you can validate your understanding before moving on.

~1 EXAFLOP FP8 Performance

Prerequisites

This guide assumes you have a working familiarity with the following topics. If any of these feel unfamiliar, follow the linked resources before diving into the main content.

  • Basic Linux Administration Comfortable with the shell, systemd services, package management, user permissions, and reading logs. You should be able to SSH into a server and troubleshoot a failed service without a GUI.
  • Networking Fundamentals (TCP/IP, Subnetting) Understand the OSI model through Layer 4, IPv4 subnetting and CIDR notation, basic firewall rules, DNS resolution, and how a packet traverses a routed network.
  • Basic Understanding of Virtualization Know what a hypervisor does, the difference between Type-1 and Type-2 virtualization, and why hardware-assisted virtualization (VT-x / AMD-V) matters for performance.
  • Familiarity with Containers and Kubernetes Concepts Understand container images, container runtimes, pods, deployments, services, and namespaces. You do not need to be a Kubernetes expert—this guide will cover GPU-specific orchestration in depth.

Layer 0

Physical Infrastructure

The foundation of GPU-accelerated HPC: facility design, power delivery, thermal management, and floor planning for modern AI/ML datacenters.

1. Datacenter Facility

GPU superpod deployments demand facilities engineered far beyond traditional enterprise datacenters. A single NVIDIA DGX SuperPOD with 32 DGX H100 nodes draws approximately 330 kW of IT power alone — before cooling overhead. At scale, a facility hosting thousands of GPUs requires 20–50+ MW of total capacity, robust redundancy, and strict physical security.

Uptime Institute Tier Classification

+
Attribute Tier III — Concurrently Maintainable Tier IV — Fault Tolerant
Uptime SLA 99.982% (1.6 hrs downtime/yr) 99.995% (26.3 min downtime/yr)
Distribution Paths Multiple paths, one active Multiple active paths (2N distribution)
Redundancy N+1 (components can be serviced without IT impact) 2N+1 fully fault-tolerant; single fault causes no downtime
Maintenance Impact All planned maintenance transparent to IT load All planned and unplanned events transparent
Typical Build Cost $12–$18M per MW $18–$25M+ per MW
GPU Superpod Suitability Common for hyperscaler GPU deployments (cost-optimized) Preferred for mission-critical AI training with SLA requirements
SRE Note: Most CoreWeave-class GPU cloud providers target Tier III facilities with selective Tier IV elements (e.g., 2N power to every rack, N+1 cooling). This balances cost against the reality that large-scale training jobs already checkpoint frequently and can tolerate brief cooling maintenance windows.

Typical Facility Specifications

+

Total Capacity

20–50+ MW per facility; individual halls typically provisioned at 2–8 MW each. A 40 MW campus can support roughly 4,000–6,000 H100 GPUs depending on cooling overhead and PUE.

Whitespace

50,000–200,000+ sq ft of raised-floor or slab data hall space. GPU-dense halls often allocate 80–120 sq ft per rack (including hot-aisle containment and CDU footprint).

Network Meet-Me Rooms

Carrier-neutral MMRs with diverse fiber entry points. Minimum two physically separate fiber paths from different conduit routes. Cross-connects to upstream transit and peering providers.

Staging & Storage

Dedicated build rooms for rack assembly and burn-in testing before deployment to the data hall. Spare parts inventory for GPUs, NICs, NVMe drives, fans, PSUs, and coolant hoses.

Physical Security Controls

+
Control Implementation Details
Perimeter Fenced compound with anti-climb barriers, vehicle bollards at entry, 24/7 security guard station, license plate recognition (LPR) cameras at gate.
Mantraps / Airlocks Two-door interlocking vestibules at data hall entry. First door must close and lock before second opens. Anti-tailgating sensors (weight pads or optical beams) prevent piggybacking.
Biometrics Multi-factor: badge (RFID/proximity) + biometric (fingerprint or palm-vein scanner). Some facilities add PIN as a third factor for Tier IV zones.
CCTV IP cameras with ≥90-day retention. Coverage of all entry points, aisles, loading docks, and mechanical/electrical rooms. Motion-triggered alerts for off-hours access.
Visitor Management Pre-approved visitor list, government-issued ID verification, escorted access only, sign-in/sign-out logs retained for ≥1 year. Visitors issued temporary badges that auto-expire.
Audit & Compliance Access logs correlated with CCTV timestamps during SOC 2 Type II audits. Physical access reviews conducted quarterly. Badge deprovisioning within 24 hours of role change or termination.

Compliance Frameworks

+

SOC 2 Type II

Covers Trust Services Criteria: Security, Availability, Processing Integrity, Confidentiality, and Privacy. Type II reports examine controls over a 6–12 month audit period (not just point-in-time). Key controls include physical access management, change management, incident response, and monitoring. Annual re-certification required.

ISO 27001:2022

Information Security Management System (ISMS) certification. Annex A controls relevant to physical infra include A.7 (Physical Controls): A.7.1 physical security perimeters, A.7.2 physical entry, A.7.3 securing offices/rooms, A.7.4 physical security monitoring, A.7.5 protecting against physical/environmental threats, A.7.8 equipment siting and protection, A.7.12 cabling security, A.7.13 equipment maintenance.

Additional Standards

PCI DSS if processing payment data; HIPAA for healthcare workloads (physical safeguards §164.310); FedRAMP for US government workloads (PE control family). GPU cloud providers increasingly pursue ISO 27017 (cloud security) and ISO 27018 (PII in cloud) as well.

2. Power Distribution

Power architecture for GPU-dense facilities is fundamentally different from traditional datacenters. A single 42U rack of 4× DGX H100 systems can draw 40–100+ kW — an order of magnitude above legacy 5–10 kW server racks. Every element of the power chain must be designed for these loads with full redundancy.

Utility Feed & Medium Voltage Switchgear

+

Dual Utility Feeds

Critical GPU facilities receive power from two independent utility substations fed by separate transmission lines. This eliminates single-point-of-failure at the utility level. Each feed is typically rated at 100% of the facility's total load, providing true 2N utility redundancy.

Common configurations: 2×35 kV or 2×13.8 kV feeds, depending on local utility infrastructure. Facilities >30 MW often take delivery at 69 kV or 138 kV and step down on-site via customer-owned substations.

Medium Voltage Switchgear

Incoming utility at 13.8 kV (or 34.5 kV) is routed through metal-clad, arc-resistant switchgear rated to ANSI C37.20.2. Vacuum circuit breakers provide fault isolation with interrupting capacity typically rated at 25–40 kA.

Step-down transformers (13.8 kV → 480V, dry-type or liquid-filled) serve the mechanical and IT loads. For GPU racks, 480V is further stepped to 208V three-phase (via 480:208/120V Delta-Wye PDU transformers) or delivered at 240V single-phase in some configurations.

UPS Systems

+
Attribute Rotary UPS (e.g., Hitec UNIBLOCK) Battery UPS (e.g., Eaton 93PM, Vertiv Liebert EXL)
Energy Storage Kinetic energy in flywheel (steel or composite rotor) Lithium-ion (LFP) or VRLA lead-acid battery strings
Typical Runtime 10–20 seconds (bridge to generator start) 10–15 minutes at full load; scalable with additional battery cabinets
Efficiency 97–98% in normal mode; no double-conversion losses 96–97% double-conversion; 99% in eco-mode (bypass)
Footprint Compact; no battery room required Requires dedicated battery room with HVAC, spill containment, hydrogen detection (for VRLA)
Maintenance Bearing replacement every 5–7 years Battery replacement every 3–5 years (VRLA) or 10–15 years (Li-ion)
GPU Suitability Excellent for high-density; smaller footprint per MW; pairs well with fast-start generators More flexible runtime; better for facilities where generator start exceeds 10 seconds
Critical: GPU workloads (especially large-scale training jobs) cannot tolerate even sub-second power interruptions. The UPS must provide zero transfer time (double-conversion online topology or rotary flywheel continuously in-line). Static bypass switches must transfer in <4 ms per IEC 62040-3 Class 1 requirements.

Diesel Generators

+

Startup & Transfer

Modern standby generators (e.g., Caterpillar 3516, MTU 4000 series) are rated for startup to full load in 10–15 seconds. Block heaters keep coolant at 40–50°C for reliable cold-start. The Automatic Transfer Switch (ATS) detects utility loss (voltage sag below 80% for >100 ms) and sends a start signal. Once the generator reaches rated voltage and frequency (±3%), the ATS transfers load. Total utility-to-generator transfer: 15–25 seconds.

Fuel & Runtime

On-site fuel storage: typically 24–72 hours at full load. A 2.5 MW generator consumes roughly 700 liters/hour of diesel at full load. Priority fuel contracts with multiple suppliers guarantee resupply within 4–8 hours. Base fuel tanks (belly tanks) hold 2,000–4,000 liters; supplemental day tanks or bulk storage extend runtime.

Load Testing

Monthly: 30-minute loaded test at ≥30% rated load (prevents wet-stacking). Annually: 4-hour full-load test per NFPA 110 Level 1 requirements. Resistive load banks are used when IT load is insufficient for proper testing. All tests logged for compliance audits.

Emissions & Permitting

EPA Tier 4 Final emissions standards for new installations. Selective Catalytic Reduction (SCR) or Diesel Particulate Filters (DPF) required. Operating permits limit annual run hours (typically 200–500 hours for standby-rated units). Facilities exceeding limits must apply for prime-rated permits.

Automatic Transfer Switches (ATS)

+

The ATS is the critical switchover point between utility and generator power. Key specifications for GPU datacenter ATS units:

Specification Typical Value
Transfer Type Open-transition (break-before-make) for standby gensets; closed-transition (make-before-break, <100 ms overlap) for re-transfer to utility
Voltage Sensing Adjustable pickup/dropout: typically 85%/80% of nominal; time delay 0.5–6 seconds (adjustable to avoid nuisance transfers on momentary sags)
Current Rating 800A–4000A at 480V, depending on downstream load
Withstand Rating 65–100 kAIC (kilo-ampere interrupting capacity)
Standards UL 1008, CSA C22.2 No. 178, NFPA 110

PDUs: Rack-Level Power Distribution

+
PDU Type Capabilities Use Case
Basic Power distribution only; no monitoring or switching Cost-sensitive deployments; no remote management needed
Metered Real-time current (A), voltage (V), power (kW/kVA), energy (kWh) per-inlet and per-breaker. SNMP/HTTP/Modbus telemetry Standard for GPU facilities; enables capacity planning and billing per rack
Switched Metered + per-outlet remote on/off/reboot. Outlet-level current monitoring Essential for remote power cycling of unresponsive GPU nodes without dispatching a technician
Monitored + Switched (Intelligent) Per-outlet metering, switching, environmental sensors (temperature, humidity, airflow), locking outlets (IEC 60320 C13/C19 with SecureLock) Gold standard for GPU racks; Raritan PX4, ServerTech PRO3, or APC Rack PDU metered-by-outlet

Voltage & Phase Configurations

208V Three-Phase (Wye)

Most common in North American datacenters. Derived from 480V→208Y/120V transformer. Three hot legs + neutral. 208V line-to-line, 120V line-to-neutral. Typical PDU: 3-phase 60A (208V) = ~21.6 kVA per PDU. For a 80 kW GPU rack, two 3-phase 60A PDUs on separate power paths (A+B feeds).

240V Single-Phase

Common in some US facilities and international deployments. Higher voltage = lower current for the same power, allowing smaller conductors. A single 240V/50A circuit delivers ~12 kVA. Less common for high-density GPU racks due to limited per-circuit capacity.

400V Three-Phase (European/Hyperscale)

Growing adoption even in US hyperscale facilities. 400V three-phase eliminates one transformer stage (direct from 480V with minor ratio adjustment), improving efficiency by 1–2%. Google, Microsoft, and Meta have adopted 400V distribution for new builds.

SRE Tip: Always verify PDU firmware supports SNMP v3 (encrypted) and HTTPS. Older PDUs with SNMP v1/v2c expose community strings in plaintext. Integrate PDU telemetry into your DCIM platform (e.g., Sunbird, Nlyte, or custom Prometheus exporters) for real-time power monitoring and alerting at ≥80% circuit capacity.

Power Redundancy Models

+
Model Description Effective Capacity Cost
N No redundancy. Single power path. Any component failure causes outage. 100% of installed capacity usable Baseline
N+1 One additional component beyond minimum required. E.g., if 4 UPS modules needed, install 5. One can fail or be maintained without impact. 80–85% of installed capacity usable +20–25%
2N Fully duplicated power path. Two independent chains (utility → ATS → UPS → PDU), each sized for 100% load. Servers with dual PSUs draw from both paths. 50% of total installed capacity usable +80–100%
2N+1 Two complete paths plus one additional redundant component per path. Maximum resilience. ~48% of total installed capacity usable +100–120%
GPU Rack Consideration: DGX H100 systems have dual redundant 3 kW PSUs (6 PSUs total per node, operating in 3+3 redundancy). Each set must be connected to a separate power path (A feed and B feed). If both PDUs are on the same electrical path, you have N redundancy, not 2N, even though the hardware supports it. Verify all the way back to the UPS.

PUE & Power Density

+

Power Usage Effectiveness (PUE)

PUE = Total Facility Power ÷ IT Equipment Power

Industry average: ~1.58 (Uptime Institute 2023 survey). Best-in-class air-cooled: 1.2–1.3. Liquid-cooled GPU facilities: 1.1–1.2 achievable. Every 0.1 PUE improvement on a 40 MW facility saves ~4 MW of overhead power, translating to $2–4M/year in electricity costs at $0.06/kWh.

Per-Rack Power Density

Traditional enterprise: 5–10 kW/rack. GPU-accelerated HPC: 30–100+ kW/rack. A rack of 4× DGX H100 (8 GPUs each = 32 GPUs): ~40 kW IT + networking. A full OCP rack with 8× HGX H100 baseboard: ~80–100 kW. NVIDIA GB200 NVL72 rack: up to 120 kW.

Power Distribution Flow

Figure 1: 2N power distribution architecture. Each GPU server receives power from two independent paths (A and B), each backed by dedicated utility feed, transformer, ATS, UPS, and rack PDU. Diesel generators feed both ATS units during utility outage.

3. Cooling

Cooling is the single biggest engineering challenge in GPU-dense datacenters. An NVIDIA DGX H100 node with 8× H100 SXM5 GPUs dissipates approximately 10.2 kW of thermal energy. A rack with 4 DGX H100 systems produces 40+ kW of heat in a 42U footprint — far exceeding the 5–7 kW/rack that traditional CRAC/CRAH air cooling was designed for. At 80–120 kW/rack densities (GB200 NVL72), air cooling is physically impossible; liquid cooling is mandatory.

Traditional Air Cooling & Its Limitations

+

Conventional Computer Room Air Handlers (CRAHs) circulate chilled air through raised-floor plenums or overhead ducts. Practical limits:

Metric Air Cooling Limit GPU Rack Requirement
Heat removal per rack 15–25 kW (with containment) 40–120 kW
Airflow per rack ~3,000–5,000 CFM achievable 8,000–15,000+ CFM needed
Fan energy 30–40% of cooling energy spent on fans Unacceptable PUE impact at scale
Noise at rack 70–80 dBA typical 90+ dBA with high-speed fans — hearing protection required
Why containment helps but isn’t enough: Hot aisle/cold aisle containment prevents mixing of supply and return air, improving CRAH efficiency by 20–30%. With containment, air cooling can handle up to ~25 kW/rack. Beyond that threshold, the volumetric airflow required simply cannot be delivered through standard raised-floor tiles (each perforated tile delivers ~500–800 CFM).

Liquid Cooling Technologies

+

Direct-to-Chip (Cold Plate) Cooling

Cold plates are machined copper or aluminum blocks mounted directly on CPUs and GPUs with thermal interface material (TIM). Facility water (or a water-glycol mix) flows through microchannels in the cold plate, absorbing heat via conduction.

How It Works

Each GPU module has a dedicated cold plate connected via flexible hoses to a manifold in the rack. The manifold connects to the facility Coolant Distribution Unit (CDU). Supply water enters at 30–45°C and exits at 45–60°C. The high delta-T allows the use of warmer water, enabling free cooling for more hours of the year.

Performance

Removes 70–80% of rack heat via liquid (the remaining 20–30% from DIMMs, NVMe drives, NICs, VRMs is still dissipated via fans into room air). Flow rate: typically 1.5–4.0 liters/minute per GPU cold plate. Pressure drop across a fully populated rack manifold: 30–80 kPa (4–12 PSI).

Leak Detection

Non-negotiable requirement. Conductive fluid leak sensors on every hose connection, under every rack, and at every CDU. Leak detection ropes (e.g., RLE Technologies SeaHawk) run the length of every liquid-cooled row. Automated shutoff valves isolate a leaking rack within seconds. All connections use dripless quick-disconnect fittings (e.g., Staubli or CPC) rated for thousands of connect/disconnect cycles with <1 mL fluid loss per event.

NVIDIA Implementation

The DGX H100 liquid-cooled variant uses a custom cold plate assembly covering all 8 H100 SXM5 GPUs and the dual CPUs. The DGX SuperPOD reference architecture specifies CoolIT DCLC or Vertiv XDU CDUs. The GB200 NVL72 rack is liquid-cooled only — no air-cooled option exists.

Rear-Door Heat Exchangers (RDHx)

A passive or active heat exchanger replaces the standard rear door of the rack. Chilled water flows through a copper coil in the door, absorbing heat from exhaust air before it enters the room. Can neutralize 60–100% of rack heat, effectively making the rack “zero-emission” to the room. Suitable for up to ~40 kW/rack. Not sufficient alone for high-density GPU racks above 50 kW — often combined with direct-to-chip cooling to handle the remaining airborne heat.

Full Immersion Cooling

IT equipment is submerged in a dielectric fluid (e.g., 3M Novec 7100 engineered fluid, or mineral/synthetic oil). Single-phase immersion keeps the fluid liquid (bath temperature ~35–50°C). Two-phase immersion uses a low-boiling-point fluid (boiling point ~49–61°C for Novec fluids) that vaporizes at the chip surface and condenses on an overhead coil, providing extremely efficient heat transfer.

Immersion can handle >100 kW/tank but requires custom server form factors (no fans, open chassis design, compatible connectors). Adoption is growing but still niche for GPU superpods due to serviceability challenges — you must extract servers from fluid to replace components.

CDUs, Chillers & Cooling Distribution

+

Coolant Distribution Units (CDUs)

The CDU is the bridge between the facility cooling loop and the IT cooling loop. It contains a liquid-to-liquid heat exchanger (plate-and-frame or brazed-plate type), circulation pumps (N+1 redundancy), a fluid reservoir, filtration (5–20 micron), and control valves.

Primary (facility) side: chilled water from the chiller plant, typically 7–15°C supply, returning at 15–25°C. Secondary (IT) side: treated water or water-propylene glycol mix (20–30% glycol for freeze protection if exposed to outdoor temps), 25–45°C supply, returning at 40–60°C.

Typical CDU capacity: 250–1,400 kW per unit. One CDU per 4–8 GPU racks (row-level) or one per rack for extreme density.

Chiller Plants

Water-cooled chillers (centrifugal or screw-type compressors) with cooling towers are the standard for large GPU facilities. Coefficient of Performance (COP): 5.0–7.0 (i.e., 1 kW of compressor work removes 5–7 kW of heat). Typical chiller plant: N+1 redundancy, each chiller sized at 500–2,500 tons (1,750–8,800 kW thermal).

Air-cooled chillers (no cooling tower) have lower COP (2.5–4.0) and higher noise but simpler installation. Used in smaller deployments or where water is scarce.

Primary vs. Secondary Loops

Primary loop (facility): chiller → primary pumps → header piping → CDU primary side → return to chiller. Variable-flow with VFD-driven pumps, controlled by differential pressure setpoint. Secondary loop (IT): CDU secondary side → rack manifolds → cold plates → return to CDU. Constant or variable flow, controlled by IT inlet temperature setpoint. The two loops are hydraulically separated by the CDU’s heat exchanger, so a leak in the IT loop does not drain the facility loop (and vice versa).

Free Cooling / Economizer

When outdoor wet-bulb temperature drops below the required supply water temperature, the chiller compressors can be partially or fully bypassed. A waterside economizer uses a plate heat exchanger to transfer heat directly from the primary loop to the cooling tower loop. In climates like the US Pacific Northwest or Northern Europe, free cooling can provide 3,000–6,000+ hours/year of compressor-free operation, reducing cooling energy by 40–60%.

Direct-to-chip liquid cooling enables higher supply water temperatures (35–45°C vs. 7–12°C for CRAH coils), which dramatically expands free cooling hours — warm-water cooling at 45°C supply can free-cool year-round in most climates.

Temperature & Humidity Monitoring

+
Parameter ASHRAE A1 Recommended ASHRAE A1 Allowable GPU Facility Target
Intake Air Temperature 18–27°C (64–81°F) 15–32°C (59–90°F) 20–25°C (target mid-range for headroom)
Relative Humidity Dew point: 5.5°C–15°C and ≤60% RH Dew point: -12°C–17°C and 8–80% RH 40–55% RH (avoid static and condensation)
Delta T (intake to exhaust) — — 10–15°C for air-cooled; 15–25°C for liquid (water side)
GPU Junction Temp — — <83°C sustained (H100 throttles at 83°C, shuts down at 95°C)
Liquid Supply Temp — — 25–45°C depending on design; NVIDIA recommends ≤40°C for DGX LC
SRE Alert Protocol: Configure monitoring thresholds as follows: Warning at intake >27°C or humidity >60%. Critical at intake >30°C or humidity >70%. Emergency shutdown trigger at intake >35°C or coolant supply >50°C. Use redundant sensor paths (BMC/IPMI sensors + standalone wireless sensors like Vertiv Geist Watchdog or Packet Power) to avoid single-sensor false positives.

Liquid Cooling Loop Architecture

Figure 2: Dual-loop liquid cooling architecture. The primary (facility) loop runs chilled water from the chiller plant through the CDU. The secondary (IT) loop circulates treated water from the CDU through rack manifolds to GPU/CPU cold plates. The two loops are hydraulically isolated by the CDU's plate heat exchanger. Temperature annotations show typical values for a direct-to-chip cooling deployment.

4. Floor Planning

GPU datacenter floor planning requires careful consideration of structural loading, cable routing, cooling infrastructure placement, and fire suppression — all at densities that traditional datacenter design guides don’t anticipate.

Raised Floor vs. Slab Floor

+
Attribute Raised Floor Slab (Ground-Level) Floor
Description Pedestal-supported tiles (typically 24"x24" or 600mm x 600mm) creating an underfloor plenum of 18–36 inches Equipment sits directly on concrete slab (sometimes with anti-static epoxy coating)
Cooling Air Delivery Pressurized underfloor plenum delivers cold air through perforated tiles. Good for <15 kW/rack deployments In-row cooling units, overhead ducting, or liquid cooling. Preferred for high-density GPU racks
Cable Routing Power and data cables routed under floor (can obstruct airflow if not managed); overhead cable trays also used All cables via overhead ladder rack / cable trays. Cleaner separation from cooling infrastructure
Liquid Cooling Piping Can route supply/return under floor, but leak risk in inaccessible space; joints must be above floor Piping routed overhead or at floor level; all joints accessible and visible for leak detection
Load Capacity Standard raised floor: 1,250–2,500 lbs/sq ft concentrated load. May require reinforced pedestals for GPU racks exceeding 2,000 lbs Concrete slab: typically rated for 250+ lbs/sq ft distributed; can handle any rack weight with proper spreading
GPU Superpod Preference Being phased out for new GPU-dense builds Strongly preferred for liquid-cooled GPU facilities. Better structural support, easier CDU placement, simpler plumbing access

Cable Routing

+

Overhead Cable Trays

Standard for modern GPU facilities. Ladder rack or wire basket cable trays mounted at 7–9 feet above finished floor. Separate trays for: (1) high-voltage power cables (600V rated), (2) low-voltage data cables (Cat6A, fiber), (3) liquid cooling hoses. Minimum 12-inch separation between power and data trays per NEC Article 300.3 and TIA-568 standards to prevent EMI.

Fiber & Network Cabling

GPU superpods generate massive cabling volumes. A 256-GPU DGX SuperPOD requires 256 InfiniBand cables (400G OSFP connections) from DGX nodes to leaf switches, plus spine uplinks. Each DGX H100 has 8 ConnectX-7 NICs with one 400G port each, totaling 8 cables per node. Pre-terminated fiber trunk cables (MTP/MPO-16 to LC breakout) reduce install time. Cable lengths must be carefully managed: single-mode fiber for runs >100m, multimode OM4/OM5 for <100m at 400G.

Cable Management at the Rack

Vertical cable managers on both sides of each rack. Horizontal cable managers between every 1–2U of equipment. Velcro straps (never zip ties — they damage cable jackets and are single-use). Minimum bend radius: 10x cable diameter for copper, per manufacturer spec for fiber (typically 7.5–15mm for bend-insensitive single-mode). Service loops at both ends for future re-termination.

Weight & Structural Considerations

+
Equipment Approximate Weight Structural Notes
DGX H100 node (single) ~125 lbs (57 kg) —
Full DGX rack (4 nodes + networking + PDUs) ~2,000–2,500 lbs (900–1,130 kg) Requires floor rating ≥2,500 lbs concentrated. Seismic bracing in zones 3–4
GB200 NVL72 rack ~3,000 lbs (1,360 kg) fully loaded with coolant Structural engineer review required. Floor reinforcement may be necessary on upper stories
CDU (row-level, 500kW class) ~1,500–2,000 lbs (680–900 kg) wet Requires drip pan, floor drain, and vibration isolation pads. Weight includes coolant reservoir
In-row CRAH unit ~800–1,200 lbs (360–540 kg) Standard floor rating sufficient
Rack PDU (vertical, 3-phase 60A) ~30–50 lbs (14–23 kg) each Two per rack (A+B feed); mounted vertically in 0U space
Floor Loading: Always verify floor loading capacity with the facility structural engineer before deploying GPU racks. Many colocation facilities were designed for traditional 5–10 kW racks weighing 800–1,200 lbs. A 2,500 lb GPU rack on raised floor tiles designed for 1,250 lb concentrated loads will cause tile cracking or pedestal failure. Load-spreading plates (steel plates distributing weight across multiple tiles) are a common retrofit.

Fire Suppression

+

Clean Agent Systems

GPU datacenters use gaseous clean agent fire suppression to avoid water damage to multi-million-dollar GPU infrastructure. The two dominant agents:

  • FM-200 (HFC-227ea): Stored as a liquefied compressed gas at 360–600 PSI. Discharged in ≤10 seconds. Extinguishes by heat absorption (chemical and physical). Design concentration: 7.0–8.5% by volume. Safe for occupied spaces at design concentrations (NOAEL: 9.0%). ODP: 0, GWP: 3,220 (high — being phased down in some jurisdictions under F-gas regulations).
  • 3M Novec 1230 (FK-5-1-12): Fluoroketone fluid, stored as a liquid. Design concentration: 4.2–5.9%. ODP: 0, GWP: 1 (negligible — preferred for new installations). Atmospheric lifetime: ~5 days. Safe for occupied spaces (NOAEL: 10%). Higher cost per pound than FM-200 but increasingly specified for new builds due to environmental profile.

Detection & Activation

VESDA (Very Early Smoke Detection Apparatus) is the standard for GPU data halls. Aspirating smoke detectors continuously sample air through a pipe network, detecting smoke particles at the incipient stage (0.02–0.2% obscuration/m) — far earlier than conventional spot detectors (1–3% obscuration).

Activation sequence: VESDA Alert → VESDA Action (pre-alarm, investigation) → VESDA Fire 1 (siren, strobes, abort timer starts, 30–60 sec countdown) → VESDA Fire 2 (agent discharge, HVAC dampers close, doors seal). Double-interlock (also called cross-zone): requires two independent detectors in alarm before discharge, reducing false releases.

Why Not Water?

Pre-action sprinkler systems (dry pipe, charged only on detector activation) are used in some facilities as a secondary/backup system per code requirements (NFPA 75, NFPA 76). However, wet sprinklers are never used in GPU data halls due to the risk of catastrophic water damage. A single sprinkler head activation over a row of DGX H100 racks could destroy millions of dollars of GPU hardware. Pre-action systems provide a safety net while ensuring water only flows if both a detector activates AND a sprinkler head fuses (dual-action).

Liquid Cooling & Fire Risk

Liquid-cooled GPU facilities introduce a unique consideration: water-glycol coolant in the secondary loop. While propylene glycol is not combustible at typical concentrations (20–30%), a large leak could interact with electrical equipment. Design mitigations: leak detection auto-shutoff, drip pans under all CDUs and manifolds, sloped floors to floor drains, and GFCI/ground fault protection on all circuits in proximity to liquid cooling infrastructure.

Knowledge Check: Physical Infrastructure

Test your understanding of the concepts covered in this section. Select the best answer for each question.

Question 1: Power Redundancy

A GPU datacenter uses 2N power redundancy. Each DGX H100 node has dual PSU sets (A and B). During a routine UPS maintenance window, the A-side UPS is taken offline. What is the expected impact on the running GPU training job?

Correct: B. In a 2N configuration, each power path (A and B) is independently sized to carry 100% of the IT load. The DGX H100's PSU architecture (3+3 redundancy across 6 PSUs) ensures that either the A-set or B-set alone can power the full node. When one path is taken offline for maintenance, the other continues without any performance degradation or interruption. This is what makes Tier III (concurrently maintainable) facilities viable for GPU workloads.

Question 2: Cooling Architecture

Your facility uses direct-to-chip liquid cooling with a CDU-based architecture. The monitoring system alerts that GPU junction temperatures across an entire rack have risen from 72°C to 81°C over the past 15 minutes, approaching the 83°C throttle threshold. The CDU secondary loop supply temperature reads normal at 35°C. What is the most likely root cause?

Correct: B. The key diagnostic clue is that the CDU supply temperature is normal (35°C) but GPU temperatures are rising. If the chiller or primary loop had failed (option A), the CDU supply temperature would also have risen. Since the supply is normal, the issue is flow rate, not supply temperature. Reduced flow through the cold plates means less thermal mass to absorb GPU heat per unit time, causing junction temperatures to climb even though the entering coolant temperature is correct. Common causes include a failing secondary pump, a clogged 5-20 micron inline filter, a partially closed isolation valve, or an air pocket trapped in the highest point of the loop. SRE response: check CDU secondary pump speed/status, differential pressure across the filter, and flow meter readings.

Question 3: Fire Suppression

Your facility uses Novec 1230 clean agent fire suppression with VESDA aspirating smoke detection and a double-interlock pre-action sprinkler system as backup. A VESDA detector enters "Alert" status in Zone 3 (GPU data hall). What is the correct SRE response, and at what stage would clean agent discharge occur?

Correct: C. VESDA systems have multiple alarm levels specifically to enable graduated response. Alert is the earliest stage (0.02–0.06% obscuration/m) and may indicate dust, minor contamination, or an incipient condition — investigation is required but discharge is not imminent. Action indicates higher smoke levels; the on-call team should be responding. Fire 1 triggers audible/visual alarms and starts the abort countdown. Fire 2 (or cross-zone confirmation from a second detector) triggers actual agent discharge. The 30–60 second abort timer allows personnel to evacuate and also provides an opportunity to abort a false alarm. The double-interlock pre-action sprinklers add an additional layer: even if a clean agent discharge fails to suppress the fire, water only flows if both a detector is in alarm AND a sprinkler head physically activates from heat (>68°C / 155°F). This dual-action requirement prevents accidental water release.

Racks & Hardware

From 42U steel to 700-watt GPUs — the physical foundation of AI supercomputing infrastructure.

1. IT Rack Standards

The 42U Rack — EIA-310-E

Nearly every piece of datacenter equipment is designed around the EIA-310-E standard (also referenced as IEC 60297 and DIN 41494). One rack unit (1U) equals 44.45 mm (1.75 in) of vertical mounting space. A standard full-height cabinet provides 42U of usable space — roughly 1,867 mm (73.5 in) of mounting height inside an overall cabinet that stands approximately 2,000–2,100 mm tall including casters and roof panels.

The 19-inch (482.6 mm) mounting width between the inner edges of the front vertical rails is the universal constant. Equipment faceplates are designed to span exactly this opening, secured with screws or tool-less clips into the rail holes.

Rack Dimensions & Form Factors

Parameter Standard Option Wide/Deep Option Notes
External Width 600 mm (23.6″) 800 mm (31.5″) 800 mm needed for high-density cabling or side-mount PDUs
External Depth 1,000 mm (39.4″) 1,200 mm (47.2″) GPU servers (e.g., DGX H100 at ~900 mm depth) typically require 1,200 mm
Height (with casters) 2,000–2,100 mm (42U usable) Some facilities use 48U or 52U tall racks
Mounting Width 482.6 mm (19″) between rails EIA-310-E universal standard

Open Frame vs. Enclosed Cabinet

Open Frame (2-Post / 4-Post)

  • No side panels, doors, or roof — maximum airflow
  • Lower cost, lighter weight, easier cable access
  • Used in private, access-controlled datacenter rooms
  • 2-post for lightweight network and patch panels; 4-post for servers
  • Cannot form hot/cold aisle containment without additional accessories

Enclosed Cabinet

  • Locking front and rear doors, removable side panels
  • Perforated doors (typically 64–80% perforation) for front-to-back airflow
  • Required for hot-aisle/cold-aisle containment strategies
  • Physical security for colocation environments
  • Acoustic dampening in some models (noise reduction for edge deployments)

Vertical Cable Management

Vertical cable managers run along the sides of the rack between the mounting rails and the cabinet walls. They come in single-sided (finger) or double-sided (hinged) designs. For high-density GPU racks carrying 8–16 fiber cables and multiple power cords per server, double-sided vertical managers with at least 6″ (152 mm) of cable trough width are recommended. The NVIDIA DGX H100 alone requires 10 rear-panel cable connections (8× InfiniBand + 2× management Ethernet), making vertical management essential.

Horizontal Cable Management

1U or 2U horizontal cable managers are placed between equipment to organize cables laterally and route them to the vertical managers. In GPU-dense racks, a 1U horizontal manager should be placed above each DGX node to prevent cable congestion from blocking airflow.

Blanking Panels

Every unused rack unit must be covered with a blanking panel. Without them, hot exhaust air recirculates from the rear of the rack to the front intake through the empty openings — a phenomenon called hot-air bypass. Studies show that leaving just 4U uncovered in a 42U rack can raise intake temperatures by 5–10°C, leading to thermal throttling in GPUs. Snap-in tool-less blanking panels allow rapid reconfiguration.

Mounting Hardware

  • Cage nuts & screws: Traditional square-hole mounting. Insert cage nut with a cage-nut tool, then fasten with a M6 screw. Secure but slower to reconfigure.
  • Tool-less (clip-nut / snap-in) rails: Spring-loaded latches on server rail kits that clip into the rack’s square or round holes. Most modern 1U/2U servers ship with tool-less inner rails.
  • Sliding rail kits: Telescoping rails (ball-bearing or roller) that allow servers to slide forward for maintenance without removing cables. Required for heavy systems like the DGX H100 (approx. 60 kg / 132 lbs per 8U chassis).

Weight Capacity

Rating Type Typical Range Meaning
Static Load 1,000–1,500 kg (2,200–3,300 lbs) Maximum weight when rack is stationary and bolted to the floor
Dynamic / Rolling Load 600–1,000 kg (1,320–2,200 lbs) Maximum weight while rack is being moved on casters
Seismic Rated Varies by zone Certified to withstand specified seismic forces (e.g., Zone 4 / GR-63-CORE NEBS)

A fully loaded GPU rack with 4× DGX H100 nodes (8U each = 32U), networking switches, PDUs, and cable management can easily weigh 500–700 kg. Raised-floor tiles beneath GPU racks must also be rated accordingly — standard floor tiles support ~450–570 kg per tile (concentrated load), so reinforced tiles or structural support are often required.

2. NVIDIA DGX / HGX Systems

HGX vs. DGX — Understanding the Product Line

NVIDIA’s AI compute platform is delivered in two tiers:

  • HGX (Hyperscale GPU Accelerator) is a baseboard reference design — a GPU tray containing the GPU modules, NVSwitch interconnect, and NVLink wiring. NVIDIA sells HGX baseboards to OEM partners (Dell, HPE, Lenovo, Supermicro, etc.) who integrate them into their own server chassis with their own choice of CPUs, memory, storage, and network adapters. This allows customization but requires more integration effort.
  • DGX is NVIDIA’s fully integrated, turnkey AI system. It includes the HGX baseboard plus NVIDIA-selected CPUs, memory, NVMe storage, ConnectX NICs, BlueField DPUs, the chassis, cooling, firmware, and the DGX OS (based on Ubuntu Linux). DGX systems are sold directly by NVIDIA and through select partners as validated, pre-configured appliances.

Think of HGX as the engine and DGX as the complete car.

DGX B200

The DGX B200 is the direct successor to the DGX H100, built around the NVIDIA Blackwell B200 GPU. Each B200 GPU features 208 billion transistors on a TSMC 4NP process with a dual-die design connected via a 10 TB/s chip-to-chip interconnect. Key specs per GPU:

  • 192 GB HBM3e memory at 8 TB/s bandwidth
  • Up to 9 PFLOPS FP4 Tensor performance (with sparsity)
  • NVLink 5.0: 1,800 GB/s bidirectional per GPU (2× the H100)
  • TDP: 1,000W per GPU

A DGX B200 node with 8× B200 GPUs delivers ~72 PFLOPS of FP4 AI training performance and contains 1.5 TB of HBM3e memory.

GB200 NVL72 — The Rack-Scale GPU

The GB200 NVL72 represents a fundamental shift from node-scale to rack-scale computing. A single NVL72 rack contains:

  • 36× Grace CPUs (NVIDIA’s Arm Neoverse V2-based processor, 72 cores each)
  • 72× Blackwell GPUs arranged as 36 Grace-Blackwell Superchips (each Superchip pairs 1 Grace CPU + 2 Blackwell GPUs via NVLink-C2C at 900 GB/s)
  • All 72 GPUs connected via NVLink 5.0 through 9× NVSwitch 4th gen chips, forming a single unified memory domain of ~13.5 TB HBM3e
  • Liquid-cooled design (mandatory — ~120 kW per rack)
  • ~720 PFLOPS FP4 AI performance per rack

The NVL72 is designed for trillion-parameter model training where the entire model’s weights can reside in the GPU memory of a single rack, eliminating the need for slower fabric-level communication during many training steps.

DGX SuperPOD at a Glance

A DGX SuperPOD is NVIDIA’s reference architecture for scaling DGX nodes into a cluster. For the H100 generation:

3. GPU Architecture Deep Dive

NVIDIA H100 SXM5 — Architecture Overview

The H100 is based on the Hopper microarchitecture (GH100 die). It is fabricated on TSMC’s custom 4N process (a variant of N4/N5 optimized for NVIDIA) and contains 80 billion transistors on a die area of 814 mm².

Specification H100 SXM5 H100 PCIe

FP16 Tensor1,979 TFLOPS*1,513 TFLOPS*
FP8 Tensor3,958 TFLOPS*3,026 TFLOPS*
* With sparsity (2:4 structured sparsity). Without sparsity, divide by 2.

Hopper SM Architecture

Each Hopper Streaming Multiprocessor (SM) contains:

  • 128 FP32 CUDA Cores (organized as 4 processing blocks of 32 each)
  • 4 Fourth-Generation Tensor Cores — these are the workhorses for AI training and inference. Each Tensor Core can execute matrix multiply-accumulate operations on FP8, FP16, BF16, TF32, FP64, and INT8 data types.
  • 4 Texture Units
  • 256 KB Register File per SM
  • 256 KB combined L1 / Shared Memory (configurable split, up from 192 KB in Ampere A100)

The H100 SXM5 activates 132 of the 144 SMs on the GH100 die (the remaining 12 are disabled for yield improvement). This gives 132 × 128 = 16,896 CUDA cores and 132 × 4 = 528 Tensor Cores.

The H100 introduces the Transformer Engine, a hardware-software co-design that dynamically switches between FP8 and FP16 precision on a per-tensor, per-layer basis during training. Each Tensor Core can execute FP8 matrix operations (E4M3 or E5M2 formats) at 2× the throughput of FP16, while maintaining model accuracy through automatic scaling factors managed by the engine.

The Transformer Engine stores a per-tensor scaling factor (computed from the previous iteration) that maps the dynamic range of FP8 to the actual value distribution of each tensor. This allows gradients and activations with different dynamic ranges to each use the full precision of FP8 without overflow or underflow. Layers that are sensitive to precision automatically fall back to FP16 computation.

This is why the H100 can achieve ~4 PFLOPS FP8 per GPU (with sparsity) — double the FP16 rate — while training GPT-3-class models to the same converged accuracy as FP16 training on the A100.

NVLink 4.0 & NVSwitch 3rd Generation

NVLink 4.0 is NVIDIA’s proprietary high-bandwidth, low-latency GPU interconnect. Each H100 SXM5 GPU has 18 NVLink 4.0 links, each running at 25 GB/s per direction (50 GB/s bidirectional), for an aggregate of 900 GB/s bidirectional per GPU.

Within a DGX H100 node, the 8 GPUs are connected in an all-to-all topology via 4× NVSwitch 3rd-generation ASICs. Each NVSwitch chip has:

  • 64 NVLink 4.0 ports
  • 13.6 TB/s total aggregate switching bandwidth
  • SHARP v3 (Scalable Hierarchical Aggregation and Reduction Protocol): in-network allreduce and other collective operations executed directly in the switch silicon, reducing collective communication time by up to 2×

The 4 NVSwitch chips collectively provide every GPU with a direct, full-bandwidth path to every other GPU in the node. This means GPU 0 can read from GPU 7’s HBM3 memory at the full 900 GB/s — there is no bandwidth reduction regardless of which GPU pair is communicating. This non-blocking, all-to-all fabric is critical for large-model tensor parallelism, where layers are split across GPUs and each training step requires all GPUs to exchange activations and gradients.

DGX H100 Internal Architecture — NVSwitch All-to-All Topology

PCIe Gen5 & CPU-GPU Communication

While NVLink provides the primary high-bandwidth path between GPUs, the PCIe Gen5 bus connects each GPU to its host CPU and to its dedicated ConnectX-7 NIC. PCIe Gen5 x16 provides 128 GB/s bidirectional (64 GB/s per direction), which is sufficient for:

  • Feeding training data from NVMe storage through the CPU to GPU memory
  • Moving gradient data from GPU to ConnectX-7 NIC for inter-node allreduce over InfiniBand
  • CPU-side operations (data preprocessing, checkpoint writes)

In the DGX H100, each GPU has a dedicated PCIe Gen5 x16 connection to a ConnectX-7 NIC, ensuring GPUDirect RDMA can transfer data directly from GPU memory to the network without staging through CPU memory. This is critical for achieving line-rate 400 Gb/s InfiniBand throughput.

Multi-Instance GPU (MIG) allows a single H100 to be securely partitioned into up to 7 independent GPU instances. Each MIG instance has:

  • Its own dedicated set of SMs (CUDA cores and Tensor Cores)
  • Its own partition of HBM3 memory and memory bandwidth
  • Its own L2 cache partition
  • Its own video decode and JPEG engines
  • Fault isolation — an error in one instance cannot affect another
MIG Profile SMs Memory Memory BW Use Case
1g.10gb1610 GB~480 GB/sInference (small models)
2g.20gb3220 GB~960 GB/sInference (medium models)
3g.40gb4840 GB~1.6 TB/sTraining / fine-tuning
4g.40gb6440 GB~1.6 TB/sTraining / fine-tuning
7g.80gb13280 GB3.35 TB/sFull GPU (no partition)

MIG is managed via nvidia-smi mig commands or Kubernetes device plugins. In a multi-tenant inference cluster, MIG allows a single H100 to serve 7 different users or models simultaneously with hardware-level isolation — far more secure than time-slicing or MPS (Multi-Process Service).

Note: MIG is available on both H100 SXM5 and H100 PCIe variants. When MIG is enabled, NVLink between GPUs is not available for the individual MIG instances — each instance sees only its own partition. This is a deliberate design choice for isolation.

4. DGX SuperPOD Architecture

Scaling Hierarchy

NVIDIA defines a clear scaling hierarchy for H100-based infrastructure:

Scale Unit Composition GPUs FP8 (Sparse)
Scalable Unit (SU) 1× DGX H100 8 ~32 PFLOPS
SuperPOD 32× DGX H100 256 ~1 ExaFLOP
Multi-SuperPOD (example: 4x) 128× DGX H100 1,024 ~4 ExaFLOPS

SuperPOD Networking — Leaf-Spine InfiniBand Fabric

The DGX H100 SuperPOD uses a two-tier leaf-spine fat-tree topology built entirely with NVIDIA Quantum-2 QM9700 InfiniBand NDR switches (64-port, 400 Gb/s per port).

Compute Fabric

  • Leaf switches: Each QM9700 leaf switch connects to 32 DGX ports (one per NIC, across multiple DGX nodes). With 64 ports per switch, the remaining 32 ports uplink to spine switches. This gives a 1:1 subscription ratio (full bisection bandwidth).
  • Spine switches: The spine layer aggregates all leaf switches. For a 32-node SuperPOD with 256 ConnectX-7 NICs, the fabric uses 8 leaf switches and 8 spine switches (Quantum-2 QM9700), delivering a non-blocking fabric where any DGX node can communicate with any other node at the full 400 Gb/s per NIC × 8 NICs = 3.2 Tb/s per node.

Storage & Management Fabric

  • The 64 BlueField-3 DPUs (2 per node) connect to a separate InfiniBand fabric dedicated to storage traffic (connecting to NVIDIA-qualified parallel file systems like Lustre, GPFS/Spectrum Scale, or VAST Data) and in-band management.
  • An additional out-of-band Ethernet management network provides BMC/IPMI access.

DGX H100 SuperPOD — Leaf-Spine InfiniBand Fabric

For workloads requiring more than 256 GPUs, multiple SuperPODs are interconnected by adding a third tier of spine switches (sometimes called “super-spines” or “core switches”). Each SuperPOD’s existing spine switches become effectively “aggregation” switches that uplink to the super-spine layer.

NVIDIA’s Eos supercomputer (used internally for AI research) is built from 4,608 H100 GPUs (18 SuperPODs’ worth) using this multi-tier approach. The Meta AI Research SuperCluster (RSC) and Microsoft Azure’s ND H100 v5 clusters use similar architectures at even larger scale (tens of thousands of H100s).

At these scales, careful attention must be paid to:

  • Adaptive routing: InfiniBand NDR supports adaptive routing to avoid congestion hotspots in the fabric
  • Rail-optimized topology: NVIDIA’s “rail-optimized” design connects the same-numbered NIC from each DGX (e.g., NIC 3 from all 32 nodes) to the same leaf switch. This means allreduce patterns that operate per-rail see minimal switch hops.
  • Job placement: Schedulers (e.g., NVIDIA Base Command Manager) topology-aware scheduling to place multi-node jobs on nodes sharing leaf switches whenever possible
  • SHARP (Scalable Hierarchical Aggregation and Reduction Protocol): Offloads allreduce collective operations to the switch ASICs themselves, reducing network traffic by up to 2× for data-parallel training

5. NVIDIA BlueField DPU

What is a DPU (Data Processing Unit)?

A Data Processing Unit (DPU) is a specialized processor that combines a high-performance network interface with a programmable multi-core CPU and hardware acceleration engines. It sits on the PCIe bus alongside GPUs and CPUs but handles infrastructure-level tasks that would otherwise consume host CPU cycles or require dedicated appliances:

  • Network virtualization: VXLAN/Geneve encap/decap, OVS offload, connection tracking
  • Storage disaggregation: NVMe-oF (NVMe over Fabrics) target and initiator, compression, encryption
  • Security: IPsec/TLS inline encryption, hardware root of trust, secure boot chain
  • Telemetry: Per-flow monitoring, congestion detection, What Just Happened (WJH) analytics

In a DGX H100, the 2 BlueField-3 DPUs handle all storage I/O and management-plane networking, freeing the host CPUs and GPUs to focus exclusively on AI computation. The DPU runs its own ARM-based operating system (typically Ubuntu on the ARM cores) and presents virtualized devices to the host.

BlueField-3 Specifications

Specification BlueField-3
Network Speed400 Gb/s InfiniBand NDR or Ethernet
Network Ports1x 400G or 2x 200G (OSFP/QSFP)
ARM CPU Cores16x Arm Cortex-A78 cores (up to 3.0 GHz)
On-chip Memory16 GB DDR5 (on-board, dedicated to DPU)
PCIeGen5 x16 host interface
Crypto EnginesHardware IPsec, TLS, AES-XTS (line-rate 400G)
Regex EnginesHardware regex accelerators for DPI / IDS
Storage OffloadNVMe-oF SNAP (Software-defined Network Accelerated Processing)
Virtio EmulationHardware virtio-net, virtio-blk acceleration
RoCE / IBRDMA with GPUDirect Storage support
TDP~75W
Form FactorOSFP or OCP 3.0 SFF
SDKNVIDIA DOCA (Data Center Infrastructure on a Chip Architecture)

DOCA (Data Center Infrastructure on a Chip Architecture) is NVIDIA’s SDK for programming BlueField DPUs. It provides:

  • DOCA Flow: Hardware-offloaded packet processing pipeline (match + action, similar to P4 or OpenFlow concepts). Programs the DPU’s eSwitch hardware to process packets at line rate without involving the ARM cores.
  • DOCA GPUNetIO: Enables GPUDirect RDMA from GPU memory through the DPU, allowing GPU-to-storage data paths that bypass the host CPU entirely.
  • DOCA Compress / Decompress: Hardware-accelerated LZ4 and Deflate for inline data reduction on storage traffic.
  • DOCA SHA / AES / Regex: Cryptographic and pattern-matching acceleration for security workloads.
  • DOCA App Shield: Runtime integrity monitoring of the host OS and applications from the DPU’s isolated security domain.

Developers write DOCA applications in C, which run on the BlueField’s ARM cores. The DOCA libraries abstract the DPU’s hardware accelerators, allowing developers to express intent (e.g., “encrypt this flow at line rate”) without managing register-level hardware details. DOCA also provides a gRPC-based orchestration layer so that a central controller can configure DPU policies across hundreds of nodes.

In the DGX SuperPOD context, the BlueField-3 DPUs are primarily used for:

  1. GPUDirect Storage: Moving training data from the parallel file system directly to GPU memory at 200 Gb/s per DPU, bypassing host CPU and system memory. This is essential for keeping 8 H100 GPUs fed with data during training.
  2. In-band management: Running NVIDIA Base Command Manager agents on the DPU’s ARM cores for health monitoring, telemetry collection, and firmware updates without impacting the host OS.
  3. Network isolation: Providing tenant-level isolation in multi-user environments by virtualizing the storage network through the DPU’s hardware SR-IOV and virtio capabilities.

DPU vs. SmartNIC vs. Standard NIC

Knowledge Check

Question 1: NVSwitch Topology

In a DGX H100 node, how are the 8 H100 GPUs interconnected via NVSwitch?

Correct: C. The DGX H100 uses 4 NVSwitch 3rd-gen chips to create a non-blocking all-to-all topology. Each of the 8 GPUs connects to all 4 NVSwitch chips. The switches act as crossbars, routing traffic so that any GPU pair communicates at the full 900 GB/s bidirectional NVLink bandwidth. This is not a direct mesh (which would require 28 separate point-to-point links) — the NVSwitch chips provide the switching fabric that makes the all-to-all connectivity feasible in the 8-GPU package.

Question 2: DGX SuperPOD Scale

A DGX H100 SuperPOD consists of 32 DGX H100 nodes. What is the total number of ConnectX-7 NICs used for the compute InfiniBand fabric, and why is this number significant?

Correct: B. Each DGX H100 contains 8 ConnectX-7 NICs — one per GPU — connected via PCIe Gen5 x16. This 1:1 GPU-to-NIC ratio ensures that each GPU can perform GPUDirect RDMA over InfiniBand at 400 Gb/s independently and simultaneously, giving each node 8 × 400 Gb/s = 3.2 Tb/s of aggregate fabric bandwidth. Across 32 nodes, that is 256 NICs total. The 64 BlueField-3 DPUs (2 per node) are on a separate storage/management fabric and are not counted in the compute NIC total.

Question 3: BlueField DPU Role

What is the primary reason the DGX H100 includes 2 BlueField-3 DPUs in addition to its 8 ConnectX-7 NICs?

Correct: D. BlueField-3 DPUs are infrastructure processors, not compute accelerators. They run their own ARM-based OS and handle storage traffic (via NVMe-oF / GPUDirect Storage), in-band management (NVIDIA Base Command Manager), security (hardware IPsec/TLS), and telemetry. This separation ensures that the host Xeon CPUs are not burdened with storage and management overhead during AI training, where CPU cycles are needed for data loading and preprocessing. The DPUs connect to a separate InfiniBand fabric dedicated to storage, distinct from the compute fabric used by the ConnectX-7 NICs.

03

Physical Networking & RDMA Fabric

InfiniBand, RoCE, GPUDirect, and the cabling that binds a GPU Superpod together

1. InfiniBand Deep Dive

What is InfiniBand? InfiniBand (IB) is a lossless, switched-fabric interconnect designed from the ground up for Remote Direct Memory Access (RDMA). Unlike Ethernet, which was originally a shared-medium broadcast protocol adapted over decades for datacenter use, InfiniBand was purpose-built for low-latency, high-bandwidth, credit-based communication between compute nodes. It provides kernel-bypass data transfers where application memory is registered with the NIC (called an HCA—Host Channel Adapter in IB terminology) and data moves directly between application buffers across the network without any CPU intervention on the data path.

InfiniBand Speed Generations

InfiniBand defines per-lane signaling rates. Links are assembled from 1x, 4x, or 12x lanes. In HPC/AI, 4x links are standard. Each generation roughly doubles the per-lane rate of the previous.

Generation Per-Lane Rate Encoding Effective Per-Lane 4x Link BW Year (approx.)
SDR 2.5 Gb/s 8b/10b 2.0 Gb/s 8 Gb/s 2001
DDR 5.0 Gb/s 8b/10b 4.0 Gb/s 16 Gb/s 2005
QDR 10.0 Gb/s 8b/10b 8.0 Gb/s 32 Gb/s 2008
FDR 14.0625 Gb/s 64b/66b 13.64 Gb/s 54.5 Gb/s (~56) 2011
EDR 25.78125 Gb/s 64b/66b 25.0 Gb/s 100 Gb/s 2014
HDR 50.0 Gb/s 64b/66b 48.48 Gb/s 200 Gb/s 2018
NDR 100.0 Gb/s 64b/66b + FEC ~96.97 Gb/s 400 Gb/s 2022
XDR 200.0 Gb/s 64b/66b + FEC ~193.94 Gb/s 800 Gb/s 2025
Link width notation: You will see ports labeled as 4X NDR or 4X HDR. This means 4 lanes at that generation's per-lane rate. A 4X NDR port delivers 4 × 100 Gb/s = 400 Gb/s signaling rate. 12x links exist in the spec but are extremely rare in modern deployments—4x is the universal standard for compute interconnects.

Subnet Manager (SM)

An InfiniBand fabric requires a Subnet Manager to function. Unlike Ethernet where switches independently learn MAC addresses and forward frames, IB is centrally managed. The SM discovers the fabric topology, assigns Local Identifiers (LIDs) to every port, computes forwarding tables (linear forwarding tables in each switch), and pushes them out. Without a running SM, no traffic flows.

OpenSM

The open-source reference implementation of the IB Subnet Manager, part of the OFED (OpenFabrics Enterprise Distribution) stack. OpenSM runs as a daemon on a management node (or on a switch with a management CPU). It supports multiple routing algorithms:

  • Min-Hop: Default algorithm; routes based on minimum hop count
  • Up/Down: Prevents routing loops in non-pure-fat-tree topologies by assigning direction to links
  • Fat-Tree: Optimized for fat-tree topologies; gives balanced dispersive routing across uplinks
  • DFSSSP: Deadlock-Free Single-Source Shortest Path for arbitrary topologies

OpenSM typically recalculates routes on topology changes (link up/down events). The -R flag sets the routing engine. Configuration lives in /etc/opensm/opensm.conf.

NVIDIA UFM (Unified Fabric Manager)

UFM is NVIDIA's commercial IB fabric management platform. It provides an embedded SM plus advanced features far beyond OpenSM:

  • Real-time telemetry: Per-port counters, error rates, congestion detection
  • Adaptive routing: Dynamically adjusts paths based on congestion signals
  • SHARP aggregation management: Configures in-network computing trees
  • Health monitoring: Detects BER (Bit Error Rate) degradation, flapping links, and cable faults before they cause job failures
  • Event-driven automation: REST API and webhook integrations for alerting/remediation
  • Topology visualization: Web GUI with full fabric map

In production Superpod deployments, UFM is essentially mandatory. It runs on a dedicated management node and communicates with the IB fabric via in-band management datagrams (MADs).

RDMA Core Concepts

RDMA communication is built on three fundamental kernel-bypass objects:

Queue Pairs (QP)

A QP is the fundamental communication endpoint. Each QP consists of a Send Queue (SQ) and a Receive Queue (RQ). Applications post Work Requests (WRs) to these queues. The HCA processes WRs asynchronously without kernel involvement. QP types include:

  • RC (Reliable Connected): Point-to-point, guaranteed delivery, in-order. Used for most HPC/AI traffic (NCCL collective operations, MPI point-to-point).
  • UC (Unreliable Connected): Point-to-point, no ACK/retransmit. Rarely used.
  • UD (Unreliable Datagram): One-to-any, message size limited to MTU. Used for management traffic, service discovery.
  • XRC (Extended Reliable Connected): Reduces QP count by sharing receive queues. Important for large-scale jobs where N×N QPs would exhaust HCA resources.

Completion Queues (CQ)

When the HCA finishes processing a WR, it posts a Completion Queue Entry (CQE) to the associated CQ. Applications poll the CQ (low-latency) or block on an event (lower CPU cost). A single CQ can serve multiple QPs. Completion status tells you if the operation succeeded or reports error codes (e.g., IBV_WC_REM_ACCESS_ERR for remote memory access violations).

Memory Regions (MR)

Before any RDMA transfer, memory must be registered with the HCA via ibv_reg_mr(). Registration pins the pages in physical memory (preventing swapout), creates a virtual-to-physical mapping in the HCA's page tables, and returns a pair of keys:

  • lkey (Local Key): Used by the local side to reference this memory in WRs
  • rkey (Remote Key): Shared with remote side to allow RDMA Read/Write into this region

Memory registration is expensive (kernel call, TLB shootdowns). Production codes pre-register buffers. Libraries like UCX and NCCL maintain registration caches to amortize cost.

The Verbs API (provided by libibverbs) is the standard programming interface for RDMA. It is intentionally low-level, giving applications direct control over HCA resources. The typical workflow:

  1. ibv_get_device_list() — enumerate HCAs in the system
  2. ibv_open_device() — open a device context
  3. ibv_alloc_pd() — create a Protection Domain (isolation boundary)
  4. ibv_reg_mr() — register memory
  5. ibv_create_cq() — create completion queue
  6. ibv_create_qp() — create queue pair
  7. Transition QP through states: RESET → INIT → RTR → RTS
  8. ibv_post_send() / ibv_post_recv() — post work requests
  9. ibv_poll_cq() — poll for completions

RDMA Operation Types

Operation Initiator Remote CPU Involved? Description
Send / Receive Sender Yes (must pre-post receive) Two-sided operation. Receiver must have a Receive WR posted. Data lands in receiver's buffer. Like traditional message passing.
RDMA Write Writer No One-sided. Writer specifies remote address + rkey. Data is placed directly into remote memory. Remote CPU is unaware. Used heavily by NCCL for GPU-to-GPU transfers.
RDMA Read Reader No One-sided. Reader pulls data from remote memory. Remote CPU is unaware. Useful for polling remote status or fetching shared state.
Atomic (CAS, Fetch&Add) Initiator No Performs atomic read-modify-write on remote memory. Compare-and-Swap (CAS) and Fetch-and-Add are the two standard atomics. Executed by the remote HCA, not the remote CPU.

InfiniBand uses Partition Keys (PKeys) to segment traffic on a shared fabric, analogous to VLANs on Ethernet. Each PKey is a 16-bit value. The high bit indicates full vs. limited membership:

  • Full member (0x8xxx): Can communicate with any member of the partition (full or limited)
  • Limited member (0x0xxx): Can only communicate with full members, not other limited members

Every IB port has a PKey table (up to 128 entries on ConnectX HCAs). The default partition is PKey 0x7FFF (limited) or 0xFFFF (full), and all ports must be members. The SM configures PKey tables based on the partitions.conf file.

Use cases in Superpod:

  • Separate storage traffic (Lustre/GPFS) from compute (MPI/NCCL) traffic
  • Isolate tenants in multi-tenant clusters
  • Restrict management traffic to admin partitions

Key difference from VLANs: PKeys provide membership enforcement at the HCA level. A node cannot forge PKey membership—the SM controls the PKey table, and the HCA enforces it in hardware.

InfiniBand vs Ethernet: Design Philosophy

Credit-Based Flow Control

InfiniBand is lossless by design. Every link uses credit-based flow control: a sender can only transmit when it has credits from the receiver, meaning the receiver has buffer space. This is fundamentally different from Ethernet, where packets are sent optimistically and may be dropped if a switch buffer overflows.

  • InfiniBand: Sender checks credits → transmits only if receiver has buffer space → zero packet drops on a healthy fabric. Congestion manifests as back-pressure (reduced throughput), not drops.
  • Ethernet: Sender transmits immediately → switch may drop if buffer overflows → relies on TCP retransmits for reliability. Drops are expected and handled by upper layers.

This is why InfiniBand achieves consistently lower latency: there is no retransmission overhead, no TCP slow-start, and the fabric provides hardware-level reliability guarantees. For RDMA workloads where a single dropped packet can stall a GPU collective operation across thousands of GPUs, this lossless guarantee is critical.

2. RoCE (RDMA over Converged Ethernet)

Why RoCE? RoCE enables RDMA semantics (kernel bypass, zero-copy, low latency) over standard Ethernet infrastructure. This lets organizations leverage existing Ethernet switches, cabling, and operational expertise while getting most of the performance benefits of RDMA. It is especially compelling when InfiniBand's specialized switch infrastructure is not available or when Ethernet is already deployed at scale.

RoCEv1 vs RoCEv2

Feature RoCEv1 RoCEv2
Encapsulation IB transport over Ethernet (EtherType 0x8915) IB transport over UDP/IP over Ethernet
Routing L2 only (same VLAN / broadcast domain) L3 routable (crosses subnets)
UDP Port N/A Destination port 4791; source port is a hash of QP/flow for ECMP
ECMP Support No (L2 only) Yes—switches hash on UDP source port for multi-path load balancing
Deployment Legacy, rarely used Industry standard for all modern RDMA-over-Ethernet deployments

In practice, RoCEv2 is the only version deployed today. The UDP encapsulation solved the critical limitation of RoCEv1 (L2-only), enabling ECMP across multi-tier leaf-spine fabrics.

Making Ethernet Lossless: PFC, ECN, and DCQCN

RDMA assumes a lossless transport. A single dropped packet causes an RDMA operation to fail at the transport level (unlike TCP, which retransmits transparently). Ethernet is inherently lossy, so achieving lossless behavior requires careful configuration of several mechanisms working together:

Priority Flow Control (PFC) — IEEE 802.1Qbb

PFC extends Ethernet pause frames to work on a per-priority basis (using 802.1p priority bits in the VLAN tag). When a switch input buffer for a given priority exceeds a threshold, it sends a PFC pause frame to the upstream sender for that priority only, leaving other priorities unaffected.

  • RoCEv2 traffic is typically assigned to priority 3 (configurable via DSCP-to-priority mapping)
  • PFC is enabled only for the RoCE priority class—other traffic classes (management, best-effort) remain lossy
  • PFC deadlock risk: If a circular buffer dependency forms across switches, PFC pause frames can cascade and deadlock the entire fabric. This is a real operational risk in leaf-spine topologies and requires careful buffer tuning and sometimes PFC watchdog timers.

PFC configuration example (NVIDIA Spectrum switch, Cumulus Linux):

# Enable PFC on priority 3 only
nv set qos pfc priority 3 enable on
nv set interface swp1-64 qos pfc priority 3 enable on

# Set PFC buffer thresholds
nv set qos pfc priority 3 xoff-threshold 80000
nv set qos pfc priority 3 xon-threshold 40000

Explicit Congestion Notification (ECN) — RFC 3168

ECN is the early-warning system that prevents PFC from ever firing under normal conditions. When a switch buffer occupancy exceeds a configured threshold (but before it is full enough to trigger PFC), the switch marks packets by setting the ECN bits in the IP header to CE (Congestion Experienced, binary 11).

  • ECN operates at L3 (IP header) and works per-packet
  • The receiver detects the CE marking and signals congestion back to the sender via a CNP (Congestion Notification Packet)
  • The sender reduces its injection rate in response
  • ECN thresholds must be set lower than PFC thresholds so that rate reduction kicks in before any pause is needed

DCQCN (Data Center QoS Congestion Notification)

DCQCN is the end-to-end congestion control algorithm used in RoCEv2 networks. It combines ECN marking at the switches with rate-limiting at the sender NIC. The algorithm:

  1. Switch marks packets with ECN when queue depth exceeds threshold (RED/WRED-style probabilistic marking)
  2. Receiver generates CNP (Congestion Notification Packet) back to sender when it sees CE-marked packets
  3. Sender reduces rate by a configurable factor (alpha) upon receiving CNP
  4. Sender gradually recovers rate using timer-based increase, byte-counter-based increase, or a combination (similar to TCP's AIMD but tuned for datacenter RTTs of ~10µs)

DCQCN parameters on ConnectX NICs are tuned via mlxreg or mlnx_qos. Key knobs include the rate-reduction factor, recovery timer interval, and the number of CNPs before rate cut. Mistuning causes either excessive PFC (thresholds too high) or unnecessary throughput reduction (thresholds too low).

InfiniBand vs RoCE: Side-by-Side Comparison

InfiniBand Characteristics

  • Latency: ~0.6–1.0 µs end-to-end (measured via ib_write_lat)
  • Lossless by design: Credit-based flow control at every hop. No configuration needed—it just works.
  • Subnet Manager: Central control plane computes all routes. Enables advanced features like adaptive routing, QoS SLs (Service Levels), and virtual lanes (VLs).
  • Fabric management: UFM provides deep telemetry, health monitoring, and topology awareness. SM detects and reroutes around failed links automatically.
  • In-network computing: NVIDIA SHARP performs MPI_Allreduce, MPI_Barrier, and other collectives inside the switch network, reducing traffic and latency for AI training.
  • Partitioning: PKeys enforced in hardware at the HCA for strong multi-tenant isolation.
  • Switches: Requires purpose-built InfiniBand switches (Quantum-2, etc.). These are not interchangeable with Ethernet switches.
  • Cabling: Same physical cables (QSFP-DD, fiber, DAC) but different electrical signaling. IB and Ethernet ports are not cross-compatible.

RoCE (v2) Characteristics

  • Latency: ~2–3 µs end-to-end (additional overhead from Ethernet framing, PFC/ECN processing)
  • Lossless via configuration: Requires PFC + ECN + DCQCN all configured correctly across every switch and NIC. A single misconfigured switch can cause drops and RDMA failures.
  • Routing: Standard IP/Ethernet routing (BGP, OSPF, ECMP). No special control plane needed, but ECMP hashing must be tuned for RoCE flows.
  • Fabric management: Uses standard Ethernet NOS (Cumulus, SONiC, EOS). Monitoring via SNMP, gNMI, streaming telemetry. Less IB-specific insight.
  • No in-network computing: SHARP is InfiniBand-only. RoCE relies entirely on host-side collectives (NCCL tree/ring algorithms).
  • Isolation: Standard Ethernet VLANs, ACLs, VRFs. Weaker than PKey enforcement but well-understood operationally.
  • Switches: Standard Ethernet switches (Spectrum-4, Tomahawk, Trident, etc.). Same switches can carry RoCE, TCP, and management traffic.
  • Operational familiarity: Most network engineers know Ethernet. IB requires specialized skills.

Head-to-Head Comparison

Dimension InfiniBand (NDR 400G) RoCE v2 (400GbE)
Latency ~0.6–1.0 µs ~2–3 µs
Bandwidth 400 Gb/s per port 400 Gb/s per port
Lossless mechanism Native credit-based flow control PFC + ECN + DCQCN (complex config)
Configuration complexity SM handles routing (simpler data plane) PFC/ECN/DCQCN tuning per switch (complex)
ECMP / multi-path Adaptive routing via SM Standard IP ECMP (hash-based)
In-network computing SHARP (allreduce in switches) Not available
Switch cost Higher (IB-only switches) Lower (commodity Ethernet switches)
Operational expertise Specialized IB skills needed Standard Ethernet skills
Multi-tenant isolation PKeys (hardware-enforced) VLANs / VRFs (software/switch-enforced)
Superpod recommendation Preferred for DGX/HGX clusters Viable for smaller-scale or Ethernet-only shops
When to use which: NVIDIA Superpod reference architectures specify InfiniBand as the primary compute fabric due to its lossless guarantees, lower latency, adaptive routing, and SHARP in-network computing. RoCE is a strong choice when building on existing Ethernet infrastructure, when the cluster is smaller (<256 GPUs), or when Ethernet is mandated by organizational policy. Many large deployments use both: InfiniBand for GPU-to-GPU compute traffic and Ethernet for storage, management, and out-of-band access.

3. NVIDIA Networking Hardware

NVIDIA ConnectX-7 Network Adapter

The ConnectX-7 is the current-generation SmartNIC/HCA used in DGX H100 and HGX H100 systems. Each DGX H100 has eight ConnectX-7 adapters (one per GPU), plus additional ports for storage and management.

Specification Detail
Port speed 1x 400Gb/s or 2x 200Gb/s (InfiniBand NDR / Ethernet)
Host interface PCIe Gen5 x16 (64 GT/s, ~63 GB/s bidirectional)
Protocol support InfiniBand and Ethernet (dual-protocol, configured at boot)
RDMA Native IB Verbs, RoCEv2, iWARP
GPUDirect RDMA Yes—direct NIC-to-GPU memory path via PCIe P2P
GPUDirect Storage Yes—NVMe/NVMe-oF direct to GPU memory
Crypto offload IPsec and TLS inline encryption/decryption at line rate
RoCE offloads Hardware DCQCN, ECN marking, PFC
Form factor QSFP-DD (single-port 400G) or OSFP
Firmware management mlxfwmanager, mst (Mellanox Software Tools)
SRE note: ConnectX firmware updates are critical for stability. Use mlxfwmanager --online -u to update from NVIDIA's firmware repository. Always update in a maintenance window—firmware activation requires link reset or reboot. Check current firmware with mlxfwmanager --query or ibstat.

NVIDIA Quantum-2 InfiniBand Switch (QM9700)

The Quantum-2 ASIC powers NVIDIA's NDR InfiniBand switches. In a Superpod, these switches form the leaf and spine layers of the compute fabric.

Specification Detail
Ports 64 ports × 400 Gb/s NDR (or 128 × 200 Gb/s NDR200)
Aggregate bandwidth 51.2 Tb/s
Latency ~90 ns port-to-port (cut-through)
Adaptive routing Hardware-based adaptive routing with real-time port load monitoring
SHARP (in-network computing) Supports SHARP v3—performs allreduce, reduce-scatter, allgather, and barrier operations inside the switch ASIC
Virtual lanes Up to 32 VLs for QoS traffic separation
Congestion control Credit-based flow control + congestion control (CC) with dedicated congestion management tables
Form factors QM9700 (1U fixed), QM9790 (modular chassis)
Management In-band via SM/MADs, out-of-band via serial/Ethernet management port, UFM integration

SHARP (Scalable Hierarchical Aggregation and Reduction Protocol) offloads collective operations from the hosts into the switch network itself. Instead of data traversing the full tree for an allreduce (up to root, then back down), intermediate switches perform partial reductions as data passes through:

  • Allreduce: Each leaf switch aggregates (sums/max/min) data from its connected nodes, sends the partial result up. Spine switches aggregate partials from leaf switches. The final result propagates back down. This reduces network traffic by up to 2x and latency by the depth of the tree.
  • Barrier: Implemented as a zero-byte allreduce. Completes in O(log N) switch hops instead of O(N) host-to-host messages.
  • Supported data types: FP16, BF16, FP32, FP64, INT32, INT64
  • NCCL integration: NCCL 2.12+ supports SHARP via the NCCL_COLLNET_ENABLE=1 environment variable. The SHARP plugin (libnccl-net-sharp.so) handles tree construction and in-network reduction.

SHARP is particularly impactful for large-scale LLM training where allreduce of gradient tensors dominates communication time. Benchmarks show 10–30% training throughput improvement on 256+ GPU jobs.

NVIDIA Spectrum-4 Ethernet Switch (SN5600)

The Spectrum-4 ASIC powers NVIDIA's top-of-line Ethernet switches, used for RoCE compute fabrics, storage networks, and in-band management.

Specification Detail
Ports 64 × 400GbE (or 128 × 200GbE, or mixed breakout configs)
Aggregate bandwidth 51.2 Tb/s
Latency ~300 ns port-to-port (cut-through)
Buffer ~132 MB on-chip shared buffer (critical for PFC/lossless operation)
RoCE support Full PFC, ECN/WRED marking, DCQCN-aware per-priority queuing
Adaptive routing Hardware adaptive routing across ECMP paths based on real-time port load
NOS Cumulus Linux or NVIDIA DOCA-based SONiC

Adaptive routing on Spectrum-4 dynamically selects the least-congested ECMP next-hop for each packet, rather than using a static hash. Traditional ECMP hashes on the 5-tuple (src IP, dst IP, src port, dst port, protocol) to pick a single path per flow. This can lead to hash collisions where multiple large flows land on the same link while others are idle.

Spectrum-4's adaptive routing monitors real-time egress queue depths across all ECMP members and steers packets to the least-loaded path. This happens per-packet (or per-flowlet), providing much better load balancing for elephant flows common in RDMA/NCCL traffic patterns.

  • Per-packet adaptive routing: Maximum load balancing, but may cause packet reordering. Requires receiver-side reordering support (RoCE NICs handle this in hardware).
  • Flowlet-based adaptive routing: Only changes path during idle gaps in a flow, avoiding reordering. Good middle ground.

This is configured via Cumulus/SONiC as adaptive-routing on ECMP groups, and is one of the key advantages of Spectrum-4 over commodity Ethernet ASICs for RoCE deployments.

4. Cables and Transceivers

Cable Types

Cable Type Max Reach Cost Latency Use Case
DAC (Direct Attach Copper) <5 m (typically 1–3 m) Lowest Lowest (no E/O conversion) Within-rack connections (server to ToR switch). Passive DACs up to ~3m at 400G; active DACs slightly further.
AOC (Active Optical Cable) 5–30 m (up to 100m for some) Moderate Low (integrated optics) Intra-row and short inter-row connections. Between racks within the same pod. Fixed cable—cannot swap transceivers.
Fiber + Transceiver 30 m to 10 km+ Highest Low (E/O conversion adds ~5 ns) Inter-row, inter-pod, inter-building connections. Most flexible—swap transceivers or cables independently.
SRE cost optimization: In a Superpod, the vast majority of cables are intra-rack DACs (server to leaf switch) and short AOCs (leaf to spine within the same row). Only spine-to-core or cross-building links need structured fiber with transceivers. DACs can save 5–10x per link versus transceiver + fiber combinations.

Transceiver Form Factors

Port / Connector Form Factor Comparison

Form Factor Lanes Max Speed Common Uses
SFP+ 1 10 Gb/s 10GbE management interfaces, legacy storage
SFP28 1 25 Gb/s 25GbE server connectivity, breakout from 100G
SFP56 1 50 Gb/s 50GbE, breakout from 200G
QSFP28 4 100 Gb/s 100GbE, EDR InfiniBand (100 Gb/s), breakout to 4x25G
QSFP56 4 200 Gb/s HDR InfiniBand (200 Gb/s), 200GbE
QSFP-DD 8 400 Gb/s NDR InfiniBand (400 Gb/s), 400GbE. Double-density: 8 lanes in a QSFP-compatible faceplate.
OSFP 8 800 Gb/s XDR InfiniBand (800 Gb/s), 800GbE. Wider than QSFP-DD with better thermal envelope for higher power optics.

Fiber Types

Fiber Type Core Size Standard 400G Reach Connector Use Case
MMF OM3 50 µm IEEE 802.3bs ~70 m MPO-16 / MPO-12 Within-room, legacy installs
MMF OM4 50 µm IEEE 802.3bs ~100 m MPO-16 / MPO-12 Standard datacenter intra-building. Most common choice for Superpod.
MMF OM5 50 µm TIA-492AAAE ~150 m MPO-16 / MPO-12 Extended reach MMF, supports SWDM (Short Wavelength Division Mux)
SMF OS2 9 µm IEEE 802.3cu 2–10+ km LC duplex / CS Inter-building, campus, long-haul. Required for distances >150m at 400G.

MPO/MTP (Multi-fiber Push On / Mechanical Transfer Push-on)

Multi-fiber connectors that bundle 8, 12, 16, or 24 fibers into a single ferrule. Used for parallel optics in high-speed links:

  • MPO-12: 12 fibers. Used for 40GbE (4 Tx + 4 Rx, 4 unused) and 100GbE SR4.
  • MPO-16: 16 fibers. Used for 400GbE SR8 (8 Tx + 8 Rx). Becoming the standard for 400G MMF deployments.
  • MPO-24: 24 fibers. Used for higher-density trunk cables and some 400G variants.
  • Polarity matters: MPO connectors can be Type A (straight), Type B (reversed), or Type C (pair-flipped). Mismatched polarity causes link failures. Always verify polarity type when connecting structured cabling.

LC (Lucent Connector)

Small form-factor duplex connector (2 fibers: Tx + Rx). The standard for SMF connections and SFP-based transceivers. LC connectors use a 1.25 mm ferrule and snap-in latch. At 400G, LC is used with DR4 and FR4 transceivers that use WDM (4 wavelengths over 2 fibers).

CS (Compact SN) Connector
