A newer, smaller duplex connector designed for higher faceplate density. Used in some 400G and 800G transceiver designs where LC is too large. Same fiber alignment as LC but ~40% smaller footprint.

Breakout Cables

Breakout cables (also called fan-out or splitter cables) split a single high-speed port into multiple lower-speed ports. They physically separate the individual lane groups in the multi-lane transceiver into separate connectors:

  • 1×400G → 4×100G: Splits a QSFP-DD (8 lanes) into 4× QSFP28 (2 lanes each). Common for connecting 400G spine switches to 100G leaf switches or servers.
  • 1×400G → 2×200G: Splits a QSFP-DD into 2× QSFP56 (4 lanes each). Used for HDR InfiniBand connectivity from NDR switches.
  • 1×200G → 2×100G: Splits a QSFP56 (HDR) into 2× QSFP28 (HDR100). Common in mixed-generation IB fabrics.
  • 1×100G → 4×25G: Splits a QSFP28 into 4× SFP28. Classic breakout for connecting to 25GbE server NICs.

The switch port must support breakout mode in its configuration. Example on Cumulus Linux: nv set interface swp1 breakout 4x100G.

Cable Color Coding Conventions

Jacket Color Convention
Orange Multi-mode fiber (OM1, OM2)
Aqua OM3 / OM4 multi-mode fiber (laser-optimized 50µm). Most common in modern datacenters.
Lime Green OM5 multi-mode fiber (wideband, supports SWDM)
Yellow Single-mode fiber (OS2). Long-reach.
Black DAC (direct attach copper) or AOC (active optical cable). Also generic outdoor fiber.
Violet Sometimes used for OM4+ or proprietary coding. Check vendor documentation.
Note: These are industry conventions, not standards. Vendors may deviate. Always verify fiber type by checking the cable label or using an optical power meter / fiber identifier. In structured cabling plants, document fiber types in your DCIM (Data Center Infrastructure Management) system.

5. GPUDirect Technologies

The fundamental problem: In traditional data transfer, data from the network must be copied into host (CPU) memory, then copied again to GPU memory. For AI training workloads that transfer terabytes of gradient data per minute across thousands of GPUs, this double-copy through CPU memory creates a bottleneck that wastes PCIe bandwidth and adds latency. GPUDirect eliminates these copies.

GPUDirect RDMA: Data Flow Comparison

GPUDirect RDMA

GPUDirect RDMA enables the network adapter (ConnectX HCA) to directly read from and write to GPU memory (HBM) via PCIe peer-to-peer transactions, completely bypassing CPU host memory. This eliminates two memory copies per transfer and removes the CPU from the critical data path.

How it works:
  1. GPU memory is registered as an RDMA Memory Region, and the NIC is given the physical addresses (BAR mapping) of GPU HBM.
  2. When a remote RDMA Write targets a GPU buffer, the NIC DMAs the incoming data directly to GPU HBM via PCIe P2P.
  3. The CPU is not involved in the data transfer at all—it only sets up the operation (posts the WR, manages QP state).
Requirements:
  • NVIDIA GPU driver with nvidia-peermem kernel module loaded (replaces the older nv_peer_mem module)
  • PCIe topology: NIC and GPU must be on the same PCIe root complex (or use PCIe ACS override). In DGX H100, each ConnectX-7 is affinity-mapped to a specific GPU via the PCIe switch topology.
  • NCCL integration: NCCL automatically uses GPUDirect RDMA when available. Verify with NCCL_DEBUG=INFO—look for NET/IB : Using GPUDirect RDMA in logs.
Performance impact:

Without GPUDirect RDMA, inter-node GPU communication is limited by the CPU memory bandwidth (~200 GB/s on a Xeon) and requires two PCIe traversals (NIC→CPU, CPU→GPU). With GPUDirect RDMA, the path is NIC→GPU directly, achieving full PCIe Gen5 x16 line rate (~63 GB/s per direction, per NIC-GPU pair). On a DGX H100 with 8 NICs, this provides up to ~400 GB/s aggregate network injection bandwidth.

GPUDirect Storage (GDS)

GPUDirect Storage extends the direct-path concept to storage I/O. Instead of reading data from NVMe into CPU memory and then copying to GPU, GDS enables a direct DMA path from the NVMe controller (or NVMe-over-Fabrics target) to GPU HBM.

  • API: cuFile API (part of CUDA toolkit). Applications call cuFileRead()/cuFileWrite() instead of standard POSIX I/O.
  • Supported filesystems: ext4 (with O_DIRECT), XFS, Lustre, GPFS/Spectrum Scale, WekaFS, VAST Data
  • NVMe-oF: GDS works over NVMe over Fabrics (RDMA or TCP), enabling remote storage arrays to DMA directly to GPU memory.
  • Use case: Training data loading (checkpointing, dataset reading), especially for large models where loading terabytes of checkpoint data through CPU memory becomes a bottleneck.

GDS reduces checkpoint load time by 2–5x depending on storage backend and data sizes. It also frees CPU memory bandwidth for other tasks (preprocessing, CPU-side computation).

GPUDirect Peer-to-Peer (P2P)

GPUDirect P2P enables direct memory access between GPUs within the same node, without staging through CPU memory. The interconnect can be:

  • NVLink: In DGX H100, all 8 GPUs are connected via NVLink 4.0 (4th generation NVSwitch), providing 900 GB/s bidirectional bandwidth per GPU. This is the primary intra-node communication path and is roughly 7x faster than PCIe Gen5.
  • PCIe: If NVLink is not available (or for GPUs not directly connected via NVLink), P2P falls back to PCIe peer-to-peer. This is slower but still avoids the CPU memory copy.

NCCL automatically uses NVLink for intra-node collectives and GPUDirect RDMA for inter-node. The combination provides the hierarchical communication pattern needed for efficient data-parallel and tensor-parallel training.

Why this matters for SREs: When debugging NCCL performance issues, the first things to check are: (1) Is nvidia-peermem loaded? (lsmod | grep nvidia_peermem). (2) Are NICs affinity-mapped to the correct GPUs? (check nvidia-smi topo -m). (3) Is GPUDirect actually being used? (set NCCL_DEBUG=INFO and look for GDR/P2P messages). A single misconfigured NIC-GPU affinity can reduce training throughput by 20–40% due to cross-NUMA PCIe traffic.

Knowledge Check

Test your understanding of physical networking concepts for GPU Superpod infrastructure.

Question 1: InfiniBand Lossless Behavior

InfiniBand achieves lossless behavior by design. What is the fundamental mechanism that prevents packet drops on a healthy IB fabric, and how does this differ from how RoCE achieves lossless behavior on Ethernet?

Correct answer: B. InfiniBand's credit-based flow control is a link-layer mechanism where the receiver explicitly grants credits (buffer space) to the sender. A sender will never transmit without credits, so buffer overflow is physically impossible on a healthy link. This requires zero configuration—it is inherent to the protocol. RoCE, running over Ethernet, must retrofit lossless behavior using PFC (per-priority pause frames), ECN (congestion marking), and DCQCN (end-to-end rate control). All three must be configured correctly and consistently across every switch in the path. This is one of the primary operational advantages of InfiniBand over RoCE for RDMA workloads.

Question 2: Transceiver Form Factors and Breakout

You have a Quantum-2 (QM9700) InfiniBand switch with 64 ports of QSFP-DD (400Gb/s NDR). You need to connect to a legacy system that has ConnectX-6 adapters with QSFP56 ports (HDR, 200Gb/s). What is the correct approach?

Correct answer: B. NDR (400Gb/s) ports support 2x200G breakout mode, splitting the 8 lanes (100Gb/s each) into two groups of 4 lanes (200Gb/s each, matching HDR speed). A breakout cable with a QSFP-DD connector on the switch end and two QSFP56 connectors on the host end is the standard solution. This is a common scenario when upgrading spine switches to NDR while leaf or host connections remain at HDR. Option D is partially true—QSFP-DD cages do accept QSFP56 modules—but the port must still be reconfigured, and you get only one 200G connection instead of potentially two from the breakout approach.

Question 3: GPUDirect RDMA Troubleshooting

A user reports that their multi-node NCCL training job is running 35% slower than expected. You run NCCL_DEBUG=INFO and notice the logs say NET/IB : Using [Send/Recv] (no GPUDirect) instead of NET/IB : Using GPUDirect RDMA. What are the most likely causes, and what would you check first?

Correct answer: B. The most common cause of GPUDirect RDMA not being used is the nvidia-peermem module not being loaded. This module (which replaced the older out-of-tree nv_peer_mem module starting with CUDA 11.4 / driver 470+) registers a callback with the RDMA core subsystem so that ibv_reg_mr() can handle GPU virtual addresses. Without it, the NIC cannot access GPU memory and NCCL falls back to staging through host memory. The second most common cause is PCIe topology issues—if ACS (Access Control Services) is enabled in the BIOS or on PCIe bridges between the NIC and GPU, P2P DMA transactions are blocked. Check with lspci -vvv | grep ACSCtl. Option C is incorrect: GPUDirect RDMA works with both InfiniBand and RoCEv2. Option A is incorrect: NCCL does not fall back from GPUDirect due to congestion—it is a capability check at initialization time.

Network Architecture

The network fabric is the nervous system of a GPU Superpod. Every collective operation, every gradient exchange, every activation transfer flows through this infrastructure. A poorly designed network turns a $200M compute investment into idle silicon. This section covers the topology, protocols, and redundancy mechanisms that make large-scale distributed GPU training possible.

1. Spine-Leaf (Clos) Topology

Why Spine-Leaf?

Traditional three-tier architectures (access → aggregation → core) create bottlenecks, require Spanning Tree Protocol (STP) to prevent loops, and produce unpredictable latency. The spine-leaf (Clos) topology eliminates these problems:

  • Predictable latency: Every server-to-server path traverses exactly one leaf → spine → leaf hop (or leaf → spine → super-spine → spine → leaf in 3-tier). Maximum hop count is fixed regardless of fabric size.
  • No STP: All links are active simultaneously. ECMP (Equal-Cost Multi-Path) provides loop-free multi-pathing at L3. No blocked ports, no wasted bandwidth.
  • Horizontal scalability: Adding capacity means adding more leaf switches (for ports) or more spine switches (for bandwidth). The architecture scales linearly.
  • Full bisection bandwidth: In a non-blocking configuration, the aggregate bandwidth between any two halves of the fabric equals the total server-facing bandwidth. Every server can communicate at line rate simultaneously.

Fat-Tree Topology for HPC

Charles Leiserson's fat-tree (1985) is the theoretical basis for modern Clos fabrics. In a fat-tree, links get "fatter" (higher bandwidth) as you move up the tree. In practice, this is achieved by increasing the number of equal-bandwidth links at each tier rather than using physically wider links.

For GPU clusters, a non-blocking fat-tree is essential. During AllReduce operations, every GPU sends data to every other GPU simultaneously. Any oversubscription at the spine layer creates a bottleneck that slows the entire collective, wasting GPU cycles across the cluster.

Leaf Switches (Top-of-Rack / ToR)

  • Positioned at the top of each server rack
  • Downlink ports connect to servers, GPUs (via ConnectX-7 NICs), and storage nodes
  • Uplink ports connect to every spine switch in the fabric
  • Typical: NVIDIA Spectrum-4 SN5600 with 64x 400GbE ports, or Quantum-2 QM9700 with 64x NDR (400Gb/s) InfiniBand ports
  • Each leaf has a unique /31 or unnumbered link to every spine
  • Performs L2 switching locally (within rack) and L3 routing to the fabric

Spine Switches

  • Every spine connects to every leaf switch in the pod
  • No servers connect directly to spine switches
  • Purpose: provide full mesh connectivity between all leaves
  • For a non-blocking fabric: total spine uplink bandwidth from each leaf must equal total server-facing bandwidth on that leaf
  • Example: 32 server ports at 400G = 12.8 Tb/s down → need 32 uplinks at 400G = 12.8 Tb/s to spines

Super-Spine (3-Tier Clos)

  • Required when the fabric exceeds what a single spine tier can interconnect
  • A pod = one complete leaf+spine fabric. Super-spines connect pods together.
  • NVIDIA DGX SuperPOD with 256+ DGX nodes typically requires super-spine for the InfiniBand compute fabric
  • Each spine connects to every super-spine; each super-spine connects to every spine across all pods
  • Maximum 3-tier fat-tree with 64-port switches: 64 pods × 32 leaves/pod × 32 servers/leaf = 65,536 endpoints

Oversubscription Ratios

Ratio Meaning Use Case Cost Impact
1:1 (Non-blocking) Spine bandwidth = leaf server bandwidth. Every port can run at line rate simultaneously. GPU training clusters, HPC, InfiniBand fabrics. Mandatory for AllReduce-heavy workloads. Highest cost. Requires maximum spine switch count.
2:1 Half the spine bandwidth vs server bandwidth. Statistical multiplexing assumed. Storage networks, inference serving, enterprise workloads where not all hosts communicate simultaneously. ~50% fewer spine switches and uplinks.
3:1 One-third spine bandwidth vs server bandwidth. General enterprise, web serving, microservices. Acceptable when traffic is bursty and not all-to-all. Significant savings on spine infrastructure.

Rail-Optimized Topology

In NVIDIA's rail-optimized design for DGX H100/B200 clusters, each of the 8 GPU NICs in a server connects to a different leaf switch (a different "rail"). GPU 0 across all servers connects to Rail 0 leaf switches, GPU 1 to Rail 1 leaf switches, and so on.

This means intra-rail traffic (GPU 0 on server A talking to GPU 0 on server B) stays within a single leaf switch or traverses one leaf-spine-leaf path, while cross-rail traffic uses NVSwitch/NVLink within the node. This aligns the physical topology with NCCL's ring/tree algorithms, reduces the number of spine switches needed, and confines most collective traffic to a single rail, dramatically simplifying traffic engineering.

For a 32-node DGX H100 pod with 8 rails: 8 leaf switches (one per rail), each with 32 downlinks (one per server's GPU N) and uplinks to shared spine switches. A 256-node SuperPOD uses 8 rails × 8 leaf switches per rail = 64 leaf switches.

Spine-Leaf Topology: Compute Fabric (InfiniBand) & Management/Storage Fabric (Ethernet)

2. East-West Traffic (Data Plane)

What Is East-West Traffic?

East-west traffic flows laterally between servers, GPUs, and storage nodes within the datacenter. In a GPU training cluster, east-west traffic constitutes 90-99% of all network traffic. This is fundamentally different from traditional enterprise datacenters where north-south (client-server) traffic dominates.

During distributed training of a large language model across 2,048 GPUs, every GPU must exchange gradient data with every other GPU during each training step. A single AllReduce operation on a 175B-parameter model moves approximately 350 GB of gradient data across the fabric in under 1 second. This is why non-blocking fabric design is non-negotiable.

NCCL: NVIDIA Collective Communications Library

NCCL is the communication backbone for distributed GPU training. It abstracts the underlying network transport (InfiniBand Verbs, RoCE, TCP sockets) and implements optimized collective operations:

Collective Operation Training Use Case Traffic Pattern
AllReduce Reduce data across all ranks, result available on all ranks Data parallelism: aggregate gradients from all workers All-to-all; each GPU sends and receives ~2 × (N-1)/N × data_size
AllGather Gather data from all ranks to all ranks FSDP (Fully Sharded Data Parallel): reconstruct full model weights All-to-all; total data moved = N × shard_size
ReduceScatter Reduce and scatter result chunks across ranks FSDP: reduce gradients and distribute shards All-to-all; each GPU ends with 1/N of reduced result
Broadcast Send data from one rank to all others Weight initialization, hyperparameter sync One-to-all; tree-shaped distribution
Send/Recv (P2P) Point-to-point data transfer between two specific ranks Model/pipeline parallelism: activation exchange between stages Point-to-point; creates predictable flows

Ring Algorithm

GPUs are arranged in a logical ring. Data is chunked and pipelined around the ring in two phases: scatter-reduce (each GPU reduces a chunk and passes it on) and allgather (the fully reduced chunks circulate). For N GPUs and data size D:

  • Each GPU sends: 2 × (N-1)/N × D total bytes
  • Latency: 2 × (N-1) × α (where α is per-hop latency)
  • Bandwidth-optimal: approaches theoretical limit for large D
  • Latency-suboptimal: O(N) latency terms; slow at large GPU counts with small messages
  • NCCL creates multiple rings across NVLink and network to maximize bandwidth

Tree Algorithm

GPUs are arranged in a binary tree (or double binary tree for full-duplex utilization). Data flows up the tree (reduce) and down the tree (broadcast).

  • Latency: 2 × log2(N) × α — dramatically better than ring for large N
  • Bandwidth: each GPU sends 2 × D total bytes (less efficient than ring for large D)
  • NCCL auto-selects ring for large messages, tree for small messages (crossover ~256KB-4MB depending on GPU count)

Mapping to InfiniBand / RoCE

NCCL uses RDMA verbs directly over InfiniBand or RoCE v2. Key transport mechanisms:

  • GPUDirect RDMA: NIC reads/writes GPU memory directly via PCIe BAR, bypassing CPU and system memory. Reduces latency by ~5μs per transfer.
  • GPUDirect Async: GPU kernel triggers RDMA operations without CPU involvement, enabling full overlap of compute and communication.
  • SHARP (Scalable Hierarchical Aggregation and Reduction Protocol): Offloads reduction operations to InfiniBand switch ASICs. The network performs the math during transit, halving the data traversal. Available on Quantum-2 switches.

Traffic Patterns During Distributed Training

Data Parallelism (AllReduce-dominant)

Each GPU holds a full copy of the model and trains on different data. After the backward pass, gradients are synchronized via AllReduce. For a model with P parameters in FP16, each AllReduce moves approximately 2 × P bytes. A 70B-parameter model: 2 × 70×109 = 140 GB per AllReduce. With BF16 gradients, this must complete within the backward pass time (~500ms for typical LLM training) to avoid becoming the bottleneck.

Model (Tensor) Parallelism (P2P-dominant)

The model is split across GPUs within a node (typically 8-way tensor parallelism over NVLink). Each layer requires two AllReduce operations on activations (forward + backward). Traffic volume per layer: 2 × batch_size × sequence_length × hidden_dim × 2 bytes. This traffic stays within the NVSwitch domain (900 GB/s per GPU on H100) and does not touch the InfiniBand fabric.

Pipeline Parallelism (Sequential P2P)

The model is split into stages across nodes. Micro-batches flow between stages via point-to-point Send/Recv. Traffic is sequential and relatively low-bandwidth compared to AllReduce, but latency-sensitive. Typical activation size per micro-batch: batch_size × seq_len × hidden_dim × 2 bytes (~50-200 MB).

Fully Sharded Data Parallel (FSDP / ZeRO-3)

Model parameters, gradients, and optimizer states are sharded across GPUs. Before each forward/backward layer, an AllGather reconstructs parameters; after backward, a ReduceScatter distributes gradient shards. This trades 3x more communication for N-fold memory savings. Communication volume per layer: 2 × layer_params × 2 bytes (AllGather + ReduceScatter).

Bandwidth Is Non-Negotiable

In a 2,048-GPU cluster running AllReduce over a ring, each GPU NIC must sustain near line-rate throughput (~385 Gb/s usable out of 400 Gb/s NDR) for the entire duration of the gradient synchronization phase. Even a 10% bandwidth reduction on a single link cascades into a proportional training slowdown across all 2,048 GPUs, because the collective cannot complete until the slowest participant finishes.

ECMP and Adaptive Routing

ECMP (Equal-Cost Multi-Path) is the standard mechanism for distributing traffic across multiple spine links. When a leaf switch has 32 equal-cost paths to another leaf (through 32 spine switches), ECMP selects a path using a hash of the packet header fields (5-tuple: src IP, dst IP, src port, dst port, protocol).

The Elephant Flow Problem

NCCL generates a small number of very large, long-lived flows ("elephant flows"). With 5-tuple hashing, multiple elephant flows can hash to the same spine link, creating congestion on that path while other paths are idle. In a 32-spine fabric, the probability of at least one collision with just 8 flows is approximately 75% (birthday problem).

Solutions
  • Adaptive Routing (NVIDIA Quantum-2): InfiniBand switches dynamically reroute packets based on real-time port congestion. Each packet can take a different path, achieving near-perfect load balancing. This is the preferred solution for InfiniBand fabrics.
  • Packet Spraying: Each packet is independently routed across all paths. Achieves perfect load balance but requires reordering at the receiver. Supported by some Ethernet switches (e.g., Memory-based Memory Fabrics, Barefoot/Intel Tofino).
  • Flowlet Switching: Splits flows into "flowlets" (bursts separated by idle gaps) and assigns each flowlet to a different path. Balances load without reordering within a burst. Used by SONiC's dynamic hashing.
  • Larger Hash Entropy: Using NCCL's multi-port/multi-QP feature to create more parallel connections, increasing the number of flows and reducing collision probability.

3. North-South Traffic (Control Plane + Ingress/Egress)

What Is North-South Traffic?

North-south traffic flows vertically between the datacenter and external networks (the internet, other datacenters, cloud providers). In a GPU training cluster, this is typically less than 5% of total traffic but is critical for operations:

  • Model and dataset ingestion: Downloading training data from S3/GCS/Azure Blob, pulling model checkpoints (can be 100s of GB but infrequent)
  • Checkpoint upload: Saving model checkpoints to remote storage during training
  • API serving: If the cluster also serves inference traffic, client requests enter via north-south paths
  • Management: SSH access, monitoring (Prometheus/Grafana), log shipping (Elasticsearch/Splunk), LDAP/SSO authentication
  • Software updates: OS patches, driver updates, container image pulls

North-South vs East-West Traffic Flows

North-South Infrastructure Components

  • Border / Edge Routers: Terminate BGP sessions with ISPs and transit providers. Typically deployed in redundant pairs (active-active with ECMP or active-standby). Handle route filtering, AS-path prepending, and community tagging. Common hardware: Arista 7280R3, Cisco 8000 series, Juniper MX304.
  • Firewalls: Stateful packet inspection, ACLs, and DDoS mitigation at the datacenter edge. For GPU clusters, the primary concern is protecting management interfaces (SSH, IPMI/BMC) from external access. Training traffic (InfiniBand) never touches the firewall because it stays on the compute fabric. Typical throughput: 100Gbps+ (Palo Alto PA-7000, Fortinet 4400F).
  • Load Balancers: Required if the cluster serves inference APIs.
    • L4 (Transport): Distributes TCP/UDP connections based on 5-tuple hash. Low overhead, does not inspect payload. Good for distributing inference requests across GPU servers. Examples: NVIDIA BlueField DPU, DPDK-based (Katran/Facebook), IPVS/LVS.
    • L7 (Application): Inspects HTTP headers, URLs, cookies. Can route based on model name, API version, or request content. Higher overhead. Examples: Envoy, HAProxy, NGINX, AWS ALB equivalent.

4. BGP (Border Gateway Protocol)

BGP in the Datacenter

BGP (RFC 4271) was originally designed for inter-domain routing on the internet, but has become the dominant routing protocol for datacenter fabrics, replacing OSPF and IS-IS in modern deployments. The key insight: treat each switch in the spine-leaf fabric as its own "autonomous system" and run eBGP between them (RFC 7938: "Use of BGP for Routing in Large-Scale Data Centers").

eBGP for External Peering

Border routers establish eBGP sessions with upstream ISPs, transit providers, and peering partners. Each peer advertises its routes; the border router selects the best path based on BGP's path selection algorithm:

  1. Highest local preference (prefer one ISP over another)
  2. Shortest AS path (fewest autonomous systems traversed)
  3. Lowest origin type (IGP < EGP < incomplete)
  4. Lowest MED (Multi-Exit Discriminator, hint from peer)
  5. eBGP over iBGP (prefer external routes)
  6. Lowest IGP metric to next-hop
  7. Oldest route (stability preference)
  8. Lowest router ID (tiebreaker)

Typical configuration: the datacenter's own public ASN (e.g., AS 396356 for NVIDIA), advertising the datacenter's IP prefixes to upstream peers with appropriate communities for traffic engineering.

iBGP Within the Datacenter

Traditional iBGP (same ASN on all routers) requires either a full mesh of iBGP sessions (N×(N-1)/2 sessions) or route reflectors to propagate routes. This creates scaling problems:

  • Full mesh with 128 switches = 8,128 iBGP sessions
  • Route reflectors add complexity and single points of failure
  • iBGP does not modify the AS path, making loop detection harder
  • Next-hop-self configuration required on every device

For these reasons, modern datacenter designs prefer eBGP with private ASNs (see BGP as DC IGP tab) over traditional iBGP.

BGP as an IGP (RFC 7938)

The most common approach in modern datacenter fabrics: assign each switch its own private ASN (from the 4-byte range 4200000000-4294967294) and run eBGP on every link.

Design Pattern
  • Each leaf switch: unique ASN (e.g., AS 4200000001, 4200000002, ...)
  • Each spine switch: unique ASN (e.g., AS 4200001001, 4200001002, ...)
  • eBGP session on every leaf-spine link (point-to-point /31 or unnumbered)
  • No route reflectors needed (eBGP propagates routes naturally)
  • Loop prevention via AS-path (inherent in eBGP)
  • ECMP across all spine paths (multipath relax with bestpath as-path multipath-relax)
Advantages Over OSPF/IS-IS
  • No complex area design or LSA flooding
  • Policy control at every hop (route-maps, communities)
  • Slower convergence is mitigated by BFD (sub-second detection)
  • Battle-tested at massive scale (every major cloud provider uses this)
  • Simpler troubleshooting: show bgp summary immediately shows peer state
Unnumbered Interfaces (RFC 5765)

Instead of assigning /31 subnets to every point-to-point link (which consumes IP addresses and creates management overhead), BGP unnumbered uses IPv6 link-local addresses for peering and advertises IPv4 routes with the link-local next-hop. This is supported by FRRouting (FRR) on Cumulus/SONiC and NVIDIA Spectrum switches natively.

EVPN-VXLAN with BGP

When Layer 2 connectivity across racks is needed (e.g., for VM migration, legacy applications, or container networking), EVPN (Ethernet VPN) provides an overlay solution using VXLAN encapsulation with BGP as the control plane:

  • VXLAN: Encapsulates L2 frames in UDP (port 4789) for transport over the L3 spine-leaf fabric. 24-bit VNI (VXLAN Network Identifier) supports ~16 million virtual networks.
  • BGP EVPN (Address Family L2VPN EVPN): Distributes MAC addresses, IP addresses, and VNI-to-VTEP mappings via BGP rather than flood-and-learn. Eliminates BUM (Broadcast, Unknown unicast, Multicast) flooding.
  • Type-2 routes: MAC/IP advertisement (host reachability)
  • Type-5 routes: IP prefix routes (inter-VXLAN routing)
  • Distributed Anycast Gateway: Every leaf switch acts as the default gateway for its local VNIs using the same virtual MAC and IP. No VRRP/HSRP needed.

In GPU clusters, EVPN-VXLAN is primarily used on the Ethernet management/storage fabric, not on the InfiniBand compute fabric. It provides isolated network segments for BMC/IPMI management, storage traffic, and container networking (e.g., Kubernetes pod networks).

BFD, Graceful Restart, and Route Dampening

  • BFD (Bidirectional Forwarding Detection): Lightweight protocol running independently of BGP to detect link/node failures in ~50ms (configurable: 3 × transmit interval, typically 50ms × 3 = 150ms). When BFD detects a failure, it notifies BGP to immediately withdraw routes rather than waiting for the BGP hold timer (default 180 seconds). Essential for sub-second failover in spine-leaf fabrics.
  • Graceful Restart (GR): Allows a router to maintain forwarding during BGP process restart (e.g., software upgrade). The restarting router signals GR capability; peers maintain stale routes for a configurable period (restart-time, typically 120s). Critical for maintenance without traffic disruption. However, GR conflicts with BFD (BFD detects the restart as a failure), so Long-Lived Graceful Restart (LLGR) is sometimes used instead.
  • Route Dampening: Suppresses routes that flap repeatedly (go up/down/up/down). Assigns a penalty on each flap; when the penalty exceeds a threshold, the route is suppressed until the penalty decays below a reuse threshold. Prevents instability from propagating across the fabric. Typically used on external BGP sessions, not internal DC fabric links.
  • BGP Communities: 32-bit tags attached to routes for policy signaling. Standard communities (e.g., NO_EXPORT, NO_ADVERTISE), extended communities (for EVPN), and large communities (RFC 8092, 12-byte) for complex traffic engineering. Examples: tag routes from ISP-A for backup-only use, tag internal routes by datacenter region.

BGP Peering Architecture: External ISPs + Internal eBGP Fabric

5. Blended ISP / Transit

What Is Blended Transit?

Blended transit means connecting the datacenter to multiple upstream ISPs and transit providers simultaneously to achieve redundancy, performance, and cost optimization. Rather than relying on a single provider, traffic is distributed across multiple paths based on BGP routing policy.

Transit vs Peering vs IXP

Type Definition Cost Model Typical Use
Transit Paying an upstream provider to carry your traffic to the entire internet. They advertise your prefixes to their peers and provide you with a full routing table (~950K IPv4 routes as of 2025). Per-Mbps commit + 95th percentile overage. $0.30-$2.00/Mbps/month depending on volume and location. Primary internet access. Every datacenter needs at least one transit provider (usually two for redundancy).
Peering (PNI) Direct private interconnection with another network (e.g., a cloud provider or CDN). Settlement-free (no payment) or paid. Each party only advertises its own routes. Often free (settlement-free). Cross-connect fee: $200-500/month. Paid peering: negotiated rate. High-volume traffic exchange with specific networks. Reduces transit costs and latency. E.g., peering with AWS for model checkpoint uploads.
IXP (Internet Exchange) A shared switching fabric (usually Ethernet) where multiple networks peer with each other. Route servers simplify multilateral peering. Port fee: $500-$5,000/month for 10G-100G. No per-Mbps charge. Each peer relationship is settlement-free. Peer with many networks simultaneously. Major IXPs: DE-CIX, AMS-IX, LINX, Equinix IX. Reduces transit usage significantly.

Multi-Homed Active-Active Setup

A properly multi-homed datacenter has:

  • At least 2 transit providers with physically diverse paths (different fiber routes, different buildings for upstream POPs)
  • Own ASN and Provider-Independent (PI) IP space (at minimum a /24 IPv4 and /48 IPv6 from an RIR allocation)
  • Active-active traffic engineering: Both ISPs carry traffic simultaneously. BGP local-preference determines outbound ISP selection; AS-path prepending and MED influence inbound traffic from the internet.
  • Automatic failover: If one ISP fails, BFD detects the link down in <200ms, BGP withdraws routes via that ISP, and all traffic shifts to the remaining provider within 1-5 seconds.
Anycast for Global Services

If inference endpoints are served from multiple datacenter locations, anycast allows the same IP address to be advertised via BGP from multiple sites. Users are routed to the nearest datacenter based on BGP path selection, providing automatic geographic load balancing and failover.

DDoS Scrubbing

GPU clusters serving public inference APIs are targets for volumetric DDoS attacks. Solutions include:

  • Upstream scrubbing: Providers like Cloudflare, Akamai Prolexic, or AWS Shield proxy traffic and filter attacks before they reach the datacenter. Traffic is routed through the scrubbing center via BGP (either always-on or on-demand via BGP community signaling).
  • On-premise mitigation: Dedicated DDoS appliances (Arbor/Netscout, A10 Thunder TPS) at the datacenter edge for lower-latency filtering.
  • Flowspec: BGP Flowspec (RFC 5575) allows border routers to install packet-level filtering rules distributed via BGP, enabling upstream providers to filter attack traffic at their edge.

Typical ISP Uplink Sizing for GPU Datacenters

Cluster Size ISP Bandwidth Billing Model Notes
64-256 GPUs (single pod) 2 × 10 GbE per ISP (2 ISPs) 95th percentile, 1-5 Gbps commit Sufficient for dataset ingestion + management. Training traffic stays on IB fabric.
512-2048 GPUs (SuperPOD) 2 × 100 GbE per ISP (2 ISPs) 95th percentile, 10-40 Gbps commit Higher bandwidth needed for checkpoint upload to cloud storage and multi-site training sync.
4096+ GPUs (multi-site) 4 × 100 GbE per ISP + IXP peering Blended commit 50-100+ Gbps May include dedicated wavelength (DWDM) links between sites for inter-cluster gradient sync.

95th Percentile Billing

Most transit providers bill based on the 95th percentile of traffic measured in 5-minute samples over a month. The top 5% of samples are discarded (allows for short bursts). The remaining highest sample determines the bill. For a GPU cluster, large dataset downloads create temporary spikes, but steady-state north-south traffic is typically low. The commit rate is the minimum guaranteed bandwidth and minimum monthly charge. Overages above the commit are billed per-Mbps at a negotiated rate.

6. Redundancy & High Availability

Link Redundancy: MLAG / VPC

MLAG (Multi-Chassis Link Aggregation Group), also called VPC (Virtual Port Channel) on Cisco or MC-LAG, allows two physical switches to appear as a single logical switch to downstream devices. This is used on the Ethernet fabric (not InfiniBand, which uses its own redundancy mechanisms).

MLAG Components
  • Peer Link: A high-bandwidth link (typically 2-4 × 100GbE LAG) between the two MLAG peer switches. Carries orphan traffic (traffic destined for a host connected to only one peer) and control-plane synchronization. This is not the primary data path — in a well-designed MLAG, the peer link carries minimal data traffic.
  • Keepalive Link: A low-bandwidth out-of-band link (1GbE management port) used solely for heartbeat detection between peers. If the keepalive is lost but the peer link is up, the secondary switch disables its MLAG ports to prevent split-brain.
  • Orphan Ports: Server ports connected to only one of the two MLAG peers (not dual-homed). Traffic to/from orphan ports must traverse the peer link to reach the other peer's uplinks. Minimize orphan ports in production designs.
  • Dual-Homed Servers: Each server's bonded NIC (LACP bond) connects one link to each MLAG peer. From the server's perspective, it has a single LAG to a single switch. If one peer fails, the server's traffic flows entirely through the surviving peer with no reconfiguration.

LACP (802.3ad / 802.1AX)

Link Aggregation Control Protocol dynamically negotiates and manages link aggregation groups (LAGs / bonds). Key aspects:

  • LACP negotiation: Both endpoints exchange LACPDUs (every 1 second in fast mode, 30 seconds in slow mode) to agree on LAG membership. If a link fails to exchange LACPDUs, it is removed from the LAG.
  • Active vs Passive: Active mode sends LACPDUs immediately; Passive mode only responds. Best practice: one side Active, one Passive (or both Active).
  • Hash-based distribution: Outbound traffic is distributed across member links using a hash (typically L3+L4: src/dst IP + src/dst port). This means a single flow uses a single link; aggregate bandwidth is achieved across multiple flows.
  • Min-links: Configure the minimum number of active links required for the LAG to remain up. If active links drop below this threshold, the entire LAG is brought down, triggering BFD/BGP failover to alternate paths.

Active-Active vs Active-Standby Comparison

Aspect Active-Active Active-Standby
Bandwidth utilization Both links carry traffic simultaneously — full aggregate bandwidth available Standby link carries no traffic — 50% bandwidth wasted
Failover time Sub-second (traffic already flowing on surviving link, just redistributed) 1-5 seconds (standby must activate, MAC tables update)
Complexity Higher: requires ECMP or MLAG, consistent hashing, state sync between peers Lower: clear primary/secondary roles, simpler troubleshooting
Use in GPU DC Preferred for all fabric links. Spine-leaf inherently active-active via ECMP. Sometimes used for management/OOB networks where simplicity is prioritized.

Additional HA Mechanisms

  • BFD (Bidirectional Forwarding Detection): As covered in the BGP section, BFD provides sub-second failure detection (50-300ms). It runs independently of routing protocols and notifies BGP/OSPF immediately upon detecting a link failure. Critical for maintaining training job continuity — a 3-minute BGP hold-timer expiry would cause NCCL timeouts and kill the training job.
  • VRRP/HSRP (Gateway Redundancy): Virtual Router Redundancy Protocol (VRRP, RFC 5798) and Hot Standby Router Protocol (HSRP, Cisco proprietary) provide a virtual IP that floats between two routers. One is active (responds to ARP), one is standby. Less common in modern spine-leaf designs because anycast gateway (via EVPN) is preferred — every leaf advertises the same gateway IP with the same virtual MAC, so there is no single active router.
  • Redundant Control Planes: High-end switches (Cisco Nexus 9000, Arista 7500R) have dual supervisor modules. If the active supervisor fails, the standby takes over with stateful switchover (SSO) — forwarding continues uninterrupted during the transition. On InfiniBand, the Subnet Manager (OpenSM or NVIDIA UFM) runs on redundant management nodes in active-standby configuration.
  • Anycast Gateway (Distributed Gateway): In an EVPN-VXLAN fabric, every leaf switch is configured with the same virtual gateway IP and MAC for each VLAN/VNI. Hosts always reach their gateway locally on their own leaf switch — no hairpinning to a centralized gateway. If a leaf fails, hosts on surviving leaves are unaffected. This replaces VRRP/HSRP entirely.

7. Routing Protocols Inside the DC

OSPF in the Datacenter

OSPF (Open Shortest Path First, RFC 2328 for OSPFv2, RFC 5340 for OSPFv3) is a link-state protocol. Each router builds a complete topology map (Link-State Database / LSDB) and runs Dijkstra's SPF algorithm to compute shortest paths.

OSPF Area Design for DC
  • Area 0 (Backbone): Spine switches and inter-area links
  • Non-backbone areas: Each pod or rack group in its own area to limit LSA flooding
  • Stub / NSSA areas: Reduce routing table size on leaf switches by summarizing external routes
LSA Types
  • Type 1 (Router LSA): Every router advertises its links and costs within an area
  • Type 2 (Network LSA): DR (Designated Router) on multi-access segments advertises attached routers. Unnecessary on point-to-point links (use ip ospf network point-to-point to suppress DR election).
  • Type 3 (Summary LSA): ABR (Area Border Router) advertises inter-area routes. Spine switches acting as ABRs summarize leaf subnets.
  • Type 5 (External LSA): ASBR (Autonomous System Border Router) redistributes external routes into OSPF.
OSPF Limitations in Large DC Fabrics
  • SPF recalculation on every topology change floods the entire area
  • No policy control at individual router level (OSPF is a shortest-path algorithm; you cannot prefer one path for specific prefixes without hacks)
  • DR election on broadcast segments adds complexity (mitigated by point-to-point links)
  • Full LSDB replication in large areas consumes memory and CPU
  • ECMP limited by SPF cost calculation (all equal-cost paths must have identical metric)

BGP as the DC Routing Protocol

As detailed in Section 4, BGP with private ASNs per switch (RFC 7938) has become the dominant routing protocol in large-scale datacenter fabrics. Key operational advantages:

Unnumbered Interfaces

BGP unnumbered (supported in FRRouting/Cumulus/SONiC) establishes BGP sessions over IPv6 link-local addresses while carrying IPv4 NLRI. Benefits:

  • No /31 subnets to allocate, manage, or document for inter-switch links
  • Each switch needs only a single loopback IPv4 address (used as router-id and next-hop)
  • Dramatically simplifies IP address management in fabrics with hundreds of switches
  • Configuration is identical on every leaf-spine link, enabling zero-touch provisioning
Route Summarization at Spine

Spine switches can summarize routes from their connected leaves before advertising to other spines or super-spines. For example, if Leaf 0-7 (a pod) uses 10.0.0.0/24 through 10.0.7.0/24, the spine advertises a single 10.0.0.0/21 aggregate. This reduces the routing table size on super-spines and border routers, improving convergence time and reducing memory consumption.

BGP Configuration Example (FRRouting / SONiC)
router bgp 4200000001   ! Leaf-0 ASN
 bgp router-id 10.255.0.1
 bgp bestpath as-path multipath-relax
 neighbor fabric peer-group
 neighbor fabric remote-as external
 neighbor fabric bfd
 neighbor swp1 interface peer-group fabric   ! Uplink to Spine-0
 neighbor swp2 interface peer-group fabric   ! Uplink to Spine-1
 neighbor swp3 interface peer-group fabric   ! Uplink to Spine-2
 neighbor swp4 interface peer-group fabric   ! Uplink to Spine-3
 !
 address-family ipv4 unicast
  network 10.0.0.0/24   ! Server subnet on this leaf
  maximum-paths 64       ! Enable ECMP across all spine paths
 exit-address-family

BGP vs OSPF for Datacenter Fabrics

Criterion OSPF BGP (eBGP w/ private ASN)
Convergence speed Fast SPF recalculation (~ms), but LSA flooding can cause churn in large areas Slower inherent convergence (best-path recalculation), but BFD provides equivalent failure detection speed
Scalability Requires careful area design. Single area limited to ~200-300 routers before LSDB becomes problematic. Scales to thousands of switches. No area design needed. Each switch is its own AS.
Policy control Minimal. Can only manipulate link cost. No per-prefix policy at individual routers. Rich policy: route-maps, prefix-lists, communities on every session. Can filter, prefer, or reject any prefix at any hop.
Multi-path ECMP across equal-cost SPF paths. Maximum paths typically 8-64. ECMP with multipath-relax across different ASNs. Maximum paths 64-256. More flexible.
Configuration complexity Moderate. Area assignment, network statements, DR tuning on broadcast segments. Low with unnumbered interfaces and peer-groups. Identical config on every leaf. Automation-friendly.
Troubleshooting Requires understanding LSDB, LSA types, SPF tree. show ip ospf database output is verbose. Straightforward. show bgp summary shows peer state at a glance. show bgp shows selected paths.
Industry adoption Legacy DC deployments, some enterprise DCs All hyperscalers (Meta, Google, Microsoft, AWS), NVIDIA DGX Cloud, most modern DC fabrics

Bottom Line

For GPU cluster datacenter fabrics, BGP with private ASNs is the clear winner. It provides the scalability needed for large fabrics, the policy control needed for traffic engineering, and the operational simplicity that comes from uniform configuration across all switches. OSPF remains viable for smaller deployments (<50 switches) where the simpler conceptual model may be preferred, but offers no advantage at scale.

Knowledge Check: Network Architecture

Question 1: In a rail-optimized topology for a DGX H100 cluster with 8 GPUs per node, how are the GPU NICs connected to the leaf switches?

Correct. Rail-optimized topology assigns each GPU index to a separate "rail" (set of leaf switches). GPU 0 on every server in the pod connects to Rail 0 leaf switches, GPU 1 to Rail 1, etc. This means intra-rail AllReduce traffic (GPU N on Server A talking to GPU N on Server B) stays within a single rail's leaf-spine sub-fabric. Cross-GPU communication within a node uses NVSwitch/NVLink (900 GB/s on H100), not the network fabric. This design dramatically reduces cross-rail traffic and simplifies ECMP load balancing.

Incorrect. In a rail-optimized design, each of the 8 GPUs in a DGX node connects its NIC to a different leaf switch rail. GPU 0 across all servers connects to Rail 0 leaves, GPU 1 to Rail 1 leaves, etc. This aligns the network topology with NCCL's collective algorithms, keeping most AllReduce traffic within a single rail and using intra-node NVLink for cross-GPU communication.

Question 2: Why does RFC 7938 recommend using eBGP with private ASNs (one per switch) instead of OSPF or iBGP for datacenter spine-leaf fabrics?

Correct. The key advantages of eBGP with per-switch ASNs are: (1) no OSPF area design needed since each switch is its own AS, (2) no iBGP full-mesh or route reflectors since eBGP naturally propagates routes, (3) AS-path provides loop prevention, and (4) route-maps and communities enable per-hop policy control. The "bestpath as-path multipath-relax" knob enables ECMP even when paths traverse different ASNs. BFD is used alongside BGP to achieve sub-second failure detection, compensating for BGP's inherently slower convergence.

Incorrect. eBGP actually converges slower than OSPF by default (answer A is wrong). OSPF can be used as a VXLAN underlay (answer C is wrong). BGP does maintain per-prefix state (answer D is misleading). The correct answer is B: the combination of no area design, no full-mesh requirement, AS-path loop prevention, and rich policy control makes eBGP with private ASNs the simplest and most scalable approach for large datacenter fabrics.

Question 3: What is the "elephant flow" problem with ECMP in GPU training clusters, and how does NVIDIA's InfiniBand Quantum-2 address it?

Correct. NCCL generates a small number of very large, persistent flows during collective operations like AllReduce. With standard ECMP 5-tuple hashing, the birthday problem makes hash collisions likely even with moderate flow counts: with 32 spine paths and 8 flows, there is approximately a 75% chance of at least one collision. A collision means two elephant flows share one spine link (creating congestion) while another spine link sits idle. NVIDIA Quantum-2 switches implement adaptive routing at the hardware level, inspecting real-time port utilization and dynamically selecting the least-congested path for each packet. This per-packet decision eliminates hash collision hotspots and achieves near-theoretical bisection bandwidth.

Incorrect. Elephant flows are not about MTU/fragmentation (A) or short bursts (B) or multicast (D). They are large, long-lived data flows from NCCL collectives that persist for the duration of gradient synchronization. The problem is that ECMP's static 5-tuple hash can map multiple such flows to the same spine link, creating a hotspot. Quantum-2's adaptive routing solves this by making per-packet routing decisions based on real-time congestion, not static hashing.

GPU Virtualization & Multi-Tenancy

From QEMU/KVM fundamentals through VFIO passthrough, SR-IOV, MIG partitioning, and cloud-native GPU orchestration for HPC and AI infrastructure.

1. QEMU/KVM Fundamentals

KVM — Kernel-based Virtual Machine

KVM is a type-1 (bare-metal) hypervisor implemented as a loadable Linux kernel module. When loaded, it turns the Linux kernel itself into a hypervisor. KVM exposes the /dev/kvm character device, which userspace programs use via ioctl() calls to create and manage virtual machines. KVM handles CPU virtualization (ring-based privilege separation using Intel VT-x VMCS / AMD-V VMCB structures) and memory virtualization (Extended Page Tables / Nested Page Tables for hardware-assisted guest-physical to host-physical address translation).

KVM operates by placing the host kernel in VMX root mode and guest code in VMX non-root mode. Sensitive instructions trigger a VM exit back to the host, where KVM handles the event and resumes the guest via VM entry. Modern hardware virtualization extensions minimize the frequency of VM exits, yielding near-native CPU performance.

QEMU — Quick EMUlator

QEMU is a userspace emulator and virtualizer. Running standalone, it uses dynamic binary translation (Tiny Code Generator / TCG) to emulate foreign CPU architectures. When paired with KVM, QEMU delegates CPU and memory virtualization to the kernel module and focuses on device emulation: disks (virtio-blk, virtio-scsi), network (virtio-net with vhost-net kernel acceleration), display (virtio-gpu, QXL), and other peripherals.

The virtio paravirtualized device framework avoids the overhead of emulating real hardware by using a shared-memory transport (virtqueues) between guest driver and host backend. vhost-net moves the virtio-net datapath into the kernel, and vhost-user moves it into a userspace process (e.g., DPDK/OVS) for even higher throughput.

QEMU/KVM Architecture Layers

Layer Component Responsibility
Hardware Intel VT-x / AMD-V, EPT/NPT, VT-d/AMD-Vi CPU virtualization extensions, IOMMU for device DMA isolation
Kernel KVM module (kvm.ko, kvm-intel.ko / kvm-amd.ko) VM lifecycle via /dev/kvm, vCPU scheduling, EPT/NPT management
Kernel VFIO, vhost-net, vhost-vsock Device passthrough, in-kernel virtio datapaths
Userspace QEMU (qemu-system-x86_64) Device emulation, machine model, migration, monitor/QMP
Userspace libvirt (libvirtd / virtqemud) VM management API, XML domain definitions, storage/network management
Userspace virsh, virt-manager, cockpit CLI and GUI tools for operators

VM Lifecycle

A virtual machine managed by libvirt follows a well-defined state machine:

define (persistent XML in /etc/libvirt/qemu/) → start (QEMU process launched, guest boots) → pause (vCPUs halted, memory preserved) → resume (vCPUs restarted) → shutdown (ACPI power button sent to guest) → destroy (QEMU process killed immediately). VMs can also be saved (memory snapshot to disk) and restored, or live-migrated to another host with minimal downtime (<100 ms for memory-converging migration). 

<domain type='kvm'>
  <name>gpu-worker-01</name>
  <memory unit='GiB'>64</memory>
  <vcpu placement='static'>16</vcpu>

  <cpu mode='host-passthrough' check='none'>
    <topology sockets='1' dies='1' cores='8' threads='2'/>
    <numa>
      <cell id='0' cpus='0-15' memory='64' unit='GiB'/>
    </numa>
  </cpu>

  <os>
    <type arch='x86_64' machine='q35'>hvm</type>
    <boot dev='hd'/>
  </os>

  <features>
    <acpi/>
    <apic/>
    <ioapic driver='qemu'/>
  </features>

  <devices>
    <!-- GPU passthrough via VFIO -->
    <hostdev mode='subsystem' type='pci' managed='yes'>
      <source>
        <address domain='0x0000' bus='0x41' slot='0x00' function='0x0'/>
      </source>
    </hostdev>

    <!-- virtio-net with vhost acceleration -->
    <interface type='bridge'>
      <source bridge='br0'/>
      <model type='virtio'/>
      <driver name='vhost' queues='8'/>
    </interface>

    <!-- virtio-scsi disk -->
    <disk type='file' device='disk'>
      <driver name='qemu' type='qcow2' cache='none' io='native'/>
      <source file='/var/lib/libvirt/images/gpu-worker-01.qcow2'/>
      <target dev='sda' bus='scsi'/>
    </disk>
    <controller type='scsi' model='virtio-scsi'/>
  </devices>
</domain>

QEMU Command Line — GPU Passthrough VM

The equivalent direct QEMU invocation (what libvirt generates under the hood):

# Launch a Q35-machine VM with KVM, 64 GB RAM, 16 vCPUs,
# one VFIO-passthrough GPU, and a vhost-accelerated virtio-net NIC.

qemu-system-x86_64 \
  -enable-kvm \
  -machine q35,accel=kvm,kernel-irqchip=split \
  -cpu host \
  -m 64G \
  -smp 16,sockets=1,cores=8,threads=2 \
  -drive file=/var/lib/libvirt/images/gpu-worker-01.qcow2,format=qcow2,if=none,id=disk0,cache=none,aio=native \
  -device virtio-scsi-pci,id=scsi0 \
  -device scsi-hd,drive=disk0,bus=scsi0.0 \
  -device vfio-pci,host=41:00.0,multifunction=on \
  -netdev tap,id=net0,vhost=on,queues=8 \
  -device virtio-net-pci,netdev=net0,mq=on,vectors=18 \
  -nographic \
  -monitor unix:/tmp/qemu-monitor.sock,server,nowait
Why Q35? The Q35 machine type emulates an Intel ICH9 chipset with native PCIe topology instead of PCI. This is required for proper IOMMU group separation when doing multi-device passthrough, and it supports PCIe-native hotplug. Always use Q35 for GPU passthrough workloads.

2. GPU Passthrough with VFIO

VFIO — Virtual Function I/O

VFIO is a Linux kernel framework that provides safe, userspace-driven device access with full IOMMU protection. Unlike the legacy pci-stub / UIO approach, VFIO enforces DMA isolation via the IOMMU, preventing a rogue VM from performing DMA attacks against host memory or other guests. VFIO exposes devices through /dev/vfio/ container and group file descriptors, which QEMU opens to map device BARs (Base Address Registers) and configure interrupts (MSI/MSI-X) for the guest.

IOMMU Groups

An IOMMU group is the smallest set of devices that the IOMMU can isolate from all other devices. All devices within a group share the same IOMMU domain and can DMA to each other's memory — therefore, all devices in a group must be passed to the same VM (or all remain on the host). This is dictated by the hardware topology and ACS (Access Control Services) capability of PCIe bridges.

In well-designed server platforms (DGX, HGX, Supermicro GPU servers), each GPU typically resides in its own IOMMU group because the root ports and PCIe switches implement ACS. Consumer motherboards often lump multiple devices into one group, requiring the pcie_acs_override kernel patch (not recommended for production).

VFIO GPU Passthrough Architecture

Step-by-Step GPU Passthrough

Complete procedure for passing an NVIDIA GPU to a QEMU/KVM virtual machine:

# Add kernel parameters via GRUB
# For Intel:
sudo sed -i 's/GRUB_CMDLINE_LINUX_DEFAULT="/GRUB_CMDLINE_LINUX_DEFAULT="intel_iommu=on iommu=pt /' /etc/default/grub
# For AMD:
sudo sed -i 's/GRUB_CMDLINE_LINUX_DEFAULT="/GRUB_CMDLINE_LINUX_DEFAULT="amd_iommu=on iommu=pt /' /etc/default/grub

sudo update-grub && sudo reboot

# Verify IOMMU is active after reboot
dmesg | grep -i -e DMAR -e IOMMU
# Should see: "DMAR: IOMMU enabled" or "AMD-Vi: AMD IOMMUv2 loaded"

# Verify via /proc/cmdline
cat /proc/cmdline
# ... intel_iommu=on iommu=pt ...

The iommu=pt (passthrough) parameter tells the kernel to not put host devices behind IOMMU translation unless explicitly needed (by VFIO). This avoids DMA performance overhead for devices that remain on the host. 

# Find the GPU's PCI address
lspci -nn | grep -i nvidia
# 41:00.0 3D controller [0302]: NVIDIA Corporation H100 [2330] (rev a1)

# Find the IOMMU group for this device
readlink /sys/bus/pci/devices/0000:41:00.0/iommu_group
# ../../../kernel/iommu_groups/42

# List ALL devices in that IOMMU group
ls -la /sys/kernel/iommu_groups/42/devices/
# 0000:41:00.0  (GPU)
# If other devices appear here, they must ALL be passed through together

# Script to enumerate all IOMMU groups
for d in /sys/kernel/iommu_groups/*/devices/*; do
  n=$(basename $(dirname $(dirname "$d")))
  echo "IOMMU Group $n: $(lspci -nns ${d##*/})"
done | sort -t: -k1 -n
# Check current driver binding
lspci -ks 41:00.0
# Kernel driver in use: nvidia
# Kernel modules: nvidia

# Get vendor:device ID for VFIO binding
lspci -ns 41:00.0
# 41:00.0 0302: 10de:2330 (rev a1)
# vendor=10de (NVIDIA), device=2330 (H100)

# Method 1: Runtime unbind/rebind (immediate, no reboot)
# Unbind from nvidia driver
echo 0000:41:00.0 | sudo tee /sys/bus/pci/devices/0000:41:00.0/driver/unbind

# Load vfio-pci module
sudo modprobe vfio-pci

# Bind to vfio-pci
echo 10de 2330 | sudo tee /sys/bus/pci/drivers/vfio-pci/new_id

# Verify
lspci -ks 41:00.0
# Kernel driver in use: vfio-pci

# Method 2: Persistent via modprobe config (survives reboot)
echo "options vfio-pci ids=10de:2330" | sudo tee /etc/modprobe.d/vfio.conf
echo -e "vfio-pci\nvfio\nvfio_iommu_type1\nvfio_virqfd" | sudo tee /etc/modules-load.d/vfio.conf

# Blacklist nvidia for this GPU if sharing system with other GPUs
# (use driver binding via BDF instead for selective passthrough)
sudo update-initramfs -u && sudo reboot
# Launch with QEMU directly
sudo qemu-system-x86_64 \
  -enable-kvm \
  -machine q35,accel=kvm,kernel-irqchip=split \
  -cpu host \
  -m 64G \
  -smp 16,sockets=1,cores=8,threads=2 \
  -drive file=disk.qcow2,format=qcow2,if=virtio \
  -device vfio-pci,host=41:00.0 \
  -netdev tap,id=net0,vhost=on \
  -device virtio-net-pci,netdev=net0 \
  -vnc :0

# Or use libvirt (recommended for production)
virsh start gpu-worker-01

# Inside the guest, the GPU appears natively:
nvidia-smi
# Shows H100 80GB as if bare metal
ACS (Access Control Services): For proper IOMMU isolation in multi-GPU servers, the PCIe root ports and downstream switches must support ACS. Without ACS, the IOMMU cannot distinguish peer-to-peer DMA between devices behind the same switch, forcing them into one IOMMU group. Server-grade platforms (DGX H100, HGX, Supermicro 4124GS) have proper ACS support. Verify with: lspci -vvv | grep -i "access control"

VFIO Passthrough Performance

Metric Bare Metal VFIO Passthrough Overhead
GPU Compute (TFLOPS FP16) 989 (H100 SXM) ~985 <0.5%
GPU Memory Bandwidth 3.35 TB/s ~3.34 TB/s <0.3%
PCIe DMA Latency ~1.2 µs ~1.3 µs ~8%
NCCL AllReduce (8xGPU) Baseline ~97-99% of baseline 1-3%
CUDA Kernel Launch ~3 µs ~3.1 µs ~3%
Limitation: VFIO passthrough assigns the entire physical GPU to one VM. You cannot share a single GPU among multiple VMs with VFIO alone. For GPU sharing, use MIG (hardware partitioning), vGPU (time-slicing), or SR-IOV (where supported). MIG slices can themselves be passed through via VFIO to individual VMs.

3. SR-IOV (Single Root I/O Virtualization)

What is SR-IOV?

SR-IOV is a PCIe specification that allows a single physical device to present itself as multiple virtual devices at the hardware level. The physical device exposes a Physical Function (PF) — the full-featured PCIe function with all configuration capabilities — and one or more Virtual Functions (VFs), which are lightweight PCIe functions with their own BARs, MSI-X vectors, and queue sets, but with reduced configuration space. Each VF appears as an independent PCIe device to the OS and can be assigned to a different VM via VFIO, achieving near-native I/O performance without software-based virtio emulation overhead.

PF vs VF Comparison

Attribute Physical Function (PF) Virtual Function (VF)
PCIe Config Space Full (Type 0, 4 KB standard + extended) Reduced (lightweight, limited caps)
BAR Resources All device BARs Subset (own queue memory region)
Driver on Host Full driver (e.g., mlx5_core) Can be bound to vfio-pci for passthrough
Management Controls VF creation, MAC assignment, VLAN, rate limit No management control; uses assigned resources
Typical Count 1 per port Up to 128-1024 per PF (device-dependent)

NIC SR-IOV with ConnectX-7

SR-IOV is extremely common for network adapters. The NVIDIA/Mellanox ConnectX-7 supports up to 1024 VFs per port, making it ideal for high-density VM hosting. Each VF gets a hardware queue pair, its own MAC address, optional VLAN tag, and configurable bandwidth limits — all enforced in hardware. 

# 1. Verify SR-IOV capability
lspci -vs 31:00.0 | grep -i "sr-iov"
#   Capabilities: [180] Single Root I/O Virtualization (SR-IOV)

# Total VFs supported
cat /sys/class/net/ens1f0/device/sriov_totalvfs
# 1024

# 2. Create 8 Virtual Functions
echo 8 | sudo tee /sys/class/net/ens1f0/device/sriov_numvfs

# Verify VFs appeared as PCIe devices
lspci | grep -i mellanox
# 31:00.0 Ethernet controller: Mellanox ConnectX-7 (PF)
# 31:00.1 Ethernet controller: Mellanox ConnectX-7 Virtual Function
# 31:00.2 Ethernet controller: Mellanox ConnectX-7 Virtual Function
# ... (8 VFs total)

# VFs also appear as network interfaces
ip link show ens1f0
# ... vf 0 MAC 00:00:00:00:00:00, vf 1 MAC 00:00:00:00:00:00 ...

# 3. Configure each VF (MAC, VLAN, bandwidth, spoofchk)
sudo ip link set ens1f0 vf 0 mac 52:54:00:aa:bb:01 vlan 100 rate 50000 spoofchk on
sudo ip link set ens1f0 vf 1 mac 52:54:00:aa:bb:02 vlan 200 rate 25000 spoofchk on
sudo ip link set ens1f0 vf 2 mac 52:54:00:aa:bb:03 vlan 100 rate 100000 spoofchk on
# rate is in Mbps (50000 = 50 Gbps)

# 4. Verify VF configuration
ip link show ens1f0
# vf 0: MAC 52:54:00:aa:bb:01, vlan 100, rate 50000Mbps, spoofchk on
# vf 1: MAC 52:54:00:aa:bb:02, vlan 200, rate 25000Mbps, spoofchk on

# 5. Bind VF to vfio-pci for passthrough to a VM
# Find VF's PCI BDF
VF0_BDF=$(readlink -f /sys/class/net/ens1f0/device/virtfn0 | xargs basename)
echo "$VF0_BDF"  # e.g., 0000:31:00.1

# Unbind from mlx5_core
echo "$VF0_BDF" | sudo tee /sys/bus/pci/drivers/mlx5_core/unbind

# Bind to vfio-pci
echo "vfio-pci" | sudo tee /sys/bus/pci/devices/$VF0_BDF/driver_override
echo "$VF0_BDF" | sudo tee /sys/bus/pci/drivers_probe

# 6. Pass VF to VM
qemu-system-x86_64 -enable-kvm -m 16G -smp 4 \
  -device vfio-pci,host=31:00.1 \
  -drive file=vm-disk.qcow2,format=qcow2,if=virtio \
  ...

# 7. Make VFs persistent across reboot (systemd-based)
# /etc/systemd/system/sriov-vfs.service
# [Unit]
# Description=Create SR-IOV VFs
# After=network-pre.target
# Before=network.target
#
# [Service]
# Type=oneshot
# ExecStart=/bin/bash -c 'echo 8 > /sys/class/net/ens1f0/device/sriov_numvfs'
# RemainAfterExit=yes
#
# [Install]
# WantedBy=multi-user.target

SR-IOV VF vs virtio-net Performance

Metric SR-IOV VF (ConnectX-7) virtio-net + vhost-net virtio-net (userspace)
Throughput (single flow) ~100 Gbps (line rate) ~40-60 Gbps ~10-20 Gbps
Latency (RTT) ~2-5 µs ~15-25 µs ~40-80 µs
CPU Overhead per Gbps Minimal (hardware path) Moderate (kernel switching) High (QEMU context switches)
Live Migration Requires bond failover or RDMA migration Transparent (virtqueue state) Transparent
Max Queues Hardware-limited (typically 16-64 per VF) Configurable (multiqueue) Configurable

GPU SR-IOV

GPU SR-IOV support is limited compared to NIC SR-IOV. NVIDIA does not expose native SR-IOV on most GPU products. Instead, GPU sharing is achieved through:

  • NVIDIA vGPU (GRID): A mediated passthrough (mdev) framework where the NVIDIA host driver multiplexes GPU access across VMs via time-slicing or MIG-backed profiles. This uses the mdev (mediated device) framework rather than true SR-IOV VFs.
  • MIG + VFIO: On A100/H100, MIG instances can be exposed as individual compute instances and passed to VMs. This provides true hardware isolation (each instance has dedicated SMs and memory) without SR-IOV.
  • Intel Data Center GPU Flex (Arctic Sound-M): One of the few GPUs with true SR-IOV support, exposing up to 62 VFs per PF for cloud gaming and media workloads.
Best Practice for HPC/AI: In Superpod deployments, NIC SR-IOV (ConnectX-7 VFs) is heavily used to provide each VM or container with a dedicated high-performance network path. GPU access is typically full passthrough (VFIO) or MIG partitioning rather than SR-IOV.

4. NVIDIA MIG (Multi-Instance GPU)

What is MIG?

Multi-Instance GPU (MIG) is a hardware-level GPU partitioning technology available on NVIDIA A100, A30, and H100 GPUs (Ampere and Hopper architectures). MIG divides a single physical GPU into up to 7 isolated GPU instances, each with its own dedicated set of:

  • Streaming Multiprocessors (SMs) — compute engines for CUDA/Tensor Core operations
  • Memory controllers and corresponding HBM memory slice — guaranteed memory bandwidth
  • L2 cache partition — no cache interference between instances
  • Copy engines (CE) and decoders (NVDEC/NVJPG on supported profiles)
  • Unique GPU instance ID — each instance is addressable as a separate GPU device

MIG provides true hardware-level isolation: a workload running on one MIG instance cannot observe or interfere with workloads on other instances, including memory access patterns, cache behavior, and error conditions (ECC errors are scoped to the instance). This is fundamentally different from time-slicing, where workloads share all resources and can experience interference.

MIG Availability: MIG is only available on data center GPUs with Ampere or newer architecture: A100 (40GB/80GB), A30 (24GB), H100 (80GB), and H200 (141GB). It is not available on consumer GPUs (RTX 4090), professional GPUs (RTX 6000 Ada), or older data center GPUs (V100, T4). MIG requires the GPU to be in MIG mode, which requires a GPU reset or reboot to enable/disable.

H100 MIG Partitioning Profiles

H100 SXM MIG Profile Summary

Profile GI Profile ID SMs Memory Mem Controllers L2 Cache Max Instances
1g.10gb 19 16 10 GB HBM3 1 ~7 MB 7
1g.10gb+me 20 16 10 GB HBM3 1 ~7 MB 1 (includes all media engines)
1g.20gb 15 16 20 GB HBM3 2 ~14 MB 4
2g.20gb 14 28 20 GB HBM3 2 ~14 MB 3
3g.40gb 2 56 40 GB HBM3 4 ~28 MB 2
4g.40gb 5 76 40 GB HBM3 4 ~28 MB 1
7g.80gb 0 132 80 GB HBM3 8 ~50 MB 1 
# ===== Enable MIG Mode =====
# Must be done once per GPU; requires GPU reset (no active processes)

# Enable MIG on GPU 0
sudo nvidia-smi -i 0 -mig 1
# Warning: MIG mode enable requires a GPU reset. Reboot or:
sudo nvidia-smi -i 0 --gpu-reset

# Verify MIG is enabled
nvidia-smi -i 0 --query-gpu=mig.mode.current --format=csv,noheader
# Enabled

# ===== List Available Profiles =====
nvidia-smi mig -i 0 -lgip
# +-----------------------------------------------------------------------------+
# | GPU instance profiles:                                                       |
# | GPU   Name          ID    Instances   Memory     SMs    ...                  |
# |   0   MIG 1g.10gb   19       7       9856 MiB    16     ...                  |
# |   0   MIG 2g.20gb   14       3      19968 MiB    28     ...                  |
# |   0   MIG 3g.40gb    2       2      40192 MiB    56     ...                  |
# |   0   MIG 4g.40gb    5       1      40192 MiB    76     ...                  |
# |   0   MIG 7g.80gb    0       1      81152 MiB   132     ...                  |
# +-----------------------------------------------------------------------------+

# ===== Create GPU Instances =====
# Create 7x 1g.10gb instances (maximum partitioning)
sudo nvidia-smi mig -i 0 -cgi 19,19,19,19,19,19,19

# List GPU instances
nvidia-smi mig -i 0 -lgi
# +-------------------------------------------------------+
# | GPU instances:                                         |
# | GPU   Inst   Profile    Placement    ...               |
# |   0      1   MIG 1g.10gb  {0}        ...               |
# |   0      2   MIG 1g.10gb  {1}        ...               |
# |   0      3   MIG 1g.10gb  {2}        ...               |
# |   0      4   MIG 1g.10gb  {3}        ...               |
# |   0      5   MIG 1g.10gb  {4}        ...               |
# |   0      6   MIG 1g.10gb  {5}        ...               |
# |   0      7   MIG 1g.10gb  {6}        ...               |
# +-------------------------------------------------------+

# ===== Create Compute Instances (CI) within each GI =====
# Each GI needs at least one CI to be usable
# -C flag creates default CI (uses all SMs in the GI)
sudo nvidia-smi mig -i 0 -cgi 19,19,19,19,19,19,19 -C
# Or create CIs individually:
sudo nvidia-smi mig -i 0 -gi 1 -cci 0
# Profile ID 0 = full CI (all SMs in this GI)

# List compute instances
nvidia-smi mig -i 0 -lci

# ===== Verify with nvidia-smi =====
nvidia-smi
# Shows each MIG instance as a separate entry:
# |   MIG 1g.10gb      1/7     9856MiB |  ...  |
# |   MIG 1g.10gb      2/7     9856MiB |  ...  |
# ... (7 lines)

# ===== Alternative: Mixed profile configuration =====
# Destroy all instances first
sudo nvidia-smi mig -i 0 -dci  # destroy compute instances
sudo nvidia-smi mig -i 0 -dgi  # destroy GPU instances

# Create mixed: 1x 3g.40gb + 2x 2g.20gb
sudo nvidia-smi mig -i 0 -cgi 2,14,14 -C

# ===== Use MIG Instances =====
# Each MIG instance has a UUID
nvidia-smi -L
# GPU 0: NVIDIA H100 80GB HBM3 (UUID: GPU-xxxxxxxx-xxxx-...)
#   MIG 1g.10gb  Device 0: (UUID: MIG-xxxxxxxx-xxxx-...)
#   MIG 1g.10gb  Device 1: (UUID: MIG-yyyyyyyy-yyyy-...)

# Target a specific MIG instance
export CUDA_VISIBLE_DEVICES=MIG-xxxxxxxx-xxxx-xxxx-xxxx-xxxxxxxxxxxx
python train.py  # runs only on that MIG slice

# Or use MIG device index syntax:
export CUDA_VISIBLE_DEVICES=MIG-GPU-xxxxxxxx/1/0
# Format: MIG-{GPU-UUID}/{GPU Instance ID}/{Compute Instance ID}

# ===== Disable MIG Mode =====
sudo nvidia-smi mig -i 0 -dci
sudo nvidia-smi mig -i 0 -dgi
sudo nvidia-smi -i 0 -mig 0
sudo nvidia-smi -i 0 --gpu-reset

MIG + Kubernetes

In Kubernetes, the NVIDIA GPU Operator and k8s-device-plugin can automatically discover MIG instances and expose them as schedulable resources. Each MIG slice appears as a distinct resource type in the node's capacity: 

# Pod spec requesting a specific MIG profile
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: inference-small
spec:
  containers:
  - name: model-server
    image: nvcr.io/nvidia/tritonserver:24.01-py3
    resources:
      limits:
        nvidia.com/mig-1g.10gb: 1    # Request one 1g.10gb MIG slice
---
# Node capacity (kubectl describe node gpu-node-01):
# Capacity:
#   nvidia.com/mig-1g.10gb:  7
#   nvidia.com/mig-2g.20gb:  0
#   nvidia.com/mig-3g.40gb:  0
#   nvidia.com/mig-7g.80gb:  0

The device plugin supports three MIG strategies configured via the mig.strategy Helm value:

  • none: MIG disabled; GPUs exposed as nvidia.com/gpu
  • single: All GPUs use the same MIG profile; resources named nvidia.com/gpu
  • mixed: Different MIG profiles per GPU; resources named nvidia.com/mig-{profile}

MIG vs vGPU vs Time-Slicing

Feature MIG vGPU (GRID) Time-Slicing (MPS/Default)
Isolation Level Hardware (SMs, memory, L2, ECC) Software (driver-mediated) None (shared everything)
Max Partitions 7 per GPU Up to 32 per GPU (profile-dependent) Unlimited (time-multiplexed)
Memory Isolation Dedicated HBM slice per instance Framebuffer partitioned by profile Shared (OOM affects all)
Performance Predictability Guaranteed (dedicated resources) Good (time-slice scheduling) Variable (contention-dependent)
Fault Isolation Yes (ECC errors scoped to instance) Partial No (one crash affects all)
GPU Support A100, A30, H100, H200 Most data center GPUs (T4, A100, H100, L4, L40S) All CUDA GPUs
License Required No (driver-included) Yes (NVIDIA AI Enterprise / GRID) No
Reconfiguration Requires GPU reset VM restart Dynamic
Use Case Multi-tenant inference, CI/CD, dev VDI, multi-tenant VMs, cloud Best-effort sharing, dev/test

5. NVIDIA vGPU (GRID)

Software-Based GPU Virtualization

NVIDIA vGPU (branded under NVIDIA AI Enterprise and previously NVIDIA GRID) enables multiple virtual machines to share a single physical GPU. The NVIDIA Virtual GPU Manager runs on the hypervisor host (KVM, VMware ESXi, Citrix Hypervisor) and mediates access to the GPU hardware. Each VM runs a standard NVIDIA guest driver that communicates with the vGPU Manager through a paravirtualized channel.

vGPU uses the Linux kernel's mdev (mediated device) framework on KVM hosts. The vGPU Manager creates mdev instances (e.g., /sys/class/mdev_bus/0000:41:00.0/{uuid}) that are passed to QEMU as -device vfio-pci,sysfsdev=/sys/.... Each mdev instance represents a virtual GPU with a specific profile defining its framebuffer allocation and time-slice scheduling weight. 

# ===== Install vGPU Manager on KVM Host =====
# Download NVIDIA-Linux-x86_64-535.161.08-vgpu-kvm.run from NVIDIA Licensing Portal
sudo ./NVIDIA-Linux-x86_64-535.161.08-vgpu-kvm.run --dkms

# Verify vGPU Manager loaded
lsmod | grep nvidia_vgpu_vfio
nvidia-smi  # Shows "NVIDIA-SMI ... Driver Version: 535.161.08 (vGPU Host)"

# ===== List Available vGPU Types =====
# Each GPU exposes multiple mdev types
ls /sys/class/mdev_bus/0000:41:00.0/mdev_supported_types/
# nvidia-700  nvidia-701  nvidia-702  nvidia-703 ...

# View profile details
cat /sys/class/mdev_bus/0000:41:00.0/mdev_supported_types/nvidia-700/name
# NVIDIA H100-1Q    (1 GB framebuffer, Q-series = vWS/compute)

cat /sys/class/mdev_bus/0000:41:00.0/mdev_supported_types/nvidia-700/description
# num_heads=4, frl_config=60, framebuffer=1024M, max_resolution=4096x2160

cat /sys/class/mdev_bus/0000:41:00.0/mdev_supported_types/nvidia-700/available_instances
# 16

# ===== Create a vGPU Instance =====
# Generate a UUID for the mdev device
VGPU_UUID=$(uuidgen)

# Create the mdev instance
echo "$VGPU_UUID" | sudo tee \
  /sys/class/mdev_bus/0000:41:00.0/mdev_supported_types/nvidia-703/create

# ===== Attach to a libvirt VM =====
# Add to domain XML:
# <hostdev mode='subsystem' type='mdev' managed='no' model='vfio-pci'>
#   <source>
#     <address uuid='$VGPU_UUID'/>
#   </source>
# </hostdev>

virsh start my-vm

# ===== Inside the Guest VM =====
# Install standard NVIDIA GRID guest driver
nvidia-smi
# Shows the virtual GPU (e.g., "NVIDIA H100-10C" for compute profile)

vGPU Profile Types

Series Suffix Primary Use Case CUDA / OpenCL Display Heads
Q-series -Q Professional visualization (Quadro-equivalent) Yes Up to 4
C-series -C Compute / AI inference and training Yes 0 (headless)
B-series -B Virtual desktop (VDI) No Up to 2
A-series -A Virtual applications (RDSH/app streaming) No 1

Time-Slicing vs MIG-Backed vGPU

On GPUs that support MIG (A100, H100), vGPU can operate in two modes:

  • Time-sliced vGPU: The vGPU scheduler rapidly context-switches between VMs on a fixed time quantum (default: best-effort, or configurable equal-share / fixed-share). All VMs share the full SM array and memory bandwidth but get exclusive access during their time slice. Lower isolation, higher flexibility in profile sizes.
  • MIG-backed vGPU: Each vGPU instance is backed by a MIG GPU Instance. The VM gets dedicated SMs, memory, and L2 cache with hardware isolation. Provides the isolation benefits of MIG with the management benefits of the vGPU framework (licensing, live migration, monitoring via NVIDIA DCGM). 
# MIG-backed vGPU: first create MIG instances, then vGPU types map to them
sudo nvidia-smi mig -i 0 -cgi 19,19,19,19,19,19,19 -C

# vGPU types for MIG-backed mode appear with MIG profile in the name
ls /sys/class/mdev_bus/0000:41:00.0/mdev_supported_types/ | grep MIG
# nvidia-710   (H100-1g.10gb-C, compute profile for 1g.10gb MIG)
# nvidia-711   (H100-2g.20gb-C, compute profile for 2g.20gb MIG)

cat /sys/class/mdev_bus/0000:41:00.0/mdev_supported_types/nvidia-710/name
# NVIDIA H100-1g.10gb-C
Licensing: NVIDIA vGPU requires a license from the NVIDIA Licensing Server (NLS) or a Delegated License Service (DLS) appliance on-premises. Each concurrent vGPU session consumes one license. License types include NVIDIA AI Enterprise (full stack: vGPU + containers + frameworks + support) and vWS (Quadro Virtual Workstation). Without a valid license, vGPU operates in a degraded mode with reduced framebuffer and frame rate.

6. Cloud-Native Virtualization

KubeVirt — VMs as Kubernetes Pods

KubeVirt extends Kubernetes with a CRD (Custom Resource Definition) called VirtualMachine that allows traditional virtual machines to be managed alongside containers using the Kubernetes API. Under the hood, KubeVirt runs QEMU/KVM inside a pod (the virt-launcher pod), wrapping the VM lifecycle in Kubernetes primitives: the VM is scheduled like a pod, its storage is provided via PVCs, and its network via CNI plugins.

For GPU workloads, KubeVirt supports both GPU passthrough (VFIO) and MIG device assignment to VMs. The NVIDIA GPU Operator detects KubeVirt and configures the nodes appropriately, including loading VFIO drivers and configuring IOMMU for devices designated for VM passthrough. 

apiVersion: kubevirt.io/v1
kind: VirtualMachine
metadata:
  name: gpu-worker-vm
  namespace: ml-workloads
spec:
  running: true
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: gpu-worker
    spec:
      domain:
        cpu:
          cores: 16
          sockets: 1
          threads: 2
          model: host-passthrough
          # Pin to NUMA node 0 for GPU locality
          numa:
            guestMappingPassthrough: {}
        memory:
          guest: 64Gi
          hugepages:
            pageSize: 1Gi
        devices:
          gpus:
          - name: gpu0
            deviceName: nvidia.com/H100_SXM  # Passed via VFIO
          interfaces:
          - name: default
            masquerade: {}
          - name: sriov-net
            sriov: {}
          disks:
          - name: rootdisk
            disk:
              bus: virtio
          - name: cloudinitdisk
            disk:
              bus: virtio
        machine:
          type: q35
      networks:
      - name: default
        pod: {}
      - name: sriov-net
        multus:
          networkName: sriov-net-100g
      volumes:
      - name: rootdisk
        persistentVolumeClaim:
          claimName: gpu-worker-root-pvc
      - name: cloudinitdisk
        cloudInitNoCloud:
          userData: |
            #cloud-config
            package_update: true
            packages:
              - nvidia-driver-535
              - cuda-toolkit-12-3

Bare Metal vs VM vs Container for GPU Workloads

Attribute Bare Metal VM (QEMU/KVM + VFIO) Container (Docker/K8s)
GPU Performance 100% (baseline) ~97-99% (VFIO overhead minimal) ~100% (shares host kernel & driver)
Isolation Physical (separate machines) Strong (hardware-enforced by VT-x + IOMMU) Weak (kernel namespace + cgroups)
Startup Time Minutes (BIOS/POST) 10-60 seconds Sub-second to seconds
Density 1 tenant per machine Limited by GPUs (1 GPU per VM or MIG slices) High (time-slicing, MIG, MPS)
Multi-Tenancy Security Excellent (air gap) Excellent (hardware isolation) Moderate (container escape risk)
Live Migration Not possible Supported (with limitations for passthrough devices) Checkpoint/restore (CRIU, experimental for GPU)
Driver Flexibility Any driver version Any driver version (per-VM independence) Must match host driver version
Orchestration MAAS, Foreman, manual libvirt, KubeVirt, OpenStack Kubernetes, Docker Swarm
Resource Overhead None ~2-5% CPU, ~1-2 GB RAM per VM Negligible

When to Use Each Approach

Use Bare Metal When:
  • Maximum GPU performance is critical (large-scale training)
  • Multi-node NVLink/NVSwitch topologies (DGX SuperPOD)
  • Single-tenant, dedicated infrastructure
  • GPUDirect RDMA with no abstraction overhead
Use VMs (KVM + VFIO) When:
  • Strong multi-tenant isolation required (cloud providers)
  • Different OS or driver versions per tenant
  • Compliance requirements mandate VM-level separation
  • Legacy workloads need a full OS environment
Use Containers When:
  • Rapid scaling and deployment (inference serving)
  • CI/CD pipelines and dev/test environments
  • Kubernetes-native orchestration desired
  • High density with MIG or time-slicing
Hybrid Pattern: In production Superpod deployments, a common hybrid pattern is KubeVirt + NVIDIA GPU Operator: training jobs run as bare-metal Kubernetes pods on nodes with full GPU passthrough via the NVIDIA device plugin, while inference and multi-tenant workloads run inside KubeVirt VMs with MIG-backed vGPU slices — all managed by the same Kubernetes control plane. This provides operational consistency while matching the isolation model to each workload's requirements.

Knowledge Check

Question 1
