5. Object Storage

Object storage organizes data as discrete objects in a flat namespace, each identified by a unique key. Unlike POSIX file systems, there is no directory hierarchy — objects are stored in buckets (containers) and accessed via HTTP-based APIs (primarily S3-compatible). This model scales massively and is ideal for storing large, immutable datasets.

S3-Compatible Object Storage

The Amazon S3 API has become the de facto standard for object storage. Key operations:

  • PUT: Upload an object (with metadata and tags)
  • GET: Download an object (supports byte-range requests for partial reads)
  • DELETE: Remove an object
  • LIST: Enumerate objects in a bucket (prefix-based filtering)
  • Multipart Upload: Upload large objects in parallel chunks (required for objects >5 GB)

Object storage is not POSIX: no open/close semantics, no byte-range writes to existing objects (objects are immutable once written), no hard/soft links, no rename (copy + delete instead), and no append operations (with some vendor extensions). Applications must be designed for PUT/GET patterns.

MinIO

MinIO is an open-source, S3-compatible object store designed for high performance on commodity hardware. It is written in Go and is commonly used as the object storage tier behind parallel file systems or as a standalone data lake.

Performance: A single MinIO server with NVMe drives can deliver 35+ GB/s GET and 12+ GB/s PUT throughput. Distributed MinIO across 32 nodes with NVMe can achieve 325+ GiB/s GET.

# Deploy MinIO distributed cluster (4 nodes, 4 drives each)
minio server http://minio-{1...4}/data/disk-{1...4}

# Configure mc (MinIO Client)
mc alias set gpu-store http://minio-1:9000 ACCESSKEY SECRETKEY

# Create a bucket for training data
mc mb gpu-store/training-data

# Upload a dataset with parallelism
mc cp --recursive /local/datasets/imagenet/ gpu-store/training-data/imagenet/ \
  --storage-class REDUCED_REDUNDANCY

# Set lifecycle policy (delete old checkpoints after 30 days)
mc ilm rule add gpu-store/checkpoints --expire-days 30 --prefix "epoch-"

# Enable bucket versioning
mc version enable gpu-store/model-artifacts

# Server-side encryption with KMS
mc encrypt set sse-s3 gpu-store/sensitive-data

# Replicate to remote site
mc replicate add gpu-store/training-data --remote-bucket backup-store/training-data

# Erasure coding: MinIO uses Reed-Solomon EC by default
# With 16 drives: EC:8 means 8 data + 8 parity (can lose 8 drives)
# With 16 drives: EC:4 means 12 data + 4 parity (can lose 4 drives)

Use Cases in GPU Clusters

Use Case Why Object Storage Access Pattern
Training datasets (large files) Scales to petabytes; cheap; immutable data fits PUT/GET model Bulk GET with prefetch into parallel FS or local NVMe
Model artifacts & weights Versioning; easy sharing across teams; cloud-portable PUT after training; GET for inference deployment
Checkpoint archival Lifecycle policies auto-delete old checkpoints; cheaper than SSD PUT during training; rare GET for recovery
Logs & metrics Append-like pattern with immutable log segments; cheap at scale PUT (write-once); batch GET for analysis
Data lake / analytics Query engines (Spark, Trino) natively read S3; schema-on-read Columnar scans (Parquet/ORC) via byte-range GET

6. Ceph

Ceph: Unified Distributed Storage

Ceph is an open-source distributed storage system that provides three storage interfaces on top of a single unified storage backend (RADOS):

  • RBD (RADOS Block Device): Block-level storage. Thin-provisioned virtual disks. Used extensively as Kubernetes PersistentVolumes, OpenStack Cinder volumes, and VM disks.
  • CephFS: POSIX-compliant distributed file system. Supports multiple active MDS servers for metadata scalability. Can serve as a parallel file system for HPC (though not as performant as Lustre/Weka for extreme-scale workloads).
  • RGW (RADOS Gateway): S3-compatible and Swift-compatible object storage interface. Production-grade S3 implementation used by many organizations.

CRUSH Algorithm

Controlled Replication Under Scalable Hashing (CRUSH)

CRUSH is the heart of Ceph's data distribution. Instead of maintaining a centralized lookup table mapping objects to storage devices (which would be a bottleneck), CRUSH uses a deterministic algorithmic approach. Any client can compute the exact location of any object given:

  1. The object name (hashed to a placement group, or PG)
  2. The CRUSH map (a hierarchical description of the storage topology: data centers, racks, hosts, OSDs)
  3. The CRUSH rules (placement policies, e.g., “place 3 replicas in 3 different racks”)

This eliminates any single point of failure for data location lookups and enables Ceph to scale to thousands of OSDs. When OSDs are added or removed, CRUSH deterministically rebalances the minimum amount of data.

Architecture Components

Component Role Typical Count Resource Notes
MON (Monitor) Maintains cluster map (OSD map, CRUSH map, PG map, MDS map). Paxos-based consensus for quorum. 3 or 5 (odd number for quorum) Low CPU, moderate RAM, fast SSD for monitor store
OSD (Object Storage Daemon) Stores data objects, handles replication/recovery/rebalancing. One OSD per physical disk (NVMe, SSD, or HDD). 10s to 10,000s 1–2 CPU cores per OSD, 4–8 GB RAM per OSD (NVMe: more)
MDS (Metadata Server) Manages CephFS metadata (directory hierarchy, inodes). Not needed if only using RBD/RGW. 1+ active, 1+ standby High CPU and RAM for large namespaces
MGR (Manager) Provides monitoring, metrics (Prometheus), dashboard, orchestration, and module framework. 2 (active + standby) Moderate CPU and RAM
RGW (RADOS Gateway) S3/Swift-compatible HTTP gateway. Stateless — scale horizontally behind a load balancer. 2+ (behind LB) CPU-bound for encryption; RAM for caching

Replication vs Erasure Coding

Data Protection Strategies

Aspect Replication (3x) Erasure Coding (e.g., 8+3)
Storage Overhead200% (3x raw capacity)37.5% (for k=8, m=3)
Write PerformanceFast (parallel writes to replicas)Slower (encode + write to k+m shards)
Read PerformanceFast (read from any replica)Fast for full reads; slower for partial reads requiring reconstruction
Recovery SpeedFast (copy from replica)Slower (reconstruct from k shards)
Fault ToleranceSurvives 2 failures (with 3x)Survives m failures (3 for 8+3)
Best ForHot data, RBD, CephFS metadata poolsCold/warm data, large objects, archival
 
# Deploy Ceph with cephadm (modern method)
cephadm bootstrap --mon-ip 10.0.10.1 --cluster-network 10.0.20.0/24

# Add hosts to the cluster
ceph orch host add storage-01 10.0.10.2
ceph orch host add storage-02 10.0.10.3
ceph orch host add storage-03 10.0.10.4

# Deploy OSDs on all available NVMe devices
ceph orch apply osd --all-available-devices

# Or target specific devices
ceph orch daemon add osd storage-01:/dev/nvme0n1
ceph orch daemon add osd storage-01:/dev/nvme1n1

# Create an erasure-coded pool for training data
ceph osd erasure-code-profile set ec-83 k=8 m=3 crush-failure-domain=host
ceph osd pool create training-data-ec 128 erasure ec-83

# Create a replicated pool for hot data (checkpoints)
ceph osd pool create checkpoints 64 replicated
ceph osd pool set checkpoints size 3 min_size 2

# CephFS for POSIX access
ceph fs volume create gpufs
ceph fs subvolumegroup create gpufs training

# NVMe OSD tuning (ceph.conf or central config)
ceph config set osd bluestore_cache_size_ssd 8589934592    # 8GB cache per OSD
ceph config set osd bluestore_min_alloc_size_ssd 4096      # 4K alloc for NVMe
ceph config set osd osd_op_num_threads_per_shard_ssd 2
ceph config set osd osd_memory_target 8589934592            # 8GB per OSD

# Enable msgr2 (v2 protocol) for on-wire encryption
ceph config set global ms_cluster_mode crc secure
ceph config set global ms_client_mode crc secure

# Check cluster health
ceph status
ceph osd tree
ceph df detail

# Integration with Kubernetes (Rook-Ceph)
# Deploy Rook operator:
kubectl apply -f https://raw.githubusercontent.com/rook/rook/release-1.14/deploy/examples/crds.yaml
kubectl apply -f https://raw.githubusercontent.com/rook/rook/release-1.14/deploy/examples/operator.yaml
kubectl apply -f https://raw.githubusercontent.com/rook/rook/release-1.14/deploy/examples/cluster.yaml

7. NVMe and NVMe-oF

NVMe (Non-Volatile Memory Express)

NVMe is a storage protocol designed specifically for flash (and future persistent memory) storage, running over the PCIe bus. It replaced the legacy AHCI/SATA protocol stack which was designed for spinning disks and had fundamental architectural limitations.

Feature AHCI (SATA) NVMe
Command Queues1 queue, 32 commands65,535 queues, 65,536 commands each
Interrupt HandlingSingle interrupt, coalescingMSI-X per-queue interrupts, direct CPU steering
Latency~6–10 ms (HDD), ~100 μs (SSD via SATA)<10 μs (PCIe Gen4/5 NVMe)
IOPS (peak)~100K (SATA SSD)1–2M+ IOPS per device
Bandwidth~550 MB/s (SATA III)7–14 GB/s (PCIe Gen4/Gen5 x4)
CPU OverheadHigh (register-based, complex state machine)Low (streamlined, minimal CPU cycles per I/O)

A single PCIe Gen5 x4 NVMe SSD (e.g., Samsung PM1743, Kioxia CM7) delivers ~14 GB/s sequential read and ~2M+ random IOPS. A DGX H100 node with 8 local NVMe SSDs provides ~100 GB/s of local storage bandwidth.

NVMe-oF (NVMe over Fabrics)

Extending NVMe Across the Network

NVMe-oF extends the NVMe protocol over network fabrics, allowing a host to access remote NVMe devices with near-local performance. The remote NVMe namespaces appear as local /dev/nvmeXnY devices on the initiator host.

Transport Underlying Network Added Latency Throughput Pros
NVMe/RDMA InfiniBand or RoCEv2 <10 μs Wire-speed (200–400 Gb/s) Lowest latency; zero-copy; kernel bypass
NVMe/TCP Standard Ethernet ~30–80 μs Near wire-speed (100–400 GbE) No special NIC needed; works over any TCP/IP network
NVMe/FC Fibre Channel ~20–50 μs 32–128 Gb/s per port Leverages existing FC infrastructure 
# === TARGET SIDE (Storage Server) ===

# Load NVMe target modules
modprobe nvmet
modprobe nvmet-tcp      # For NVMe/TCP
modprobe nvmet-rdma     # For NVMe/RDMA

# Create a subsystem
mkdir -p /sys/kernel/config/nvmet/subsystems/nvme-training-data
cd /sys/kernel/config/nvmet/subsystems/nvme-training-data
echo 1 > attr_allow_any_host

# Create a namespace backed by a local NVMe device
mkdir namespaces/1
echo /dev/nvme0n1 > namespaces/1/device_path
echo 1 > namespaces/1/enable

# Create a TCP port
mkdir -p /sys/kernel/config/nvmet/ports/1
echo 10.0.50.10 > /sys/kernel/config/nvmet/ports/1/addr_traddr
echo 4420 > /sys/kernel/config/nvmet/ports/1/addr_trsvcid
echo tcp > /sys/kernel/config/nvmet/ports/1/addr_trtype
echo ipv4 > /sys/kernel/config/nvmet/ports/1/addr_adrfam

# Link subsystem to port
ln -s /sys/kernel/config/nvmet/subsystems/nvme-training-data \
      /sys/kernel/config/nvmet/ports/1/subsystems/nvme-training-data


# === INITIATOR SIDE (Compute Node) ===

# Load NVMe-oF client module
modprobe nvme-tcp

# Discover available subsystems
nvme discover -t tcp -a 10.0.50.10 -s 4420

# Connect to the remote NVMe subsystem
nvme connect -t tcp -a 10.0.50.10 -s 4420 \
  -n nvme-training-data --nr-io-queues=32

# Verify the remote namespace appears as local device
nvme list
# /dev/nvme1n1  nvme-training-data  3.84 TB  ...

# For RDMA transport:
modprobe nvme-rdma
nvme connect -t rdma -a 10.0.50.10 -s 4420 \
  -n nvme-training-data --nr-io-queues=32

# Performance test
fio --name=seqread --ioengine=io_uring --direct=1 --bs=128k \
  --numjobs=16 --iodepth=32 --rw=read --size=100G \
  --filename=/dev/nvme1n1

NVIDIA GPUDirect Storage (GDS)

Direct Path: NVMe to GPU Memory

GPUDirect Storage (GDS) enables a direct DMA data path between NVMe storage (local or NVMe-oF) and GPU memory, completely bypassing the CPU and system memory. Without GDS, data must follow this path: NVMe → CPU/DRAM bounce buffer → GPU HBM. With GDS, the path becomes: NVMe → GPU HBM (via PCIe peer-to-peer or network RDMA).

Benefits:

  • Eliminates CPU bounce buffer copies (reduces latency by 30–50%)
  • Frees CPU cores for other work (data preprocessing, scheduling)
  • Reduces system memory bandwidth consumption
  • Achieves up to ~12 GB/s per GPU from local NVMe
  • Supported by cuFile API (part of CUDA toolkit) and integrated into major frameworks via kvikio library
# Install GDS (included in CUDA toolkit 11.4+)
apt install nvidia-gds

# Verify GDS is functional
/usr/local/cuda/gds/tools/gdscheck -p

# GDS configuration (/etc/cufile.json)
# {
#   "logging": { "log_level": 1 },
#   "properties": {
#     "max_direct_io_size": "16M",
#     "max_device_cache_size": "128M",
#     "max_batch_io_size": "8M",
#     "gds_rdma_write_support": true
#   }
# }

# Benchmark GDS (included tool)
/usr/local/cuda/gds/tools/gdsio -f /mnt/lustre/test_gds -d 0 \
  -w 4 -s 10G -i 1M -x 0 -I 1

# Python with kvikio (cuFile bindings)
# import kvikio
# f = kvikio.CuFile("/mnt/weka/data.bin", "r")
# buf = cupy.empty(1_000_000, dtype="float32")
# f.read(buf)  # Direct NVMe -> GPU transfer

8. Storage Networking

Separation of Concerns: Dedicated Storage Fabric

In GPU Superpod deployments, the storage network is physically separate from the GPU compute fabric (InfiniBand for NCCL/collective communication). Storage typically runs over a dedicated Ethernet network (100GbE or 200GbE) to avoid interference with GPU-to-GPU traffic. Some deployments use a dedicated InfiniBand partition for storage (LNET over o2ib), while others use separate Ethernet switches entirely.

Storage Network Topology: GPU Cluster with Dedicated Storage Fabric

RAID Levels and Beyond

RAID Level Description Min Disks Fault Tolerance Usable Capacity HPC Notes
RAID 0Striping only (no parity)20 (any failure = total loss)100%Scratch / temp only
RAID 1Mirroring21 disk failure50%OS / boot drives
RAID 5Striping + distributed parity31 disk failure(N-1)/NRarely used for HPC (write penalty, slow rebuild)
RAID 6Striping + double parity42 disk failures(N-2)/NBetter protection; still slow rebuild on large disks
RAID 10Mirrored stripes41 per mirror pair50%Good performance; used for metadata (MDT/MDS)
Erasure CodingReed-Solomon or similar across nodesk+mm failuresk/(k+m)Preferred at scale (Ceph, MinIO, Weka); node-level protection

ZFS for Storage Servers

ZFS is a combined volume manager and file system that provides data integrity, compression, and flexible storage management. It is used as the backend filesystem on Lustre OST/MDT devices and on Ceph OSD nodes.

# Create a ZFS pool with RAID-Z2 (double parity, similar to RAID 6)
zpool create -o ashift=12 tank raidz2 \
  /dev/nvme0n1 /dev/nvme1n1 /dev/nvme2n1 /dev/nvme3n1 \
  /dev/nvme4n1 /dev/nvme5n1 /dev/nvme6n1 /dev/nvme7n1

# Add a special allocation class (metadata on fast NVMe)
zpool add tank special mirror /dev/nvme8n1 /dev/nvme9n1

# Add L2ARC read cache
zpool add tank cache /dev/nvme10n1

# Add SLOG (ZIL) for synchronous write acceleration
zpool add tank log mirror /dev/nvme11n1 /dev/nvme12n1

# Enable compression (LZ4 is fast with ~2:1 ratio on many datasets)
zfs set compression=lz4 tank

# Enable checksumming (default: on, using fletcher4 or sha256)
zfs set checksum=sha256 tank

# Create datasets (filesystems)
zfs create -o recordsize=1M tank/training-data     # Large block for sequential I/O
zfs create -o recordsize=16K tank/metadata          # Small block for metadata
zfs create -o recordsize=128K tank/checkpoints

# Snapshots (instantaneous, copy-on-write)
zfs snapshot tank/checkpoints@epoch-5000
zfs list -t snapshot

# Send snapshot to remote for backup (incremental)
zfs send -i tank/checkpoints@epoch-4000 tank/checkpoints@epoch-5000 | \
  ssh backup-server zfs recv tank-backup/checkpoints

# ARC (Adaptive Replacement Cache) tuning
echo 68719476736 > /sys/module/zfs/parameters/zfs_arc_max  # 64GB ARC

BlueField DPU for Storage Offload

NVIDIA BlueField DPU: Storage Acceleration

The NVIDIA BlueField-3 DPU (Data Processing Unit) integrates an ARM CPU complex, ConnectX-7 network adapter, and hardware accelerators onto a single SmartNIC. For storage, it offloads:

  • NVMe-oF Target: The DPU can present local NVMe devices as NVMe-oF targets without consuming host CPU cycles. NVIDIA SNAP (Software-defined Network Accelerated Processing) exposes NVMe namespaces as virtio-blk devices to VMs/containers.
  • Encryption: AES-XTS hardware acceleration for at-rest and in-flight storage encryption at line rate (400 Gb/s).
  • Compression: Hardware LZ4/Deflate compression engine for inline data reduction.
  • Erasure Coding: Hardware-accelerated Reed-Solomon encoding for distributed parity without host CPU overhead.
  • Storage virtualization: Multi-tenant NVMe namespace isolation with SR-IOV and hardware-enforced QoS.

Knowledge Check: Storage Architecture

Question 1

In a Lustre parallel file system, what is the primary role of the OSS (Object Storage Server), and how does file striping improve performance?

Correct answer: B. The OSS (Object Storage Server) is responsible for storing and serving file data through its OSTs (Object Storage Targets). When a file is striped with lfs setstripe -c 4, the file is divided into fixed-size chunks that are distributed round-robin across 4 different OSTs, potentially on 4 different OSS nodes. The Lustre client opens parallel connections to all relevant OSS servers simultaneously, achieving N-times the throughput of a single server (where N is the stripe count). This is the fundamental mechanism that allows Lustre to deliver hundreds of GB/s of aggregate bandwidth. The MDS (not the OSS) handles metadata, and striping is about parallelism, not redundancy or compression.

Question 2

What is the key advantage of NVMe-oF/RDMA over NVMe-oF/TCP for GPU cluster storage, and how does GPUDirect Storage (GDS) further optimize the data path?

Correct answer: A. NVMe-oF/RDMA adds less than 10 microseconds of latency to local NVMe access by using RDMA (Remote Direct Memory Access), which bypasses the kernel network stack entirely and performs zero-copy transfers directly between NIC and application buffers. NVMe/TCP, while more universally deployable over standard Ethernet, must traverse the full TCP/IP stack, adding 30–80 μs of latency. GPUDirect Storage takes this further by establishing a direct DMA path from the NVMe device (local or remote via NVMe-oF) to GPU HBM memory. Without GDS, data follows: NVMe → system DRAM (bounce buffer) → GPU HBM (two copies, consuming CPU cycles and memory bandwidth). With GDS: NVMe → GPU HBM (one transfer, zero CPU involvement).

Question 3

A team is designing storage for a 512-GPU training cluster. Training data is 2 PB, and checkpoints of 800 GB are written every 20 minutes. They need at least 400 GB/s sustained read throughput and POSIX semantics. The data should automatically tier to S3 when cold. Which storage architecture is most appropriate?

Correct answer: B. WekaFS is the best fit for this workload profile. The requirements include: (1) 400 GB/s sustained throughput, which WekaFS can achieve with linear scaling across NVMe-backed nodes; (2) POSIX semantics, which WekaFS fully supports; (3) automatic S3 tiering, which is a native built-in feature of WekaFS (not a bolt-on script); and (4) the scale of 512 GPUs with 2 PB of data and frequent large checkpoint writes. Option A (pNFS) cannot realistically deliver 400 GB/s and has no native S3 tiering. Option C (MinIO) does not provide POSIX semantics, which are required for most ML training frameworks that use standard file I/O. Option D (CephFS) could technically work but would struggle to consistently deliver 400 GB/s at the required latency — CephFS is not optimized for GPU/HPC workloads at this scale, and 3x replication for 2 PB would require 6 PB of raw capacity. WekaFS with its kernel-bypass client, flash-optimized architecture, and native tiering is purpose-built for exactly this use case.

08

Orchestration & Cluster Management

Kubernetes, GPU Operators, RDMA Networking, and Job Scheduling for GPU Superpods

1. Kubernetes for GPU Clusters

Why Kubernetes for HPC / GPU Infrastructure?

Kubernetes has become the dominant orchestration platform for GPU clusters because it solves three fundamental challenges at scale: scheduling (placing workloads on the right hardware), scaling (elastically growing from 1 to 1,000+ nodes), and multi-tenancy (isolating teams sharing expensive GPU resources). In a Superpod environment with 32+ DGX nodes each containing 8 GPUs, manual placement of distributed training jobs is impractical. Kubernetes automates this while respecting hardware topology constraints critical for NCCL collective communication performance.

Unlike traditional HPC schedulers (Slurm, PBS), Kubernetes provides a declarative API, a rich ecosystem of operators and controllers, container-native isolation, and seamless integration with CI/CD pipelines. Many organizations run Kubernetes alongside Slurm, using K8s for inference/serving and interactive workloads, and Slurm for batch training. However, with the maturation of gang scheduling (Volcano, Kueue) and training operators (Kubeflow), Kubernetes is increasingly used for large-scale training as well.

GPU-Aware Scheduling

At the core of GPU scheduling in Kubernetes is the Extended Resource mechanism. GPUs are advertised by the NVIDIA Device Plugin as nvidia.com/gpu resources on each node. Pods request GPUs in their resource spec, and the Kubernetes scheduler only places pods on nodes with sufficient available GPUs. Unlike CPU and memory, GPU resources are whole-number only and cannot be overcommitted — if a node has 8 GPUs and 8 are allocated, no more GPU pods can be scheduled there. 

# Pod requesting all 8 GPUs on a DGX H100 node
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: training-worker-0
  labels:
    app: llm-training
    role: worker
spec:
  containers:
  - name: pytorch-trainer
    image: nvcr.io/nvidia/pytorch:24.01-py3
    resources:
      requests:
        cpu: "64"
        memory: "512Gi"
        nvidia.com/gpu: 8          # Request all 8 H100 GPUs
      limits:
        cpu: "128"
        memory: "1024Gi"
        nvidia.com/gpu: 8          # Limits must equal requests for GPUs
    env:
    - name: NCCL_DEBUG
      value: "INFO"
    - name: NCCL_IB_HCA
      value: "mlx5"               # Use InfiniBand HCAs for NCCL
    - name: NCCL_TOPO_DUMP_FILE
      value: "/var/run/nvidia/topo.xml"
    volumeMounts:
    - name: shm
      mountPath: /dev/shm
  volumes:
  - name: shm
    emptyDir:
      medium: Memory
      sizeLimit: "128Gi"           # Large shared memory for NCCL
Topology-Aware Scheduling

For distributed training with NCCL, pod placement matters enormously. Two pods on the same NVSwitch-connected DGX node communicate at 900 GB/s (NVLink). Two pods on different nodes in the same leaf switch communicate at 400 Gb/s (InfiniBand). Two pods crossing spine switches see higher latency and potential congestion. The scheduler must be topology-aware to co-locate pods in the same rack or pod (network pod) whenever possible. This is achieved via topology spread constraints and pod affinity rules, combined with node labels that encode the physical topology (rack, row, network pod).

Node Labels and Taints for GPU Types

GPU Feature Discovery (GFD), a component of the NVIDIA GPU Operator, automatically labels nodes with detailed GPU information. These labels enable precise scheduling decisions:

Label Key Example Value Purpose
nvidia.com/gpu.product NVIDIA-H100-SXM5-80GB GPU model identification for nodeSelector
nvidia.com/gpu.count 8 Number of GPUs on the node
nvidia.com/gpu.memory 81920 GPU memory in MiB per GPU
nvidia.com/cuda.driver.major 535 CUDA driver major version
nvidia.com/cuda.runtime.major 12 CUDA runtime major version
nvidia.com/gpu.compute.major 9 Compute capability (9.0 for H100)
nvidia.com/mig.capable true Whether the GPU supports MIG
nvidia.com/gpu.family hopper GPU architecture family 
# Apply taints to reserve GPU nodes for GPU workloads only
kubectl taint nodes dgx-node-01 nvidia.com/gpu=present:NoSchedule

# Only pods with the matching toleration can be scheduled on GPU nodes
# This prevents CPU-only workloads from consuming GPU node resources

# Label nodes with rack/topology information for topology-aware scheduling
kubectl label nodes dgx-node-01 topology.kubernetes.io/rack=rack-01
kubectl label nodes dgx-node-01 topology.kubernetes.io/zone=pod-a
kubectl label nodes dgx-node-01 nvidia.com/gpu.product=NVIDIA-H100-SXM5-80GB

Pod Scheduling Strategies

nodeSelector is the simplest constraint — a map of key-value label pairs that the target node must match. nodeAffinity provides richer expressions including In, NotIn, Exists, Gt, Lt operators, and supports both required (hard) and preferred (soft) rules. 

affinity:
  nodeAffinity:
    requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
      nodeSelectorTerms:
      - matchExpressions:
        - key: nvidia.com/gpu.product
          operator: In
          values:
          - "NVIDIA-H100-SXM5-80GB"
          - "NVIDIA-H200-SXM-141GB"
        - key: nvidia.com/gpu.count
          operator: Gt
          values:
          - "4"                    # Nodes with more than 4 GPUs
    preferredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
    - weight: 100
      preference:
        matchExpressions:
        - key: topology.kubernetes.io/rack
          operator: In
          values:
          - "rack-01"              # Prefer rack-01 (soft)

Pod affinity co-locates pods relative to other pods. For distributed training, you want all workers of the same job on nodes in the same rack (same topologyKey). Pod anti-affinity spreads replicas across failure domains for high availability. 

affinity:
  podAffinity:
    requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
    - labelSelector:
        matchExpressions:
        - key: training-job
          operator: In
          values:
          - "llm-gpt-run-042"
      topologyKey: topology.kubernetes.io/rack  # Same rack
  podAntiAffinity:
    preferredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
    - weight: 50
      podAffinityTerm:
        labelSelector:
          matchExpressions:
          - key: app
            operator: In
            values:
            - "inference-server"
        topologyKey: kubernetes.io/hostname  # Different nodes

Topology spread constraints distribute pods evenly across topology domains. This is useful for spreading inference replicas across racks for fault tolerance, or concentrating training workers within a network pod for NCCL performance. 

topologySpreadConstraints:
- maxSkew: 1                      # Max difference in pod count across domains
  topologyKey: topology.kubernetes.io/rack
  whenUnsatisfiable: DoNotSchedule  # Hard constraint
  labelSelector:
    matchLabels:
      app: inference-triton
- maxSkew: 2
  topologyKey: topology.kubernetes.io/zone
  whenUnsatisfiable: ScheduleAnyway  # Soft constraint (best-effort)
  labelSelector:
    matchLabels:
      app: inference-triton

2. NVIDIA GPU Operator

Automating the GPU Software Stack

The NVIDIA GPU Operator uses the Kubernetes operator framework to automate the management of all NVIDIA software components needed for GPU provisioning. Instead of manually installing drivers, container toolkits, and device plugins on every node, the GPU Operator deploys and manages these as containerized workloads via DaemonSets. When a new GPU node joins the cluster, the operator automatically installs the full stack — from kernel-mode driver to monitoring exporters — without any manual intervention. This is essential in cloud-native environments where nodes are ephemeral and auto-scaled.

Components Deployed by the GPU Operator

Component DaemonSet Name Function
NVIDIA Driver nvidia-driver-daemonset Container-based driver installation. Compiles and loads the kernel module (nvidia.ko, nvidia-uvm.ko, nvidia-modeset.ko) on the host. Supports pre-compiled drivers for specific kernel versions to avoid compilation overhead. Version is pinned in ClusterPolicy (e.g., 535.129.03).
NVIDIA Container Toolkit nvidia-container-toolkit-daemonset Formerly nvidia-docker2. Installs the nvidia-container-runtime and nvidia-container-cli on the host. Configures containerd to use the NVIDIA runtime as a handler for GPU containers. Handles injecting GPU device nodes (/dev/nvidia*) and driver libraries into containers.
NVIDIA Device Plugin nvidia-device-plugin-daemonset Implements the Kubernetes Device Plugin API. Discovers GPUs on the node and advertises them to the kubelet as nvidia.com/gpu extended resources. Handles GPU allocation, health checking, and device topology reporting. Supports time-slicing for GPU sharing and MIG device advertisement.
DCGM Exporter nvidia-dcgm-exporter Exports GPU metrics via the NVIDIA Data Center GPU Manager (DCGM) in Prometheus format. Metrics include GPU utilization, memory usage, temperature, power draw, ECC errors, NVLink throughput, PCIe throughput, and SM clock frequencies. Default scrape endpoint: :9400/metrics.
GPU Feature Discovery nvidia-gpu-feature-discovery Labels nodes with GPU properties (product name, driver version, CUDA version, compute capability, MIG capability, GPU family). These labels power nodeSelector and nodeAffinity scheduling decisions. Re-labels periodically to detect hardware changes.
MIG Manager nvidia-mig-manager Manages Multi-Instance GPU (MIG) partitioning on A100/H100 GPUs. Applies MIG profiles declaratively via ConfigMaps. Can reconfigure MIG partitions by draining pods, applying the new profile, and uncordoning the node. Supports per-node MIG configurations.
DCGM (standalone) nvidia-dcgm Runs the DCGM daemon for deep GPU health monitoring, diagnostics, and policy-based management. Provides GPU health watches, job statistics, and active health checks (memory stress tests, PCIe bandwidth tests). Used by DCGM Exporter as a data source.
Node Status Exporter nvidia-operator-validator Validates that all GPU operator components are functioning correctly. Runs post-install validation (can the container see GPUs? Are drivers loaded?). Reports node readiness status back to the operator.

ClusterPolicy CRD

The GPU Operator is configured through a single ClusterPolicy Custom Resource. This CRD defines the desired state of every component — image versions, runtime configs, feature flags, and node selectors: 

apiVersion: nvidia.com/v1
kind: ClusterPolicy
metadata:
  name: cluster-policy
spec:
  operator:
    defaultRuntime: containerd
    initContainer:
      image: nvcr.io/nvidia/cuda
      version: "12.3.1-base-ubi8"

  driver:
    enabled: true
    version: "535.129.03"
    repository: nvcr.io/nvidia
    image: driver
    rdma:
      enabled: true                 # Enable GPUDirect RDMA kernel module
      useHostMofed: true            # Use host-installed MLNX_OFED
    licensingConfig:
      nlsEnabled: false
    upgradePolicy:
      autoUpgrade: true
      maxParallelUpgrades: 1        # Rolling driver upgrades
      drain:
        enabled: true
        force: true
        timeoutSeconds: 300

  toolkit:
    enabled: true
    version: "v1.14.3-ubuntu20.04"

  devicePlugin:
    enabled: true
    version: "v0.14.3"
    config:
      name: device-plugin-config
      default: "all-gpus"           # Default config map entry

  dcgmExporter:
    enabled: true
    version: "3.3.0-3.2.0-ubuntu22.04"
    config:
      name: "dcgm-metrics-config"   # Custom metrics config map

  dcgm:
    enabled: true
    version: "3.3.0-1-ubuntu22.04"

  gfd:
    enabled: true
    version: "v0.8.2"

  migManager:
    enabled: true
    version: "v0.6.0"
    config:
      name: mig-config               # MIG partitioning profiles

  nodeStatusExporter:
    enabled: true

  sandboxDevicePlugin:
    enabled: false                    # For vGPU environments

Installation via Helm

# Add the NVIDIA Helm repository
helm repo add nvidia https://helm.ngc.nvidia.com/nvidia
helm repo update

# Install the GPU Operator
helm install gpu-operator nvidia/gpu-operator \
  --namespace gpu-operator \
  --create-namespace \
  --version v23.9.1 \
  --set driver.version=535.129.03 \
  --set driver.rdma.enabled=true \
  --set driver.rdma.useHostMofed=true \
  --set toolkit.version=v1.14.3-ubuntu20.04 \
  --set devicePlugin.config.name=device-plugin-config \
  --set dcgmExporter.config.name=dcgm-metrics-config \
  --set migManager.enabled=true

# Verify operator pods are running
kubectl -n gpu-operator get pods
# NAME                                                  READY   STATUS
# gpu-operator-xxxxx                                    1/1     Running
# nvidia-container-toolkit-daemonset-xxxxx              1/1     Running
# nvidia-cuda-validator-xxxxx                           0/1     Completed
# nvidia-dcgm-exporter-xxxxx                            1/1     Running
# nvidia-device-plugin-daemonset-xxxxx                  1/1     Running
# nvidia-driver-daemonset-xxxxx                         1/1     Running
# nvidia-gpu-feature-discovery-xxxxx                    1/1     Running
# nvidia-operator-validator-xxxxx                       1/1     Running

# Verify GPUs are visible
kubectl describe node dgx-node-01 | grep nvidia.com/gpu
#   nvidia.com/gpu:     8
#   nvidia.com/gpu:     8

3. Network Operator & RDMA in Kubernetes

NVIDIA Network Operator

The NVIDIA Network Operator complements the GPU Operator by managing the high-performance networking stack. In a Superpod, each DGX node has 8 ConnectX-7 NICs (400 Gb/s each) providing 3.2 Tb/s of aggregate InfiniBand or RoCE bandwidth for NCCL collective communication. The Network Operator automates the deployment of MLNX_OFED drivers, SR-IOV configuration, RDMA device plugins, and secondary CNI configuration — ensuring that every GPU pod can access RDMA-capable network interfaces for GPUDirect RDMA data paths.

Multus CNI: Multiple Network Interfaces per Pod

Standard Kubernetes pods get a single network interface from the cluster CNI. For GPU workloads, this is insufficient — you need both a management network (for K8s API, logging, metrics) and high-performance data-plane networks (for NCCL traffic over RDMA). Multus CNI is a meta-CNI plugin that attaches multiple network interfaces to a single pod:

  • Primary interface (eth0) — Provided by the cluster CNI (Calico or Cilium). Carries management traffic, pod-to-pod communication, service discovery, and DNS.
  • Secondary interfaces (net1..netN) — Provided by SR-IOV CNI or host-device CNI. Each interface maps to a ConnectX-7 VF (Virtual Function) or PF (Physical Function) and supports RDMA operations. Typically one per GPU for optimal NCCL topology.
# NetworkAttachmentDefinition for SR-IOV RDMA network
apiVersion: k8s.cni.cncf.io/v1
kind: NetworkAttachmentDefinition
metadata:
  name: sriov-rdma-network
  namespace: gpu-workloads
  annotations:
    k8s.v1.cni.cncf.io/resourceName: nvidia.com/rdma_shared_device
spec:
  config: |
    {
      "cniVersion": "0.3.1",
      "name": "sriov-rdma",
      "type": "sriov",
      "vlan": 0,
      "spoofchk": "off",
      "trust": "on",
      "rdma": {
        "enabled": true
      },
      "ipam": {
        "type": "whereabouts",
        "range": "192.168.100.0/24",
        "exclude": ["192.168.100.0/32", "192.168.100.255/32"]
      }
    }

SR-IOV Device Plugin & RDMA Shared Device Plugin

The SR-IOV Device Plugin discovers SR-IOV Virtual Functions on ConnectX NICs and exposes them as schedulable Kubernetes resources (e.g., nvidia.com/sriov_rdma_vf). The RDMA Shared Device Plugin exposes RDMA-capable devices as nvidia.com/rdma_shared_device, allowing multiple pods on the same node to share RDMA resources. The "shared" mode is typically used because each DGX node runs a single training pod consuming all 8 GPUs and all 8 NICs. 

# Complete pod spec with GPU + RDMA multi-network
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: gpu-rdma-worker
  namespace: gpu-workloads
  annotations:
    k8s.v1.cni.cncf.io/networks: |
      [
        {"name": "sriov-rdma-network", "interface": "net1"},
        {"name": "sriov-rdma-network", "interface": "net2"},
        {"name": "sriov-rdma-network", "interface": "net3"},
        {"name": "sriov-rdma-network", "interface": "net4"},
        {"name": "sriov-rdma-network", "interface": "net5"},
        {"name": "sriov-rdma-network", "interface": "net6"},
        {"name": "sriov-rdma-network", "interface": "net7"},
        {"name": "sriov-rdma-network", "interface": "net8"}
      ]
spec:
  tolerations:
  - key: nvidia.com/gpu
    operator: Exists
    effect: NoSchedule
  containers:
  - name: gpu-worker
    image: nvcr.io/nvidia/pytorch:24.01-py3
    securityContext:
      capabilities:
        add: ["IPC_LOCK"]          # Required for RDMA memory registration
    resources:
      requests:
        cpu: "64"
        memory: "512Gi"
        nvidia.com/gpu: 8
        nvidia.com/rdma_shared_device: 8
      limits:
        cpu: "128"
        memory: "1024Gi"
        nvidia.com/gpu: 8
        nvidia.com/rdma_shared_device: 8
    env:
    - name: NCCL_IB_HCA
      value: "=mlx5_0,mlx5_1,mlx5_2,mlx5_3,mlx5_4,mlx5_5,mlx5_6,mlx5_7"
    - name: NCCL_IB_GID_INDEX
      value: "3"                   # RoCEv2 GID index
    - name: NCCL_NET_GDR_LEVEL
      value: "PIX"                 # GPUDirect RDMA: same PCIe switch
    volumeMounts:
    - name: shm
      mountPath: /dev/shm
  volumes:
  - name: shm
    emptyDir:
      medium: Memory
      sizeLimit: "128Gi"

Pod Multi-Network Architecture: Primary (Calico) + Secondary (SR-IOV RDMA)

4. Job Scheduling for Distributed Training

The Gang Scheduling Problem

Distributed training jobs require all their workers to start simultaneously. A 256-GPU training job (32 nodes x 8 GPUs) using data-parallel + tensor-parallel strategies cannot make progress if only 31 of 32 workers are running — NCCL initialization hangs waiting for all ranks. Standard Kubernetes scheduling places pods independently, which can lead to deadlock: two jobs each get half their pods scheduled, but neither can complete because the remaining pods are blocked by the other job. Gang scheduling solves this by treating all pods in a job as an atomic unit — either all pods are placed, or none are.

Volcano Scheduler

Volcano is a CNCF project that provides batch scheduling for Kubernetes. It introduces PodGroup and Queue CRDs, implements gang scheduling, fair-share, priority, and backfill policies. It replaces (or supplements) the default kube-scheduler for batch/HPC workloads: 

# Volcano Job for distributed training
apiVersion: batch.volcano.sh/v1alpha1
kind: Job
metadata:
  name: llm-training-gpt4
spec:
  minAvailable: 32                  # Gang scheduling: all 32 tasks required
  schedulerName: volcano
  queue: high-priority-training
  policies:
  - event: PodEvicted
    action: RestartJob              # Restart entire job if any pod evicted
  - event: TaskCompleted
    action: CompleteJob
  plugins:
    sla:
    - --max-waiting-time=30m        # Fail if not scheduled in 30 minutes
  tasks:
  - replicas: 32
    name: worker
    template:
      spec:
        containers:
        - name: pytorch
          image: nvcr.io/nvidia/pytorch:24.01-py3
          command: ["torchrun"]
          args:
          - "--nproc_per_node=8"
          - "--nnodes=32"
          - "--rdzv_backend=c10d"
          - "--rdzv_endpoint=$(MASTER_ADDR):29500"
          - "train.py"
          resources:
            limits:
              nvidia.com/gpu: 8
              nvidia.com/rdma_shared_device: 8
          volumeMounts:
          - name: shm
            mountPath: /dev/shm
          - name: training-data
            mountPath: /data
        volumes:
        - name: shm
          emptyDir:
            medium: Memory
            sizeLimit: "128Gi"
        - name: training-data
          persistentVolumeClaim:
            claimName: lustre-training-data

Kueue: K8s-Native Job Queueing

Kueue is a Kubernetes-native job queueing system (SIG-scheduling project) designed for multi-tenant GPU clusters. Unlike Volcano (which provides its own scheduler), Kueue works alongside the default scheduler by controlling when workloads are admitted to the cluster. It provides:

  • ClusterQueues — Define resource pools (e.g., 256 GPUs for team-A, 128 for team-B)
  • LocalQueues — Namespace-scoped queues that map to ClusterQueues
  • ResourceFlavors — Differentiate GPU types (H100 vs A100) within a queue
  • Cohorts — Allow queues to borrow unused resources from peer queues
  • Preemption — Lower-priority workloads can be preempted when higher-priority work arrives
# Kueue ClusterQueue with resource flavors and borrowing
apiVersion: kueue.x-k8s.io/v1beta1
kind: ClusterQueue
metadata:
  name: team-research
spec:
  namespaceSelector: {}
  queueingStrategy: BestEffortFIFO
  preemption:
    reclaimWithinCohort: Any
    borrowWithinCohort:
      policy: LowerPriority
      maxPriorityThreshold: 100
  resourceGroups:
  - coveredResources: ["cpu", "memory", "nvidia.com/gpu"]
    flavors:
    - name: h100-sxm
      resources:
      - name: "nvidia.com/gpu"
        nominalQuota: 256            # Team's guaranteed 256 GPUs
        borrowingLimit: 128          # Can borrow up to 128 more from cohort
      - name: "cpu"
        nominalQuota: 2048
      - name: "memory"
        nominalQuota: "16Ti"
  cohort: research-cohort            # Share unused GPUs with peer queues
---
apiVersion: kueue.x-k8s.io/v1beta1
kind: LocalQueue
metadata:
  name: training-queue
  namespace: team-research
spec:
  clusterQueue: team-research

Kubeflow Training Operator: PyTorchJob

The Kubeflow Training Operator provides high-level CRDs for distributed training frameworks. PyTorchJob automates the setup of distributed PyTorch training — configuring MASTER_ADDR, MASTER_PORT, WORLD_SIZE, and RANK environment variables, and managing the lifecycle of master and worker pods: 

# Full PyTorchJob for large-scale LLM training
apiVersion: kubeflow.org/v1
kind: PyTorchJob
metadata:
  name: llm-training-70b
  namespace: team-research
  labels:
    kueue.x-k8s.io/queue-name: training-queue
spec:
  elasticPolicy:
    minReplicas: 16
    maxReplicas: 32
    rdzvBackend: c10d
    rdzvPort: 29500
    maxRestarts: 3
  pytorchReplicaSpecs:
    Master:
      replicas: 1
      restartPolicy: OnFailure
      template:
        metadata:
          annotations:
            k8s.v1.cni.cncf.io/networks: sriov-rdma-network
        spec:
          tolerations:
          - key: nvidia.com/gpu
            operator: Exists

            nvidia.com/gpu.product: "NVIDIA-H100-SXM5-80GB"

            - "--nproc_per_node=8"
            - "--nnodes=32"

    Worker:
      replicas: 31
      restartPolicy: OnFailure
      template:
        metadata:
          annotations:
            k8s.v1.cni.cncf.io/networks: sriov-rdma-network
        spec:
          tolerations:
          - key: nvidia.com/gpu
            operator: Exists
            effect: NoSchedule
          nodeSelector:
            nvidia.com/gpu.product: "NVIDIA-H100-SXM5-80GB"
          containers:
          - name: pytorch
            image: nvcr.io/nvidia/pytorch:24.01-py3
            command: ["torchrun"]
            args:
            - "--nproc_per_node=8"
            - "--nnodes=32"
            - "--rdzv_backend=c10d"
            - "--rdzv_endpoint=$(MASTER_ADDR):$(MASTER_PORT)"
            - "/workspace/train_llm.py"
            - "--model=llama-70b"
            - "--batch-size=2"
            - "--gradient-accumulation-steps=8"
            - "--fp8-training"
            - "--tensor-parallel-size=8"
            - "--pipeline-parallel-size=4"
            - "--data-parallel-size=8"
            resources:
              limits:
                nvidia.com/gpu: 8
                nvidia.com/rdma_shared_device: 8

            - name: NCCL_DEBUG

Priority, Preemption & Resource Quotas

# PriorityClasses for GPU workload tiers
apiVersion: scheduling.k8s.io/v1
kind: PriorityClass
metadata:
  name: critical-training
value: 1000000
globalDefault: false
preemptionPolicy: PreemptLowerPriority
description: "Mission-critical training runs (can preempt all lower)"
---
apiVersion: scheduling.k8s.io/v1
kind: PriorityClass
metadata:
  name: standard-training
value: 100000
globalDefault: false
preemptionPolicy: PreemptLowerPriority
description: "Standard training jobs"
---
apiVersion: scheduling.k8s.io/v1
kind: PriorityClass
metadata:
  name: batch-inference
value: 10000
globalDefault: false
preemptionPolicy: PreemptLowerPriority
description: "Batch inference (preemptable by training)"
---
apiVersion: scheduling.k8s.io/v1
kind: PriorityClass
metadata:
  name: dev-interactive
value: 1000
globalDefault: true
preemptionPolicy: Never
description: "Interactive dev (lowest, non-preempting)"
# ResourceQuota for team namespace
apiVersion: v1
kind: ResourceQuota
metadata:
  name: gpu-quota
  namespace: team-research
spec:
  hard:
    requests.nvidia.com/gpu: "256"    # Max 256 GPUs for this team
    requests.cpu: "4096"
    requests.memory: "32Ti"
    persistentvolumeclaims: "50"
    pods: "500"
  scopeSelector:
    matchExpressions:
    - scopeName: PriorityClass
      operator: In
      values:
      - critical-training
      - standard-training
---
# Separate quota for low-priority/dev workloads
apiVersion: v1
kind: ResourceQuota
metadata:
  name: dev-quota
  namespace: team-research
spec:
  hard:
    requests.nvidia.com/gpu: "32"     # Only 32 GPUs for dev/interactive
    pods: "100"
  scopeSelector:
    matchExpressions:
    - scopeName: PriorityClass
      operator: In
      values:
      - dev-interactive
      - batch-inference

5. Container Runtime for GPUs

How GPU Access Works in Containers

GPU containers in Kubernetes rely on a chain of components that bridge the gap between the Kubernetes resource model and the Linux device layer. The process works as follows:

  1. Pod requests nvidia.com/gpu: N — The Kubernetes scheduler finds a node with N available GPUs (tracked by the NVIDIA Device Plugin). The pod is bound to that node.
  2. Device Plugin allocates GPUs — The kubelet calls the NVIDIA Device Plugin's Allocate() RPC. The device plugin returns the specific GPU device IDs (e.g., GPU-a1b2c3d4) and the environment variable NVIDIA_VISIBLE_DEVICES=0,1,2,3,4,5,6,7.
  3. nvidia-container-runtime hook fires — When containerd creates the container, the NVIDIA container runtime (configured as a runtime handler in containerd) intercepts the OCI spec. The nvidia-container-runtime-hook (now called nvidia-container-toolkit) modifies the container spec to:
    • Bind-mount GPU device files: /dev/nvidia0../dev/nvidia7, /dev/nvidiactl, /dev/nvidia-uvm, /dev/nvidia-uvm-tools
    • Mount NVIDIA driver libraries from the host into the container (libcuda.so, libnvidia-ml.so, etc.)
    • Set NVIDIA_VISIBLE_DEVICES and NVIDIA_DRIVER_CAPABILITIES environment variables
  4. Container sees GPUs — Inside the container, nvidia-smi shows the allocated GPUs, CUDA libraries work transparently, and GPU workloads run as if on bare metal. 
# containerd configuration (/etc/containerd/config.toml)
# The NVIDIA container runtime is registered as a handler

[plugins."io.containerd.grpc.v1.cri".containerd]
  default_runtime_name = "nvidia"

[plugins."io.containerd.grpc.v1.cri".containerd.runtimes.nvidia]
  runtime_type = "io.containerd.runc.v2"
  [plugins."io.containerd.grpc.v1.cri".containerd.runtimes.nvidia.options]
    BinaryName = "/usr/bin/nvidia-container-runtime"

# Verify GPU access inside a container
kubectl run gpu-test --rm -it --image=nvcr.io/nvidia/cuda:12.3.1-base-ubuntu22.04 \
  --limits=nvidia.com/gpu=1 -- nvidia-smi

# Expected output:
# +-----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------+
# | NVIDIA-SMI 535.129.03   Driver Version: 535.129.03   CUDA Version: 12.3                 |
# |--------------------------------------------+------------------------+-------------------+
# | GPU  Name                 Persistence-M     | Bus-Id          Disp.A | Volatile Uncorr. ECC |
# | Fan  Temp   Perf          Pwr:Usage/Cap     |           Memory-Usage | GPU-Util  Compute M. |
# |==============================================+========================+===================|
# |   0  NVIDIA H100 80GB HBM3           On     | 00000000:1B:00.0  Off  |                   0 |
# | N/A   32C    P0              72W / 700W      |       0MiB / 81559MiB |      0%      Default |
# +--------------------------------------------+------------------------+-------------------+
CDI: Container Device Interface

CDI is a newer standard (part of the CNCF container ecosystem) that provides a vendor-neutral, declarative way to make devices available to containers. Instead of runtime hooks that modify the OCI spec at launch time, CDI uses JSON specification files (e.g., /etc/cdi/nvidia.yaml) that describe which device nodes, mounts, and environment variables should be injected. CDI is supported by containerd 1.7+, CRI-O 1.27+, and Podman 4.1+. The NVIDIA Container Toolkit generates CDI specs via nvidia-ctk cdi generate. CDI is the future direction for device exposure in containers, replacing the hook-based approach. 

# Generate CDI spec for NVIDIA GPUs
nvidia-ctk cdi generate --output=/etc/cdi/nvidia.yaml

# CDI spec structure (simplified)
# cdiVersion: "0.6.0"
# kind: "nvidia.com/gpu"
# devices:
# - name: "0"
#   containerEdits:
#     deviceNodes:
#     - path: /dev/nvidia0
#       hostPath: /dev/nvidia0
#     mounts:
#     - hostPath: /usr/lib/x86_64-linux-gnu/libcuda.so.535.129.03
#       containerPath: /usr/lib/x86_64-linux-gnu/libcuda.so.1
#     env:
#     - NVIDIA_VISIBLE_DEVICES=0

6. Service Mesh & Networking in Kubernetes

The management-plane network in a GPU cluster handles API server communication, pod-to-pod connectivity for non-RDMA traffic, service discovery, ingress, and observability. While the RDMA data plane carries the heavy NCCL traffic, the management network must still be reliable and performant.

Component Options Notes for GPU Clusters
CNI Plugin Calico (BGP-based, mature, NetworkPolicy support)
Cilium (eBPF-based, high performance, L7 policies) 		Calico is most common in on-prem HPC. Uses BGP peering with ToR switches for native pod routing without overlay (VXLAN overhead avoided). Cilium offers eBPF datapath for lower latency and kernel-bypass networking. Both support NetworkPolicy.
NetworkPolicy Microsegmentation rules per namespace/pod Isolate team namespaces. Allow GPU pods to communicate within a training job (same labels) but block cross-team traffic. Example: allow all traffic within app=llm-training-042 pods, deny ingress from other namespaces.
Load Balancer MetalLB (bare-metal LB using L2/BGP) Bare-metal clusters lack cloud LB integration. MetalLB assigns external IPs from a configured pool and announces them via BGP or ARP/NDP. Used for exposing inference endpoints, Jupyter notebooks, and monitoring dashboards.
Ingress Controller NGINX Ingress Controller
Istio Gateway 		Routes external HTTP/gRPC traffic to services. For Triton Inference Server, the ingress terminates TLS and routes to the correct model endpoint. Istio adds mTLS, traffic splitting (canary deployments), and observability.
Service Types ClusterIP, NodePort, LoadBalancer ClusterIP: Default, internal-only. For inter-service communication.
NodePort: Exposes on static port on all nodes. For debugging.
LoadBalancer: External IP via MetalLB. For production inference APIs.
DNS CoreDNS Cluster-internal DNS for service discovery. Training pods discover the master via DNS (llm-training-master-0.default.svc.cluster.local). Ensure CoreDNS scales to handle large clusters (100+ nodes). 
# NetworkPolicy: allow intra-job NCCL traffic, deny cross-namespace
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: NetworkPolicy
metadata:
  name: allow-training-job-traffic
  namespace: team-research
spec:
  podSelector:
    matchLabels:
      app: llm-training
  policyTypes:
  - Ingress
  - Egress
  ingress:
  - from:
    - podSelector:
        matchLabels:
          app: llm-training
    ports:
    - protocol: TCP
      port: 29500               # PyTorch rendezvous port
    - protocol: TCP
      port: 29400               # NCCL socket port range
  egress:
  - to:
    - podSelector:
        matchLabels:
          app: llm-training
  - to:                         # Allow DNS resolution
    - namespaceSelector: {}
      podSelector:
        matchLabels:
          k8s-app: kube-dns
    ports:
    - protocol: UDP
      port: 53
  - to:                         # Allow access to storage
    - ipBlock:
        cidr: 10.0.0.0/8       # Lustre/GPFS storage network

7. Cluster Management at Scale

Day-0 through Day-2 Operations

Managing a Superpod with hundreds of nodes requires automation at every layer: from bare-metal provisioning (Day 0) through initial cluster setup (Day 1) to ongoing operations, upgrades, and scaling (Day 2). The toolchain spans hardware lifecycle management, OS provisioning, Kubernetes cluster creation, and GitOps-driven application deployment.

DGX nodes are provisioned via PXE (Preboot Execution Environment) boot. The process:

  1. Node powers on and BMC (Baseboard Management Controller) network boots via DHCP/TFTP
  2. PXE server delivers boot image (iPXE chain-loads to provisioning system)
  3. MAAS (Metal as a Service) or Foreman manages the full lifecycle:
    • Hardware discovery and commissioning (detect CPUs, GPUs, NICs, storage)
    • OS deployment (Ubuntu 22.04 LTS with NVIDIA-optimized kernel)
    • Network configuration (bonding, VLANs, IP assignment)
    • Post-deploy hooks for NVIDIA driver and MLNX_OFED installation
  4. Ansible takes over for post-OS configuration:
    • NVIDIA driver installation (or GPU Operator handles this)
    • MLNX_OFED installation and configuration
    • containerd installation and configuration
    • kubelet installation and cluster join
    • BIOS/firmware settings (IOMMU, SR-IOV, ACS, power profile)
# Ansible playbook excerpt for DGX node preparation
# ansible-playbook -i inventory/dgx-nodes.yaml site.yaml

# Example tasks/gpu-setup.yaml:
- name: Load NVIDIA kernel modules
  modprobe:
    name: "{{ item }}"
    state: present
  loop:
    - nvidia
    - nvidia_uvm
    - nvidia_modeset
    - nvidia_peermem          # GPUDirect RDMA peer memory module

- name: Enable persistent mode for all GPUs
  command: nvidia-smi -pm 1

- name: Set GPU clocks to max for training
  command: nvidia-smi -lgc 1980,1980
  # Lock GPU clocks to max for consistent training performance

- name: Configure hugepages for RDMA
  sysctl:
    name: vm.nr_hugepages
    value: "16384"            # 32GB of hugepages for RDMA registration
    sysctl_set: yes

- name: Enable SR-IOV on ConnectX-7 NICs
  command: >
    mlxconfig -d {{ item }} set
    SRIOV_EN=1
    NUM_OF_VFS=8
  loop: "{{ connectx7_devices }}"

Cluster API (CAPI) is a Kubernetes sub-project that uses declarative, Kubernetes-style APIs to create, configure, and manage Kubernetes clusters. For bare-metal GPU clusters, the CAPI BYOH (Bring Your Own Host) or CAPI Metal3 providers manage the lifecycle of worker nodes: 

# Cluster API Machine definition for DGX nodes
apiVersion: infrastructure.cluster.x-k8s.io/v1beta1
kind: Metal3Machine
metadata:
  name: dgx-worker-01
  namespace: metal3
spec:
  image:
    url: http://provision-server/images/ubuntu-22.04-dgx.qcow2
    checksum: http://provision-server/images/ubuntu-22.04-dgx.sha256
  hostSelector:
    matchLabels:
      machine-type: dgx-h100
      rack: rack-01
  dataTemplate:
    name: dgx-worker-template
---
apiVersion: cluster.x-k8s.io/v1beta1
kind: MachineDeployment
metadata:
  name: dgx-gpu-workers
spec:
  clusterName: superpod-cluster
  replicas: 32
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      nodepool: gpu-workers
  template:
    spec:
      clusterName: superpod-cluster
      version: "v1.28.4"
      bootstrap:
        configRef:
          apiVersion: bootstrap.cluster.x-k8s.io/v1beta1
          kind: KubeadmConfigTemplate
          name: dgx-worker-bootstrap
      infrastructureRef:
        apiVersion: infrastructure.cluster.x-k8s.io/v1beta1
        kind: Metal3MachineTemplate
        name: dgx-worker-template

ArgoCD implements GitOps for Kubernetes — the desired cluster state is declared in Git, and ArgoCD continuously reconciles the live state to match. For GPU clusters, this manages the deployment of operators, training jobs, inference services, monitoring stacks, and cluster configurations: 

# ArgoCD Application for GPU Operator
apiVersion: argoproj.io/v1alpha1
kind: Application
metadata:
  name: gpu-operator
  namespace: argocd
spec:
  project: infrastructure
  source:
    repoURL: https://helm.ngc.nvidia.com/nvidia
    chart: gpu-operator
    targetRevision: v23.9.1
    helm:
      values: |
        driver:
          version: "535.129.03"
          rdma:
            enabled: true
            useHostMofed: true
        devicePlugin:
          config:
            name: device-plugin-config
        dcgmExporter:
          config:
            name: dcgm-metrics-config
        migManager:
          enabled: true
  destination:
    server: https://kubernetes.default.svc
    namespace: gpu-operator
  syncPolicy:
    automated:
      prune: true
      selfHeal: true
    syncOptions:
    - CreateNamespace=true
    - ServerSideApply=true
---
# ArgoCD Application for monitoring stack
apiVersion: argoproj.io/v1alpha1
kind: Application
metadata:
  name: gpu-monitoring
  namespace: argocd
spec:
  project: observability
  source:
    repoURL: https://github.com/org/gpu-cluster-config.git
    path: monitoring/
    targetRevision: main
  destination:
    server: https://kubernetes.default.svc
    namespace: monitoring
  syncPolicy:
    automated:
      prune: true
      selfHeal: true

Helm packages Kubernetes manifests as charts with templated values, enabling parameterized deployments. Kustomize uses an overlay-based approach without templates, modifying base manifests through patches. In practice, many GPU cluster operators use both: Helm for third-party charts (GPU Operator, Prometheus, ArgoCD) and Kustomize for in-house workload definitions. 

# Repository structure for a GPU cluster managed via GitOps
# gpu-cluster-config/
# |-- base/
# |   |-- kustomization.yaml
# |   |-- namespaces.yaml
# |   |-- resource-quotas.yaml
# |   |-- priority-classes.yaml
# |   |-- network-policies.yaml
# |-- overlays/
# |   |-- production/
# |   |   |-- kustomization.yaml        # Production-specific patches
# |   |   |-- gpu-quota-patch.yaml       # 512 GPUs for prod
# |   |-- staging/
# |       |-- kustomization.yaml
# |       |-- gpu-quota-patch.yaml       # 64 GPUs for staging
# |-- helm-values/
# |   |-- gpu-operator-values.yaml
# |   |-- prometheus-values.yaml
# |   |-- volcano-values.yaml
# |-- apps/
#     |-- argocd-apps.yaml               # ArgoCD Application manifests

Kubernetes GPU Cluster Architecture

Orchestration Stack Summary

Layer Component Role
Container Runtime containerd + nvidia-container-runtime GPU device injection into containers via OCI hooks or CDI
Device Management NVIDIA GPU Operator (ClusterPolicy CRD) Automated driver, toolkit, device plugin, DCGM, GFD, MIG deployment
Network Management NVIDIA Network Operator + Multus + SR-IOV RDMA-capable secondary interfaces for NCCL, GPUDirect RDMA
Scheduling kube-scheduler + Volcano / Kueue Gang scheduling, fair-share queuing, priority/preemption
Training Jobs Kubeflow Training Operator (PyTorchJob) Declarative distributed training with auto-configured env vars
Networking (Mgmt) Calico/Cilium + MetalLB + NGINX Ingress Pod networking, load balancing, ingress routing, NetworkPolicy
Lifecycle Cluster API + ArgoCD + Ansible Bare-metal provisioning, K8s lifecycle, GitOps-driven config

Knowledge Check

Question 1: GPU Resource Scheduling

In a Kubernetes cluster with the NVIDIA Device Plugin, a pod spec requests nvidia.com/gpu: 4 in its resource limits. A DGX H100 node currently has 3 GPUs free (5 are allocated to other pods). What happens when the scheduler tries to place this pod?

Correct Answer: B. Extended resources like nvidia.com/gpu are integer-only and cannot be overcommitted. The pod stays Pending until a node has at least 4 allocatable GPUs. Unlike CPU and memory, GPUs have no overcommit or fractional scheduling (unless MIG or time-slicing is explicitly configured). Preemption (option D) only occurs if the pod has a higher PriorityClass than existing pods AND preemptionPolicy: PreemptLowerPriority is set — it is not automatic without these configurations.

Question 2: Gang Scheduling

A distributed PyTorchJob requires 32 workers, each needing 8 GPUs (256 total GPUs). The cluster currently has 240 free GPUs across 30 nodes. Without gang scheduling (using the default kube-scheduler), what problem is most likely to occur?

Correct Answer: C. This is the classic partial-scheduling deadlock problem that gang scheduling solves. The default kube-scheduler places pods independently — it schedules 30 of 32 worker pods on available nodes. These 30 workers start and call torch.distributed.init_process_group(), which blocks waiting for all 32 ranks to join. The 2 remaining pods stay Pending because there aren't enough GPUs. The result: 240 GPUs are locked up doing nothing. Gang schedulers like Volcano or Kueue enforce all-or-nothing: either all 32 pods can be placed, or none are admitted.

Question 3: GPU Operator Components

A new DGX H100 node is added to a Kubernetes cluster running the NVIDIA GPU Operator. Which sequence correctly describes what happens automatically?

Correct Answer: B. This is the core value of the GPU Operator — fully automated, zero-touch GPU stack provisioning. When a new node joins the cluster and matches the GPU Operator's node selector, each DaemonSet component rolls out in dependency order: the driver container first (it compiles the kernel module against the running kernel or uses a pre-compiled driver), then the toolkit configures the container runtime, then the device plugin exposes GPUs to the scheduler, GFD labels the node, and DCGM begins monitoring. The entire process is governed by the ClusterPolicy CRD and takes 2-5 minutes depending on whether drivers need compilation. Option D is partially true — you can configure the operator with driver.enabled: false if you pre-install drivers, but by default, the operator manages the full stack including drivers.

09

Monitoring & Site Reliability Engineering

Observability, incident response, and operational excellence for GPU-scale infrastructure

GPU Monitoring with NVIDIA DCGM

NVIDIA Data Center GPU Manager (DCGM) is the foundational monitoring framework for GPU clusters. It provides a unified API and CLI for health monitoring, diagnostics, policy management, and telemetry collection across hundreds or thousands of GPUs. DCGM runs as a persistent host-level daemon (nv-hostengine) and exposes metrics via dcgmi CLI, C/Python APIs, and the DCGM Exporter for Prometheus integration.

Core DCGM Architecture

The DCGM stack consists of the nv-hostengine daemon (runs on each node with GPUs), the dcgmi command-line interface, and optional DCGM Exporter (a lightweight Go binary that queries nv-hostengine and serves Prometheus-formatted metrics on port :9400/metrics). In Kubernetes, DCGM Exporter typically runs as a DaemonSet on every GPU node.

Essential dcgmi Commands

Command Purpose Typical Use
dcgmi discovery -l List all GPUs and their group IDs Initial inventory and topology verification
dcgmi diag -r 1 Quick diagnostic (deployment-level, ~30 seconds) Pre-job health check, PCIe bandwidth verification
dcgmi diag -r 2 Medium diagnostic (~2 minutes) Targeted stress test, memory checks
dcgmi diag -r 3 Long diagnostic (~15 minutes) Full stress test: memory, compute, PCIe, NVLink, power
dcgmi health -c -g 1 Check health of GPU group 1 Continuous health monitoring via scripts/cron
dcgmi stats -e -g 1 Enable stats recording for group Job-level GPU utilization profiling
dcgmi stats -v -g 1 View recorded stats Post-job analysis of GPU utilization
dcgmi policy -s -g 1 Set policy watches (ECC, thermal, power) Automated alerts when thresholds exceeded
dcgmi topo -g 1 Display GPU topology (NVLink, PCIe affinity) Validating NVLink mesh connectivity
dcgmi nvlink -s -g 1 Show NVLink status and error counts Diagnosing NVLink degradation

Key DCGM Metric Fields

Field ID Prometheus Name Description Alert Threshold
203 DCGM_FI_DEV_GPU_UTIL GPU SM (streaming multiprocessor) utilization % <10% during active job = possible issue
204 DCGM_FI_DEV_MEM_COPY_UTIL Memory controller utilization % Sustained 100% = memory bandwidth bottleneck
252 DCGM_FI_DEV_GPU_TEMP GPU core temperature (Celsius) >83 C = thermal throttle, >90 C = critical
150 DCGM_FI_DEV_POWER_USAGE Current power draw (watts) >TDP = power throttling engaged
310 DCGM_FI_DEV_ECC_SBE_VOL_TOTAL Single-bit ECC errors (volatile, correctable) >100/hour = degrading memory, monitor closely
313 DCGM_FI_DEV_ECC_DBE_VOL_TOTAL Double-bit ECC errors (volatile, uncorrectable) Any occurrence = fatal, drain node immediately
409 DCGM_FI_DEV_NVLINK_BANDWIDTH_TOTAL Aggregate NVLink bandwidth (MB/s) Significantly below expected = link degradation
410-425 DCGM_FI_DEV_NVLINK_CRC_FLIT_ERROR_COUNT_* NVLink CRC flit errors per link Any non-zero count = investigate cable/GPU
245 DCGM_FI_DEV_PCIE_REPLAY_COUNTER PCIe transaction replay count >100/hour = PCIe link degradation
230 DCGM_FI_DEV_XID_ERRORS Last XID error code reported by GPU Any non-zero = investigate (see XID table below)
254 DCGM_FI_DEV_MEM_TEMP HBM memory temperature (Celsius) >95 C on HBM3e = throttle imminent
1001 DCGM_FI_DEV_FB_FREE Free framebuffer (GPU memory) in MB Near 0 = OOM risk for new allocations
1002 DCGM_FI_DEV_FB_USED Used framebuffer (GPU memory) in MB Contextual; track per-job trends

DCGM Diagnostics Levels

Level 1 (Quick / Deployment): ~30 seconds. Validates GPU presence, PCIe bandwidth, basic NVLink connectivity. Run this before every job or as a node health gate in Slurm prolog scripts. Catches obvious hardware failures (dead GPU, broken PCIe link).
Level 2 (Medium / Validation): ~2 minutes. Adds targeted stress tests for GPU compute and memory subsystems. Suitable for post-maintenance validation or when a node returns from repair. Detects marginal memory errors and reduced compute throughput.
Level 3 (Long / Extended): ~15 minutes. Full burn-in: extended stress on every GPU subsystem including memory, compute SMs, NVLink bandwidth, PCIe bandwidth, and power delivery. Run during commissioning of new hardware, after GPU/NVLink cable replacement, or quarterly as preventive maintenance. This is the gold standard for confirming a node is fully operational.

DCGM Exporter Configuration Example

# Kubernetes DaemonSet for DCGM Exporter
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: DaemonSet
metadata:
  name: dcgm-exporter
  namespace: monitoring
  labels:
    app: dcgm-exporter
spec:
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: dcgm-exporter
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: dcgm-exporter
      annotations:
        prometheus.io/scrape: "true"
        prometheus.io/port: "9400"
        prometheus.io/path: "/metrics"
    spec:
      nodeSelector:
        nvidia.com/gpu.present: "true"
      tolerations:
        - key: nvidia.com/gpu
          operator: Exists
          effect: NoSchedule
      containers:
        - name: dcgm-exporter
          image: nvcr.io/nvidia/k8s/dcgm-exporter:3.3.5-3.4.1-ubuntu22.04
          ports:
            - name: metrics
              containerPort: 9400
          env:
            - name: DCGM_EXPORTER_LISTEN
              value: ":9400"
            - name: DCGM_EXPORTER_KUBERNETES
              value: "true"
            - name: DCGM_EXPORTER_COLLECTORS
              value: "/etc/dcgm-exporter/dcp-metrics-included.csv"
          volumeMounts:
            - name: dcgm-csv
              mountPath: /etc/dcgm-exporter
          securityContext:
            privileged: true
          resources:
            limits:
              cpu: 200m
              memory: 256Mi
      volumes:
        - name: dcgm-csv
          configMap:
            name: dcgm-metrics-config
# Sample dcp-metrics-included.csv (controls which fields DCGM Exporter publishes)
# Format: DCGM_FIELD_ID, prometheus_type, prometheus_help
DCGM_FI_DEV_GPU_UTIL,         gauge, GPU utilization
DCGM_FI_DEV_MEM_COPY_UTIL,    gauge, Memory utilization
DCGM_FI_DEV_GPU_TEMP,         gauge, GPU temperature
DCGM_FI_DEV_MEM_TEMP,         gauge, Memory temperature
DCGM_FI_DEV_POWER_USAGE,      gauge, Power usage in watts
DCGM_FI_DEV_FB_FREE,          gauge, Free framebuffer in MB
DCGM_FI_DEV_FB_USED,          gauge, Used framebuffer in MB
DCGM_FI_DEV_ECC_SBE_VOL_TOTAL, counter, Single-bit ECC errors
DCGM_FI_DEV_ECC_DBE_VOL_TOTAL, counter, Double-bit ECC errors
DCGM_FI_DEV_PCIE_REPLAY_COUNTER, counter, PCIe replay count
DCGM_FI_DEV_XID_ERRORS,       gauge, Last XID error
DCGM_FI_DEV_NVLINK_BANDWIDTH_TOTAL, gauge, NVLink bandwidth

GPU XID Errors — Critical SRE Knowledge

XID errors are NVIDIA GPU fault codes logged by the kernel driver to dmesg and /var/log/syslog. Every SRE operating GPU infrastructure must be fluent in XID interpretation. These codes appear as kernel messages in the format: NVRM: Xid (PCI:0000:41:00): 48, pid=12345, .... The XID number is the key to diagnosis.

XID Name Severity Root Cause Action
13 GR (Graphics Engine) Fault Medium Usually a driver or CUDA runtime bug; sometimes bad application code accessing freed memory Update driver. If persistent across driver versions, check application. Rare hardware root cause.
31 GPU Memory Page Retirement High Correctable ECC errors exceeded threshold on a memory page; driver retires the page proactively Monitor frequency. >5 retirements/week = schedule GPU replacement. Check nvidia-smi -q -d RETIRED_PAGES.
43 GPU Stopped Processing Critical GPU context watchdog detected hang. Often thermal throttling, power delivery issue, or firmware bug. Check thermals, power. Reset GPU with nvidia-smi -r. If recurring, drain and RMA.
48 Double-Bit ECC Error Critical Uncorrectable memory error. Data corruption has occurred. Cannot be repaired in the field. Drain node immediately. Kill affected jobs. RMA the GPU. Do NOT attempt to continue operations.
63 Row Remapping Failure Critical GPU attempted to remap a faulty memory row but ran out of spare rows (Ampere+/Hopper). GPU memory is permanently degraded. Drain node and RMA. Indicates extensive memory wear.
64 Fallen off the Bus Critical GPU is no longer visible on the PCIe bus. Often a power or physical seating issue. Power cycle node. If recurs, reseat GPU physically. Check PCIe riser card and power cables. Possible motherboard or GPU hardware failure.
74 NVLink Error High NVLink CRC or protocol error. Cable degradation, connector issue, or NVLink circuitry fault. Check nvidia-smi nvlink -s. Reseat NVLink cables. If errors persist, replace cable then GPU.
79 GPU Access to Invalid Address Medium Application or driver attempted to access an invalid GPU virtual address. Usually an application bug. Enable CUDA_LAUNCH_BLOCKING=1 to get precise stack trace. Update CUDA toolkit and driver.
94 Contained ECC Error High ECC error was contained to the faulting application (Hopper+ feature). Process killed but GPU is OK. Job will fail; can be restarted. Monitor for frequency. If repeated on same GPU, treat as XID 48 path.
95 Uncontained ECC Error Critical ECC error could not be contained. Potential cross-process data corruption. Drain node. Reset GPU. If recurring, RMA. Similar severity to XID 48.

Checking for XID Errors

# Check kernel messages for XID errors
dmesg | grep -i "xid"

# Check specific GPU by PCI bus address
dmesg | grep "NVRM: Xid (PCI:0000:41:00)"

# Query retired pages (indicates prior XID 31 events)
nvidia-smi -q -d RETIRED_PAGES

# Check ECC error counts
nvidia-smi -q -d ECC

# Query remapped rows (Ampere+/Hopper)
nvidia-smi -q -d REMAPPED_ROWS

# View XID errors in system journal (systemd)
journalctl -k --since "1 hour ago" | grep -i xid

# DCGM health check that catches XID-worthy conditions
dcgmi health -c -g 1

# Continuous monitoring: watch for new XID in real-time
dmesg -wH | grep --line-buffered "Xid"

XID Error Troubleshooting Flowchart

SRE Rule: Any XID 48 (double-bit ECC) event is a drain-immediately situation. The GPU has experienced uncorrectable memory corruption. All running jobs on that GPU are suspect and their outputs should be treated as potentially incorrect. The node must be drained from the scheduler, the GPU replaced, and a Level 3 DCGM diagnostic run before returning to service.

Prometheus + Grafana Observability Stack

The standard observability stack for GPU clusters is Prometheus for metrics collection and storage, Grafana for visualization and dashboards, and AlertManager for alert routing. This stack is deployed either directly on the cluster or as a dedicated monitoring infrastructure, and it forms the nerve center for all operational visibility.

Monitoring Stack Architecture

Prometheus Configuration for GPU Metrics

# prometheus.yml - GPU cluster scrape configuration
global:
  scrape_interval: 15s
  evaluation_interval: 15s
  scrape_timeout: 10s

# Recording rules for pre-aggregation
rule_files:
  - /etc/prometheus/rules/gpu_alerts.yml
  - /etc/prometheus/rules/gpu_recordings.yml

# Alertmanager configuration
alerting:
  alertmanagers:
    - static_configs:
        - targets:
            - alertmanager:9093

scrape_configs:
  # DCGM Exporter (GPU metrics)
  - job_name: 'dcgm-exporter'
    kubernetes_sd_configs:
      - role: pod
        namespaces:
          names: ['monitoring']
    relabel_configs:
      - source_labels: [__meta_kubernetes_pod_label_app]
        regex: dcgm-exporter
        action: keep
      - source_labels: [__meta_kubernetes_pod_node_name]
        target_label: node
      - source_labels: [__meta_kubernetes_pod_ip]
        target_label: __address__
        replacement: "$1:9400"

  # Node Exporter (CPU, memory, disk, network)
  - job_name: 'node-exporter'
    kubernetes_sd_configs:
      - role: pod
    relabel_configs:
      - source_labels: [__meta_kubernetes_pod_label_app]
        regex: node-exporter
        action: keep
      - source_labels: [__meta_kubernetes_pod_ip]
        target_label: __address__
        replacement: "$1:9100"

  # IPMI Exporter (BMC sensors)
  - job_name: 'ipmi-exporter'
    static_configs:
      - targets:
          - bmc-gpu-001.mgmt:9290
          - bmc-gpu-002.mgmt:9290
    metrics_path: /ipmi
    params:
      module: [default]

  # InfiniBand metrics
  - job_name: 'infiniband'
    static_configs:
      - targets:
          - ufm-server:9400
    metrics_path: /metrics

GPU Alert Rules

# /etc/prometheus/rules/gpu_alerts.yml
groups:
  - name: gpu-health
    interval: 30s
    rules:
      # Temperature alerts
      - alert: GPUTemperatureWarning
        expr: DCGM_FI_DEV_GPU_TEMP > 80
        for: 5m
        labels:
          severity: warning
          team: gpu-infra
        annotations:
          summary: "GPU {{ $labels.gpu }} on {{ $labels.instance }} at {{ $value }}°C"
          description: "GPU temperature above 80°C for 5 minutes. Thermal throttling imminent at 83°C."
          runbook_url: "https://wiki.internal/runbooks/gpu-thermal"

      - alert: GPUTemperatureCritical
        expr: DCGM_FI_DEV_GPU_TEMP > 85
        for: 2m
        labels:
          severity: critical
          team: gpu-infra
        annotations:
          summary: "GPU {{ $labels.gpu }} on {{ $labels.instance }} OVERHEATING at {{ $value }}°C"
          description: "GPU temperature above 85°C. Active thermal throttling. Check cooling system."

      # ECC error alerts
      - alert: GPUDoublebitECC
        expr: DCGM_FI_DEV_ECC_DBE_VOL_TOTAL > 0
        for: 0m
        labels:
          severity: critical
          team: gpu-infra
          page: "true"
        annotations:
          summary: "FATAL: Double-bit ECC error on GPU {{ $labels.gpu }} on {{ $labels.instance }}"
          description: "Uncorrectable memory error detected. Drain node immediately and RMA GPU."

      - alert: GPUSingleBitECCHigh
        expr: rate(DCGM_FI_DEV_ECC_SBE_VOL_TOTAL[1h]) > 100
        for: 10m
        labels:
          severity: warning
          team: gpu-infra
        annotations:
          summary: "High single-bit ECC rate on GPU {{ $labels.gpu }} on {{ $labels.instance }}"
          description: "{{ $value }} correctable ECC errors/hour. Memory degrading. Plan replacement."

      # XID error alert
      - alert: GPUXidError
        expr: DCGM_FI_DEV_XID_ERRORS != 0
        for: 0m
        labels:
          severity: warning
          team: gpu-infra
        annotations:
          summary: "XID {{ $value }} on GPU {{ $labels.gpu }} on {{ $labels.instance }}"
          description: "GPU reported XID error. Check dmesg on the node for details."

      # GPU utilization anomalies
      - alert: GPUIdleDuringJob
        expr: |
          DCGM_FI_DEV_GPU_UTIL < 5
          and on(instance) slurm_node_state{state="allocated"} == 1
        for: 15m
        labels:
          severity: warning
          team: gpu-infra
        annotations:
          summary: "GPU {{ $labels.gpu }} idle on allocated node {{ $labels.instance }}"
          description: "GPU shows <5% utilization but node is allocated. Job may be stuck or misconfigured."

      # Power alerts
      - alert: GPUPowerExceedsTDP
        expr: DCGM_FI_DEV_POWER_USAGE > 700
        for: 5m
        labels:
          severity: warning
          team: gpu-infra
        annotations:
          summary: "GPU {{ $labels.gpu }} power {{ $value }}W exceeds TDP on {{ $labels.instance }}"

      # NVLink errors
      - alert: NVLinkCRCErrors
        expr: rate(DCGM_FI_DEV_NVLINK_CRC_FLIT_ERROR_COUNT_TOTAL[5m]) > 0
        for: 2m
        labels:
          severity: warning
          team: gpu-infra
        annotations:
          summary: "NVLink CRC errors on GPU {{ $labels.gpu }} on {{ $labels.instance }}"
          description: "NVLink experiencing CRC errors. Check cables and connectors."

      # PCIe replay
      - alert: PCIeReplayHigh
        expr: rate(DCGM_FI_DEV_PCIE_REPLAY_COUNTER[1h]) > 100
        for: 10m
        labels:
          severity: warning
          team: gpu-infra
        annotations:
          summary: "High PCIe replay count on GPU {{ $labels.gpu }} on {{ $labels.instance }}"
          description: "PCIe link quality degraded. Check physical seating and riser card."

      # Node-level alerts
      - alert: GPUNodeDown
        expr: up{job="dcgm-exporter"} == 0
        for: 3m
        labels:
          severity: critical
          team: gpu-infra
        annotations:
          summary: "DCGM Exporter down on {{ $labels.instance }}"
          description: "Cannot scrape GPU metrics. Node may be down or DCGM daemon failed."

Useful PromQL Queries for GPU Operations

# Average GPU utilization across the entire cluster
avg(DCGM_FI_DEV_GPU_UTIL)

# GPU utilization heatmap: per-node, per-GPU
DCGM_FI_DEV_GPU_UTIL

# Top 10 hottest GPUs in the cluster
topk(10, DCGM_FI_DEV_GPU_TEMP)

# Total power consumption across all GPUs (watts)
sum(DCGM_FI_DEV_POWER_USAGE)

# GPUs with any ECC errors in the last hour
sum by (instance, gpu) (increase(DCGM_FI_DEV_ECC_SBE_VOL_TOTAL[1h])) > 0

# GPU memory utilization percentage
(DCGM_FI_DEV_FB_USED / (DCGM_FI_DEV_FB_USED + DCGM_FI_DEV_FB_FREE)) * 100

# Nodes with GPUs above 80°C
count by (instance) (DCGM_FI_DEV_GPU_TEMP > 80)

# Average GPU utilization by rack (requires rack label)
avg by (rack) (DCGM_FI_DEV_GPU_UTIL)

# NVLink bandwidth utilization rate of change
rate(DCGM_FI_DEV_NVLINK_BANDWIDTH_TOTAL[5m])

# Cluster-wide GPU availability (fraction of healthy GPUs)
count(DCGM_FI_DEV_GPU_TEMP > 0) / count(DCGM_FI_DEV_GPU_TEMP >= 0)
